Stock Market Pays Little Heed To Rate Rise
Wednesday's Markets
Stock Market Pays Little Heed To Rate Rise
By E.S. Browning
557 words
11 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ALAN GREENSPAN RAISED interest rates, and the stock market shrugged.
The move by the Federal Reserve chairman and his colleagues was so widely expected that the reaction was
unusually calm. It was the fourth Fed rate increase since the end of June, and investors were nervously
aware that stocks sometimes have sagged after a fourth Fed rate boost. But interest rates remain so low --
the Fed's target short-term rate still is just 2%, and the 10-year Treasury bond still yields only 4.251% -- that
many investors think they won't prove a serious brake on corporate profits or on stock gains.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished the day practically unchanged, down 0.89 points, less than
one-hundredth of one percent, at 10385.48. It still is down 0.7% this year. Other big-stock indexes showed
small declines. The stock market is in a pause after last week's sharp gains, but the interest-rate news
surprised no one, said John O'Donoghue, senior stock trader at New York brokerage firm Credit Suisse First
Boston. Furthermore, the Fed's statement on the economic outlook was little changed from September. That
led investors to conclude that the Fed will raise its target rate another quarter-point next month -- as expected
-- gradually moving rates up from 40-year lows to prevent inflation.
The dollar moved sharply, rising 1.4% against the yen, its sharpest percentage gain in six months. The
September trade deficit came in much narrower than expected, and there was talk in currency markets that
Asian countries again are reluctant to see their currencies rise rapidly against the dollar. Investors have been
treating gold lately as a bet on a declining dollar. After hitting a 16-year high Tuesday of $435.70, gold
futures fell $1.70 to $434.00.
Crude-oil futures posted their biggest gain in almost two months, jumping $1.49, or 3.2%, to $48.86 a barrel
on the New York Mercantile Exchange, as the Energy Department's weekly inventory numbers were slightly
weaker than hoped. Crude still is 11% off the 21-year high of $55.17 hit last month.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index declined 0.10%, or 1.17 points, to 1162.91, still up 4.6% for
2004 and near the three-year high of 1166.17 hit Friday. The Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.43%, or 8.77
points, to 2034.56, up 1.6% this year. Its many technology stocks were weighed down by disappointing
earnings news.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.04%, or 0.07 point, to 159.87.
In major U.S. market action:
Major stock indexes retreated. But on the Big Board, where 1.51 billion shares traded, 1,943 stocks rose and
only 1,349 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 8/32, or $2.50 for each $1,000 invested. The 30-year bond
was down 14/32 to yield 4.970%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 107.17 yen, up from 105.74, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2895 from $1.2898.

Natural-Gas Terminal Plan Is Floated --- Long Island Sound Facility Could Ease Energy Crunch, But Strong Opposition Looms
The Property Report
Natural-Gas Terminal Plan Is Floated --- Long Island Sound Facility Could Ease Energy Crunch, But
Strong Opposition Looms
By Russell Gold and John J. Fialka
622 words
10 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TO FEED A TIGHT market for natural gas in the Northeastern U.S., Royal Dutch/Shell Group and
TransCanada Corp. hope to build a massive floating terminal for liquefied natural gas in the middle of Long
Island Sound.
The proposed $700 million terminal would require state and federal approval and is likely to face strong local
opposition. If built, however, it could help rein in high home-heating and electricity costs in the Northeast. As
the indigenous supply of natural gas in North America declines, energy companies have turned to increased
imports of liquefied natural gas, or LNG, from big gas-producing nations as a way to keep American homes
heated and industries humming.
Natural gas is a relatively low-polluting fossil fuel, and environmentalists in the New York area have pushed
hard for new plants to run on the fuel, exacerbating a tight supply situation in the region.
No new LNG terminals have been built in the U.S. since the 1970s, but more than three dozen have been
proposed in the past couple of years as natural-gas prices have more than doubled. Industry observers
expect no more than a half-dozen to ultimately be built. The proposed Long Island Sound terminal, called
Broadwater Energy, could open in 2010.
Proposals to build new terminals in the Northeast and New England have met with the strongest opposition.
Opponents fear that the facilities could be terrorist targets, and others point to a massive accidental explosion
at an Algerian LNG plant earlier this year that killed 27 people. John Hritcko, Broadwater's project director,
says that putting the terminal nine miles from the closest shore creates "a buffer area around this that is more
than adequate to maintain a secure facility."
The Broadwater facility will be large enough to import about one billion cubic feet of natural gas a day, more
than twice that consumed by all of Connecticut. It will be fed every two to three days by tankers the size of
aircraft carriers.
Still, approval of the project is far from certain. Earlier this year, citizen referendums in Harpswell, Maine, and
Beaumont, Quebec, killed terminal projects in those towns. TransCanada spokeswoman Hedji Feick says the
Long Island Sound location was chosen because it serves "regions that have been identified as in high need
of energy. . . . We are going to witness a supply crunch from traditional supply sources."
In a separate deal, Houston-based Cheniere Energy Inc. said that it had received commitments from
ChevronTexaco Corp. and Total SA to use its proposed LNG terminal in Sabine Pass, La. The terminal still
requires final federal permits, but construction could begin as soon as early next year.
In contrast to the Northeast, terminals along the Gulf Coast such as the proposed Sabine Pass terminal have
been largely welcomed. In Texas and Louisiana, it has been a "love fest between industry and the local
population," says Bob Nimock, president of Zeus Development Corp., a Houston-based energy research firm.
"They see it as jobs, new tax base and investment in the community. In the Northeast, they see it as a
possible terrorist target. They see all the negatives."
Natural gas imported into the Gulf Coast can be transported to the Northeast, but there is a limited amount of
pipeline capacity, and the gas usually fetches a premium of 15% -- about $1 more per million British thermal
units, or the equivalent of one thousand cubic feet.
The deals sent Cheniere stock up 45% to close at $37.97 in 4 p.m. American Stock Exchange composite
trading.

Investors Pile Into Stocks After Bush Win --- Much of Wall Street Sees Renewed Potential for Equities; A Comeback for Growth Funds?
Investors Pile Into Stocks After Bush Win --- Much of Wall Street Sees Renewed Potential for Equities;
A Comeback for Growth Funds?
By Jane J. Kim
1,758 words
10 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MUCH OF WALL STREET, anticipating President Bush's re-election, has been advising investors to take a
fresh look at their portfolios this fall. Now that the election is over, investors are taking their advice to heart.
A wide array of money managers and strategists have been moving some of their own money into stocks
from cash and bonds and telling their clients to do the same. While a fair amount of money was already
moving in that direction during recent weeks -- and the outlook for stocks is far from euphoric -- interest in
equities has sharply accelerated in the days since the vote.
On Nov. 3, the day after the election, 401(k) investors made the single largest transfer of money into equity
funds since January, according to benefits-consulting firm Hewitt Associates. So far this year, stock funds
have attracted more than $260 billion and could hit $300 billion by Dec. 31, says Avi Nachmany, director of
research at Strategic Insight. The last year in which flows into stock funds reached those levels was in 2000,
when about $338 billion went into equity funds. Bond funds, meanwhile, have lost more than $10 billion in
outflows over the same period.
This week, chief investment strategist Edward Keon of Prudential Equity Group, a unit of Prudential Financial
Inc., halved what he recommends investors hold in bonds to 20%; he advised they move that money into
stocks and cash. Last week, UBS Investment Research, a unit of UBS AG, increased the proportion of stocks
it recommends for investors from 55% to 58%.
Investment strategists at Citigroup Inc.'s Smith Barney unit and PNC Financial Services Group Inc.'s PNC
Advisors have also recently bumped up the percentage of stocks they recommend in model portfolios. For
investors with moderate-risk profiles, Citigroup now advocates 60% in stocks and PNC advises 55%.
That doesn't mean investors should dramatically shift course, since asset-allocation strategies should be
based on risk tolerance and long-term goals. Rather, experts say it's a good time to implement a few tactical
shifts.
Some money managers are recommending that investors build their positions in growth stocks, which have
been out of favor of late. During recent weeks, Morgan Stanley began recommending that investors boost
their exposure to growth, in part because these stocks had gotten so cheap relative to value stocks, which
have had a strong run during the past few years. Value stocks are companies that appear undervalued
relative to their profits or assets, while growth stocks are companies perceived as likely to increase profits
rapidly.
Portfolio strategists at J.P. Morgan Private Bank have been recommending that investors begin to overweight
large-cap stocks over small-cap stocks, since larger companies are more likely to have better control over
their margins and cost structures in an environment of rising interest rates, says Jack Caffrey, an equity
strategist for J.P. Morgan Private Bank, a unit of J.P. Morgan Chase &Co.
The so-called presidential cycle is one reason for the relative bullishness on equities. Historically, when an
incumbent president is re-elected, stocks have gained an average of about 3.3% from the day before the
election through year-end, according to Ned Davis Research. When the incumbent party loses, however,
stocks slip an average of 0.9% during the same period.
Another reason: Investors' worst fears -- including a disputed election and terrorist attacks -- didn't
materialize. And many Wall Street firms had been hoping for a Bush win, because that would mean a
continuation of lower tax rates on dividends and capital gains, which would make stocks more attractive.
Additionally, Mr. Bush's proposals to create private investment accounts for retirement, health care and Social
Security are expected to help boost profits of financial-services firms.

--- Page 9 ---
Page 9 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
At the same time, a variety of factors -- from U.S. budget deficits to the weakening dollar to the huge potential
for growth overseas -- are moderating expectations for U.S. stocks. As a result, many managers are keen on
foreign stocks, particularly emerging markets.
Others say it's a good time to lighten up on fixed income or at least shift to bonds with shorter durations and
higher credit quality, given renewed concerns over rising interest rates and the likelihood of larger deficits
under Mr. Bush. A few on Wall Street, worried about inflation, are recommending that investors add inflation
hedges, such as gold or inflation-linked securities, to their portfolio.
A number of firms, including PNC Advisors and Morgan Stanley, are recommending that investors reduce
their exposure to fixed income, given low returns and expectations that bigger deficits -- which may widen if
Mr. Bush seeks to extend current tax cuts -- could further push up interest rates, hurting bond prices. The
Federal Reserve is expected to raise the federal-funds rate by a quarter-percentage point today.
At its investment-strategy meeting next month, Citigroup Private Bank plans to either reduce its
recommended allocation to fixed-income assets in its model portfolios or move to shorter-duration bonds,
says David Rosenberg, head of investment solutions at Citigroup Private Bank.
McDonald Financial Group, part of Cleveland's KeyCorp, dropped the fixed-income exposure on its balanced
portfolios in September by five percentage points to 25%, while increasing its cash exposure, says John
Caldwell, chief investment strategist.
Morgan Stanley's Individual Investor Group, which had been considering a move to increase its bond
weighting, decided instead to maintain its underweight position in U.S. bonds at 25% last week because of
concerns over the dollar's levels and expectations that Bush's emphasis on tax cuts means that the deficits
will continue to remain large.
Merrill Lynch &Co., by contrast, has continued to maintain a relatively high weighting to bonds at 45%,
although the firm has been advocating a move to higher-quality government and corporate bonds. Slowing
profit cycles world-wide signal that investors should shift toward high-quality assets, says Richard Bernstein,
Merrill's chief U.S. strategist. What's more, the opportunity cost of being diversified is low, he says, given
relative returns on other asset classes.
Expectations of widening deficits are also contributing to a weaker U.S. dollar, which is prompting strategists
to recommend that investors increase their exposure to international stocks. Not only are international stocks
likely to benefit from an improved outlook for earnings and relative valuations, but a weaker U.S. dollar also
makes the sector more attractive because the stocks gain value in dollar terms.
Just last week, Citigroup Private Bank started telling clients they should consider investing in European
stocks. "We haven't changed our allocation yet, but when we talk to clients we tell them we think valuations in
Europe were getting very positive," Mr. Rosenberg says. What's more, there are signs that European
companies are likely to sustain their earnings growth, while the outlook for U.S. companies, which have had a
number of negative earnings forecasts in the latest reporting period, appears less rosy.
The easiest way for investors to invest in these stocks is to buy international mutual funds. Based on the
latest weekly fund flows data, of the $2.3 billion of money that investors poured into equity mutual funds last
week, about 70% of that amount went into international and global-equity funds, according to Robert Adler,
president of AMG Data Services, which tracks mutual-fund cash flows.
Other money managers worry that inflation is likely to be a greater concern. Wilmington Trust has recently
started to recommend that investors consider inflation hedges, such real estate, inflation-linked bonds or
commodities, says Dorsey Farr, director of asset allocation and portfolio strategy. For investors with a
moderate risk tolerance, for example, he is recommending they hold between 8% to 10% of their portfolio in
inflation hedges.
On Friday, Morgan Stanley also recommended that investors increase their exposure to nonfinancial assets,
such as oil and gas stocks, precious metals, timber and other commodities stocks, by a few percentage
points, given expectations that the potential for deficits to remain large could put upward pressure on interest
rates and inflation, says David Darst, chief investment strategist of Morgan Stanley's Individual Investor
Group.
---
Market Moves
Here are some of the moves that money managers are making:

-- Invest in international equity funds as a way to benefit from current
weakness in the dollar.
-- Move to growth and large-cap stocks, which look undervalued compared with
value and small-cap stocks.
-- Shift to bonds with shorter maturities, or consider Treasury
Inflation-Protected Securities.
---
What the Strategists Are Saying
Many money managers say it's a good time to put cash to work in the stock
markets and pare back bond positions. International stocks and
inflation
hedges are also popular options. Here's some of the recent advice that Wall
Street strategists are giving their clients.
COMPANY: Banc of America Securities
ADVICE: In September, the firm established a 10% weighting in Treasury
Inflation Protected Securities, given attractive valuations and concerns
over inflation. It has overweight positions in the energy and
basic-materials sectors, which may benefit in an environment of higher
prices.
COMPANY: Citigroup's Smith Barney
ADVICE: In September, the firm raised its recommended allocation of stocks
for a moderate-risk investor to 60% (up from 55%) while reducing cash to 5%
(from 10%). Strategists say stocks are likely to outperform cash and bonds
over the next 12 to 15 months.
COMPANY: McDonald Financial Group
ADVICE: Advises investors to reduce their exposure to bonds given
expectations of rising yields, which will put pressure on bond prices. The
firm currently recommends that investors with a balanced portfolio hold a
(MORE)
mix of 60% stocks, 25% bonds and 15% cash.
COMPANY: PNC Advisors
ADVICE: Recommends a 5-percentage-point overweighting in stocks, favoring
large-cap growth stocks. Adds that investors should underweight bonds due to
elevated risk and relatively little potential reward.
COMPANY: Prudential Securities
ADVICE: On Tuesday, the firm halved its recommended allocation of bonds to
20%, boosting allocation of stocks and cash to 60% and 20%, respectively, on
expectations that
interest rates are poised to rise further.
COMPANY: UBS Investment Research
ADVICE: Last week, the firm boosted its recommended weighting of stocks
while paring back its cash position. Strategists also advised clients to
boost their positions in regions such as Europe, Japan and emerging markets
due to attractive valuations.

The Jobs Rebound
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Jobs Rebound
791 words
8 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Maybe Democrats are lucky the election came early in November this year. Had it been held a week later,
President Bush would have been able to spend this past weekend touting last Friday's bullish jobs report as a
sign of the economy's continuing strength.
Nonfarm payrolls added 337,000 jobs in October -- almost double expectations. Revisions also added
113,000 more new jobs for the months of August and September, meaning that the economy has generated
2.1 million jobs in the past year. October's pace of job creation puts Mr. Bush on track to end his first term
with a net gain, notwithstanding all of the campaign references to Herbert Hoover.
This good news should not be a surprise since other economic data have been solid. Most important, capital
spending has been churning along this year after being moribund during the early stages of this expansion.
(Consumers and housing were the growth engines then.) Both outlays for heavy machinery and business
construction now look perky and, given the huge amounts of cash that companies are sitting on, capital
spending should continue to post gains. This is crucial because capital spending is the driver for expanding
production that, in turn, generates new jobs.
The good jobs news might also be due in part to slowing third-quarter productivity growth -- to 1.9%. The
productivity numbers have been unusually robust since late 2002, with a second quarter pace of 3.9%. High
productivity growth dampens the need for companies to hire. But now economists expect that firms will have
to add jobs at a faster clip just to keep up with demand. And productivity growth of 1.9% isn't shabby either;
it's the same average pace achieved during the 1990s.
At any rate, all of this reinforces the pleasant GDP figure recently reported for the third quarter. Although real
growth of 3.7% was slower than expected, any growth over 3.5% will generate a faster pace in job creation.
And it pretty much insures that the Federal Reserve will raise interest rates again, by another quarter point
to 2% this Wednesday. This makes sense considering that gold futures have just hit a 16-year high of
$433.90 an ounce, and the dollar is looking weak.
Although this expansion is now in its third year -- often a dicey time -- recent stock market performance
shows that investors are expecting the good news to continue. The market rallied smartly on Wednesday
after it was clear that Mr. Bush had been re-elected. And it surged again on Thursday after Mr. Bush's press
conference, in which he reaffirmed his desire to make the 2003 tax cuts permanent and move ahead with
personal Social Security accounts (which would allow some payroll taxes to be invested in the stock market).
The Standard &Poor's 500 finished the week at a high for the year, the Dow ended the week just a tad under
its 2004 high, and the Nasdaq is now up 1.8% for the year.
The bad news is that the dollar ended the week at a nine-year low against the euro. Recently, the dollar has
been drubbed by selling from Middle Eastern investors and others reportedly anxious about the twin deficits
in the federal budget and the current account. Although a budget deficit at 3.7% of GDP shouldn't be
disturbing, the current account deficit of over 5% of GDP is large by historical standards.
In part, the trade deficit reflects that the U.S. is growing faster than the rest of the world, China excepted. We
suspect the market also has doubts about the Bush Administration's stated commitment to a strong dollar and
its seriousness in controlling spending. One consequence of such doubts is an expectation of four more years
of a weak dollar. Other difficulties ahead include the negative impact of high oil prices, and the recent volatility
in oil markets by itself creates uncertainty that can dull animal spirits.
But business, which made a huge bet on Mr. Bush's re-election, has lots to be happy about. Republican gains
in Congress mean that the pro-growth reforms that are part of Mr. Bush's "ownership society" agenda have a
chance to become law. These include the expansion of the private market for health care, such tort reforms
as restrictions on class action lawsuits and limits on medical malpractice, and perhaps even limits on
corporate liability in asbestos-related claims.

--- Page 16 ---
Page 16 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Sensible optimists are expecting economic growth to be very good in the fourth quarter. But it's also
increasingly possible to look out over the longer term and see a durable expansion that has ample room to
run.

Gold Futures Rise To 7-Month High On a Weak Dollar
Commodities Report
Gold Futures Rise To 7-Month High On a Weak Dollar
By Gavin Maguire Dow Jones Newswires
441 words
5 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold futures on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange charged to
seven-month highs as renewed weakness in the dollar and a brightening technical picture rekindled
speculative and investor interest.
Gold for November delivery closed up $5.50 at $430.10 an ounce. The most active December contract
settled $5.40 higher at $430.80 an ounce.
The U.S. Dollar Index dropped to a nine-year low as selling pressure resumed now that the presidential
election is finished and another four years of a Bush administration are secured. This steered investors and
speculators toward dollar alternatives and havens such as gold, and helped spot prices pop to $433.25 an
ounce, the highest since 1988.
A bullish chart pattern also proved helpful as prices eroded resistance in the $432 to $433 region to bring into
view targets like $440 and the psychologically significant $450 mark.
"The dollar looks bad, and gold is technically appealing, so there's definitely a bullish feel to this market at
the moment," said Bernard Hunter, a director at Scotia Mocatta in Toronto.
"We could certainly see more funds coming into this market, and we have our eye on big numbers overhead
like $440 and $450," he added.
Bill O'Neill, managing director of commodity-advisory firm Logic Advisors, agreed: "The overall pattern here is
bullish for gold. We expect to see more dollar weakness, as there are continuing concerns about the size of
the U.S. deficits, and there's also some anticipation of inflation as energy prices remain so high."
"When you throw in a positive technical picture as well, this spells good news for gold," he said.
Mr. O'Neill agreed that $450 was the major target on the horizon but suggested that bursts of intermittent
profit-taking and consolidation could be seen before that goal is reached.
"I see us getting to $450 sometime, but not in the very near term."
In other commodity developments:
CRUDE OIL: Futures at the New York Mercantile Exchange fell below $49 a barrel for the first time since
September, amid reports that Palestinian leader Yasser Arafat had died. Those reports were later denied. Mr.
Arafat is widely believed to be an obstacle to Mideast peace negotiations. Crude oil for December delivery fell
$2.06 a barrel to $48.82.
COCOA: Futures at the New York Board of Trade rose on renewed and heightened violence in the leading
cocoa-producing country, Ivory Coast. Cocoa for December delivery rose $108 to $1,568 a metric ton.

Bush Win Gives Stocks a Boost --- Pharmaceuticals, Oil Concerns, Arms Makers Are Among Gainers; Treasurys and Dollar Are Mixed
Wednesday's Markets
Bush Win Gives Stocks a Boost --- Pharmaceuticals, Oil Concerns, Arms Makers Are Among Gainers;
Treasurys and Dollar Are Mixed
By E.S. Browning and Aaron Lucchetti
1,491 words
4 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS INITIALLY SENT stocks skyward on news of the Bush victory, though they trimmed the gains
by day's end.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, up 179.78 points within minutes of the opening bell, finished ahead
101.32 points, or 1.01%, at 10137.05. That was its sixth advance in seven sessions, although it still is down
3% for 2004. Other stock indexes followed a similar pattern.
On the New York Stock Exchange, 1.76 billion shares changed hands, the heaviest volume in almost six
months.
Several stock groups surged in a belief that they will benefit from four more years of a Bush administration,
including drug companies, oil companies, arms producers and money managers. Alternative-energy
producers and some biotechnology companies, which investors had expected to benefit from a Kerry victory,
fell back.
Within minutes of the start of trading, investors took a hard-eyed look at the market and decided that other
forces, having little to do with the election, will continue to play a big role. Those include corporate profits,
interest rates, oil prices, the fear of terrorism and the pace of economic growth.
In the short and medium term, "this election will end up being mostly an irrelevant sideshow as far as markets
and the economy are concerned," wrote Jason Rotenberg of money-management firm Bridgewater
Associates in a report to clients, although he added that it could be important over the longer term.
Treasury bonds, which had risen in price Tuesday as nervous investors sought their relative safety, began the
day yesterday with a sharp decline, but then recouped almost all their losses.
Crude-oil futures initially fell after the Energy Department reported a rise in U.S. oil supplies last week. But oil
also reversed course and soared $1.26 to $50.88 a barrel, $4.29 below a 21-year high. Traders speculated
that a Bush victory could mean less conservation and possible unrest in the Middle East.
In late New York trading, the dollar hit its lowest level against the euro in almost nine months, on expectations
of continuing U.S. deficits. The dollar's decline helped stoke renewed gold buying, and gold rose $4.60 to
$424.60 an ounce -- $4.40 short of a 16-year high.
Often, elections prove less important for stocks than expected. This time, with fears lifted of a hung election
or a terrorist attack during the campaign season, some traders forecast a stock rally that could last, with ups
and downs, into 2005.
On the 12 occasions that an incumbent has been re-elected over the past 100 years, the Dow Jones
Industrial Average rose an average 7.4% in the following year, said Bob Christian, chief economist at
Wilmington Trust in Wilmington, Del. "The president's economic policies of lower taxes and less regulation
are widely regarded to be better for stocks in the near term," Mr. Christian said.
But stocks also typically stage below-average gains in the year after an election, as new governments tend to
be stingier. Some investors worry about a sub-par 2005 for stocks.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 1.12%, or 12.66 points, to 1143.20, its highest finish since
June. It was the index's seventh consecutive gain, leaving it up 2.8% this year. The Nasdaq Composite Index
rose 0.98%, or 19.54 points, to 2004.33, its first finish above 2000 in four months. The Nasdaq is nearly
unchanged for the year so far.

--- Page 21 ---
Page 21 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Drug stocks provide an example of the issues with which investors are grappling. Fears of a Kerry health
program and tougher regulations contributed to their recent weakness, and Mr. Kerry's defeat helps explain
their gains yesterday. But they also have been held back by the threat of patent expiration and by questions
surrounding blockbuster drugs such as Merck's antiarthritis drug Vioxx. Those issues will remain.
Brokerage firm Susquehanna Financial Group of Bala Cynwyd, Pa., identified such likely winning stocks as
military-related companies, major drug producers, insurance companies, money managers, coal producers
and oil companies. Among the likely losers were hospitals, which would have benefited from the Kerry health
plan, and home-finance concerns Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, which have come under government
scrutiny.
Oil and coal companies are thought to benefit from hopes that the Bush administration will lift restrictions on
exploration and development, and arms makers from expectations of more military spending. Insurance
companies are expected to benefit from Bush efforts to limit lawsuits and to seek private-sector health plans.
Money managers such as T. Rowe Price, Eaton Vance and Franklin Resources rose on expectations that
they could receive significant new business -- possibly on the order of an extra $75 billion in annual fund
contributions -- if President Bush partially privatizes Social Security, noted Ken Worthington, an analyst with
CIBC World Markets. They also benefited from an expectation that Mr. Bush's tax cuts won't be repealed and
may be broadened.
Investors now are looking ahead nervously to tomorrow's report on October job creation, which should give
the latest economic reading. Strong numbers would help stocks, but would send bond prices down on fears of
more Federal Reserve efforts to cool the economy by raising interest rates.
Meanwhile, a weakening dollar could rekindle inflation fears by making imports more expensive and reduce
foreigners' appetite for Treasury bonds. Some investors think the dollar could prove an important market
influence, especially for bonds.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.98%, or 1.53 points, to 158.45.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, 2,489 stocks rose and 787 fell.
Bonds were mixed. The 10-year Treasury note fell 2/32, or 62.5 cents for each $1,000 invested. The yield
rose to 4.074%. The 30-year bond was up 1/32 to yield 4.828%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 106.25 yen, up from 106.16, while the euro rose against
the dollar to $1.2823 from $1.2726.
---
Karen Damato contributed to this article.

Well, It Worked Most of the Time . . .
The Bush victory in the election may end the faith that some investors had
in various "trend" indicators on elections. Three of them that got a lot of
attention in the past week:
The Redskins-Win Rule
John Kerry supporters were cheered on Sunday when the Green Bay Packers
beat the Washington Redskins in football. Even Mr. Kerry mentioned it. The
reason: With no exception, a Redskins win in the final home game before the
presidential election has meant that the incumbent party wins; a loss has
meant the incumbent party is sacked. Until now.
The Red Factor
Standard &Poor's had noted that when a team with red uniforms wins the
World Series, the Democratic candidate wins 71% of the time.With both the Red
Sox and Cardinals sporting red, this indicator was pointing in Mr. Kerry's
direction from the start. But it grounded out.
October Surprise
The incumbent has always won if the Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 0.26%
or less in October, or if it rose. But he has always lost if it fell 0.85% or
more. This year, the result was right in the middle down 0.52%, a gray area
in the past. But Mr. Bush won anyhow. Now, the rule is that the incumbent
wins when the Dow falls 0.52% or less.

We Grow, They Grow
We Grow, They Grow
By David Malpass
1,033 words
3 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Terrorism may have eclipsed the global economy as a focus in yesterday's election, but there's been a
material change in international economic circumstances which should be recognized and fostered. The
world economy is growing at its fastest pace in 30 years, 5% in 2004 according to the IMF, with a robust 4.3%
real growth rate expected in 2005.
Obscured by America's election frenzy and Western Europe's gloomy unemployment, the environment for
global growth has been the best in many decades. U.S. GDP grew a solid 3.9% in the four quarters through
September, while economies in Japan, Brazil, China, India, Turkey and Russia are expanding at or near their
highest pace in decades. Thus, fast growth likely encompassed more of the world's population and land mass
in 2004 than ever before. This broadening of global growth emphasizes the power of freedom and markets
and the past weight of communism, world wars, the 1970s inflation and the late 1990s deflation.
It also presents challenges and opportunities for the new administration. Granted, the world won't grow 5%
every year, at least not unless it wins the war on terrorism, reforms the anti-growth IMF, or adopts sound
money as a pillar of monetary policy. But the combination of a new non-deflationary monetary era and
growth in Asia creates the likelihood of a durable global expansion and the chance to build a better economic
decade than previous ones.
In the '90s, the U.S. was practically the only engine of global growth, making the outlook vulnerable and
volatile. The U.S. may have rejoiced, then, in its own economic conditions, but it should have been concerned
that economies in many parts of the world were shrinking or growing well below their potential. As the U.S.
became isolated in its full employment and wealth, economic conditions abroad affected its national security,
immigration policy and future growth.
The disinflationary, ultimately deflationary, monetary era of the '80s and '90s produced lost decades in Japan,
Latin America and Africa and the devastating Asia crisis of 1997-98. The environment was in many ways
good for U.S. growth as capital flooded to the safe haven. But it was painfully capital-draining for much of the
rest of the world, ending badly in 2000 and 2001 with the strong-dollar deflation crisis, a bitter-sweet victory
over inflation. Investments in much of the world were so depleted that per capita income growth fell way
behind the U.S.
The new environment is beginning to repair this global imbalance, though the core criticism of the U.S. -- its
inability to project abroad its economic vision and strength -- persists. Driving the global expansion, the U.S.
interest rate cuts of 2001 and the post-9/11 liquidity injection weakened the dollar, ending the strong-dollar
disinflation process. With this, the world entered the most pro-growth monetary era since the gold standard,
coincident with the start of the ongoing U.S. economic expansion in December 2001.
Japan is exiting its 12-year deflation spiral, allowing growth in the world's second-largest economy to resume.
China's growth remained fast through September, refuting the many hard-landing predictions from outside its
borders. And the promise of the euro and the faster growth that goes with sound money are spreading into
eastern Europe, burying the communist economic bloc that once saddled the region. In return, Russia and
eastern Europe are sending western Europe the challenge of lower tax rates.
Though unsettling in the transition, the new monetary era is providing a much better environment for
developing-country growth. The consolation for the deflationary extremes of the late '90s is the possibility of
below-average interest rates in much of the world for at least several years. Already, higher commodity
prices and lower interest rates have added to incomes in developing countries, contributing to 2004's fast
global growth.
The growth environment is far from perfect. Outside the U.S. and China, the world is still light on capital
formation -- the aggressive building of factories, machines, small businesses, houses, roads and educational
institutions. The global expansion will also have to contend with the U.S. trade deficit and the harmful policy

--- Page 24 ---
Page 24 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
temptations it presents. The biggest threat is for the U.S. to hunker down into protectionism, stopping an
otherwise durable expansion. Another dangerous idea being bandied about is to weaken the dollar to
pre-empt the U.S. move toward protectionism. A weak-dollar policy would risk inflation and capital flight
without addressing the issues underlying the trade deficit -- the attractiveness of the U.S. investment climate,
our heavy taxation of saving, and the weakness in investment and growth abroad.
A better alternative to protectionism and an even weaker dollar would be to launch an energetic Global
Prosperity Initiative aimed at lengthening the global expansion and helping lift living standards in developing
countries. The principles of U.S. growth are clear: limited government, sound money, trade liberalization, low
tax rates, and a brief constitution as the basis for the rule of law. An initiative based on these principles would
align our foreign economic policy with our domestic economic and security policies. It would complement the
physical and diplomatic war on terrorism, improve our self-absorbed image abroad, and help balance China's
efforts to provide commercial leadership in Asia and Latin America. A good first step in a Prosperity Initiative
would be for the U.S. to state clearly that it wants itself and foreigners to do well economically and will make
institutional changes to play a constructive role in this. Americans may think that this is self-evident -- of
course we want others to do well -- but around the world U.S. policy muddles this message.
Despite the negativism in the election, the fast global growth in 2004 and favorable monetary and tax policy
developments add to the prospects for a long global expansion and higher living standards abroad. Hope and
progress should once again be America's main message to the world, rather than reaction and caution.
---
Mr. Malpass is chief economist at Bear, Stearns.

Gold Futures Slide After Rising Before Election --- Traders Reduce Exposure Established Amid Worries About a Legal Quarrel
Commodities Report
Gold Futures Slide After Rising Before Election --- Traders Reduce Exposure Established Amid
Worries About a Legal Quarrel
By Gavin Maguire Dow Jones Newswires
643 words
3 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold futures prices, which had risen on anticipation of a possible postelection stalemate like
in 2000, plunged more than $7 an ounce, or about 1.7%, as the election proceeded.
Analysts and traders said the selling resulted partly from the slightly firmer U.S. dollar relative to other
currencies ahead of the election. But many traders also figured any legal challenges to the election might not
be severe after all. The nearby-month benchmark contract fell $7.30 an ounce to $420 on the Comex division
of the New York Mercantile Exchange; the most-active December contract ended $7.40 lower at $420.80.
December prices started off $1.70 lower at $426.50 on light profit-taking by speculative futures-trading funds
as Americans went to the polls. But this selling pressure took place amid a scarce-buyer environment, which
kept prices on the back foot through the morning. Automatic sell orders then added fuel to the descent by
generating surges in sales volumes as prices fell.
As a result, trading volume ballooned as the session progressed, jumping from an estimated 13,000 lots at 10
a.m. to more than 75,000 lots, or 7.5 million ounces, just three hours later. By the end of the session, 100,000
futures contracts and 30,000 options traded hands.
Many hedge funds and commodity speculators had built up exposure to gold in anticipation of a drawn-out
legal fight; four years ago, the future of U.S. domestic and foreign policy was shrouded in uncertainty for
weeks after the nation voted.
As the polling date neared, however, market watchers determined that gold already had "priced in" a legal
spat and closely fought contest, and so deemed it would struggle to gain much further ground should lawyers
be called in to settle disputes.
As a result, speculators turned their attention beyond the election and to the expected relief rally in the U.S.
dollar as global investors adjust to the implications of the election result and gain confidence to place more
money back into the U.S. economy.
"The funds are realizing that interest rates could rise, or oil prices could come off further, and the U.S. dollar
can rise after the election, and so are pre-empting those possibilities ahead of the election," said George
Gero, a senior vice president at Legg Mason Wood Walker.
"Normally these things would happen after an election, but the gold market is trying to get ahead of the
game," he said.
Players also reacted to the recent softening in the oil price, Mr. Gero said. The threat that sustained high
energy prices posed to global growth also was supportive for uncertainty hedges such as gold, and so oil's
easing helped tarnish gold's allure further in recent days, he added.
A swift declaration of victory for either presidential candidate is expected to lead to a further trimming of long
exposure in gold over the near term, market watchers said. But a repeat of the 2000 debacle and a
protracted fight could quickly restore gold's luster.
Downside targets for December futures on any further retreats include $417, $415 and $412, dealers said.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures continued to fall on the Nymex, closing below $50 a barrel for the first time in four
weeks amid dissipating worries about a winter supply crunch. December light, sweet crude futures fell 51
cents to end at $49.62 a barrel.

Investors' Vote: A Winner, Please
Monday's Markets
Investors' Vote: A Winner, Please
By Craig Karmin
813 words
2 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ELECTION DAY is finally here, but investors long ago made their choice clear. They want an immediate and
decisive winner.
With the race for the White House too close to call, fears are growing that investors will awaken tomorrow
morning to find a contested election and the prospect of a lengthy, bitter battle to resolve the race. Tensions
in the stock and foreign-exchange markets, where the dollar is already weak, are particularly high.
"At this point, it comes down to one thing: anything but a hung election," says Russ Koesterich, U.S. equity
strategist for State Street Global Markets in Boston. "The political uncertainty hanging over the market is
worse than at any time since Watergate. And it has the potential to be just as disruptive."
While stocks drifted higher yesterday in light trading, analysts noted that the market was a roller coaster
during the five weeks in 2000 between Election Day and Democratic candidate Al Gore's concession. Stocks
associated with risk were particularly hard hit, and the Nasdaq Composite Index skidded 14%.
"If there's a similar situation this year, the market would be susceptible to being knocked around again by a
series of news bites," says Andy Brooks, head of stock trading at T. Rowe Price. "But hopefully people would
be more careful about pulling the trigger with only incomplete information."
The Dow Jones Industrial Average advanced 26.92 points, or 0.3%, to 10054.39. The index rose for the fifth
consecutive day and is now down 3.8% for the year. The Nasdaq also rose 0.3%, or 4.88 points, to 1979.87,
a four-month high. It is down just 1.2% for the year. The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index edged up 0.03%,
or 0.31 point, to 1130.51. It is ahead 1.7% this year.
Most investors agree that a clear winner would likely give the stock market a boost that could extend until
year's end. They also recall in the weeks that followed the inconclusive presidential election of 2000 that the
dollar fell, and stock-market investors turned defensive, opting for gold stocks and consumer staples. Mr.
Koesterich says mutual-fund buying patterns suggest many institutional investors have been turning to these
same stocks in recent weeks, a trend he expects to accelerate if an uncontested winner fails to emerge.
Some sectors' fates seem more closely tied to one candidate or the other. For example, Mr. Kerry is expected
to be tougher on the health-care industry, and pharmaceutical companies are expected to suffer if he is
elected. Yet Mr. Kerry has also pledged to raise taxes on the wealthiest Americans, which could benefit the
share prices of life-insurance companies that offer investment products with tax advantages.
Mr. Koesterich says he believes the rising cost of oil makes energy-company stocks a good bet, whoever
occupies the White House in 2005. Conversely, he thinks consumer discretionary stocks -- retailing, hotels,
restaurants -- are likely to have a tougher time. He points to data showing wage increases at or below the
inflation rate, rising energy costs, the winding down of the mortgage-refinance boom, and the small chance
of any additional tax cuts coming through next year.
"The lower and middle class are getting squeezed," Mr. Koesterich says. "That means people have less
money to spend at places like Target."
There are also signs that, as the U.S. presidential race tightened in recent weeks, investors world-wide have
been reducing their appetite for risk. Stock funds across the globe investing in the U.S., Europe and Japan
have suffered strong selling, while more-conservative global bond funds have enjoyed buying. "Investors are
being cautious ahead of the U.S. presidential elections," says Brad Durham, a managing director at
EmergingPortfolio.com Fund Research.

--- Page 28 ---
Page 28 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Some investors also say that neither presidential candidate is likely to stem the fall of the U.S. currency. "The
long-term trend of the dollar is down," says Jack Ablin, chief investment officer for Harris Private Bank in
Chicago. That makes overseas investments more attractive, he added.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the New York Stock Exchange, where 1.4 billion shares traded, 1,904 stocks rose and
1,360 fell.
Bonds slipped. The 10-year Treasury note fell 15/32, or $4.6875 for each $1,000 invested, to push up the
yield to 4.089%. The 30-year bond fell 28/32 to yield 4.847%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 106.46 yen, up from 105.84, while the euro fell to
$1.2755 from $1.2798.

Whether Election Is Clear-Cut May Determine Gold's Direction
Commodities Report
Whether Election Is Clear-Cut May Determine Gold's Direction
By Elisabeth Behrmann Dow Jones Newswires
495 words
1 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
LONDON -- A clear-cut U.S. presidential election could mean "sell" for gold-futures bulls, analysts say.
But a protracted election because of legal contests, on the other hand, by prolonging uncertainty might
underpin gold after the election, the analysts say.
Historically, gold prices have dipped after the elections, with the exception of the contested Al Gore-George
W. Bush race when controversy over Florida vote counts delayed the declaration of the winner by five weeks
in 2000, Barclays precious-metals analyst Kamal Naqvi says in a report.
Therefore, a clear win could signal profit-taking by long-position holders in a market due for a correction after
running close to 16-year highs, mostly on weakness of the dollar and worries about inflation sparked by the
record oil price and uncertainties connected to the presidential race. A long position is a bet that prices will
rise.
On Friday, December-delivery gold futures gained $3.30 to $429.40 an ounce at the Comex division of the
New York Mercantile Exchange.
Speculators added to their already record net-long position for gold. The Commodity Futures Trading
Commission's Commitment of Traders Report late Friday noted that the speculator net long position stood at
167,235 contracts held for the week ended Tuesday. The CFTC is the government-oversight agency for the
futures industry.
Because of the long position already held on gold, many market commentators see the election as at best
neutral. "If there are protracted deliberations, this won't be negative for gold, but whether it might be positive
is hard to say," says UBS precious-metals analyst John Reade.
Gold's main driver, the weaker dollar, is expected to firm on a short-term basis should President Bush win by
a clear margin, because of what some see as greater clarity on policy. But indicators for the U.S. economy
still point to further weakness of the U.S. currency against the euro, analysts add.
A changeover in the White House, on the other hand, is likely to create more uncertainty over the direction of
policy, prompting investors to hold on to their long gold positions, says an analyst from a London-based trade
house.
In other commodity markets:
COPPER: Prices rose sharply on a combination of preplaced buy orders and on data suggesting that the U.S.
economy is still healthy. The rally started after copper rebounded following Thursday's break on a knee-jerk
reaction to reports that China would raise its interest rates and continued through the session. November
copper futures gained nine cents to $1.3475 a pound on the Comex.
CRUDE OIL: Futures rose at the Nymex, which analysts attributed partly to concerns of a possible terrorist
attack before the election. But the market's downturn may continue this week. December crude-oil futures
rose 84 cents to $51.76 a barrel.

ConocoPhillips Gets Profit Boost From Oil Prices
ConocoPhillips Gets Profit Boost From Oil Prices
By Russell Gold
382 words
28 October 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A6
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ConocoPhillips said net income shot up in the third quarter as record oil prices overcame declining production
and higher exploration costs. The Houston company also got a boost from its world-wide refinery operations,
which kept high utilization rates.
Large oil companies such as Conoco face limited opportunities to reinvest their windfall profits into
acquisitions and exploration projects to expand -- or at least maintain -- production in future years.
Conoco, however, broke from its peers last month by using its cash to take a plunge into the Russian oil
industry. Conoco announced a $2.4 billion strategic alliance with Russian oil company OAO Lukoil, under
which Conoco will purchase a 7.6% stake in the company.
Conoco is taking advantage of high oil prices to ready its balance sheet for the move and increased its cash
balance by $2.5 billion in the quarter.
Echoing comments from BP PLC executives this week, Conoco Chairman and Chief Executive James Mulva
said the company was facing "inflation" in the cost of steel and oil-field services that was pressuring its
capital-expenditure budget.
However, Mr. Mulva said the planned capital spending wouldn't be increased in 2004; BP said it would
increase its capital budget slightly. The same inflationary trend may affect other oil companies, such as Exxon
Mobil Corp., that announce quarterly earnings this week.
Overall, for the quarter, Conoco reported $2 billion in net income, up 54% from the same quarter a year
earlier, on revenue of $34.7 billion, which rose 31% from a year earlier. The company reported per-share
earnings of $2.86, up from $1.90 a year earlier.
The strong quarter largely resulted from record oil and high natural-gas prices. The company sold its oil for a
world-wide average of $38.77 a barrel, compared with $27 a year earlier. Still, oil production fell to 844,000
barrels a day, down 7.6% from a year earlier. Natural-gas production declined 5.9% to 3.2 billion cubic feet a
day.

One More for the Gipper
One More for the Gipper
By Steve Forbes
1,377 words
28 October 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
This presidential election gives voters their starkest choice since Ronald Reagan challenged Jimmy Carter in
1980. President Carter had left a record of high taxes, economic stagnation and out-of-control inflation on
the domestic front, and an America reeling from setbacks overseas, thanks to a gutted military and a weak,
dithering foreign policy. Reagan, in contrast, promised to slash income-tax rates, rein in runaway government
spending and push deregulation. He also vowed to launch a massive military buildup and a confident,
assertive foreign policy against the Soviet Union.
We've been living off of Ronald Reagan's legacy ever since. We won the Cold War against all expectations.
Our economy blossomed, bursting forth with fantastic innovations. The U.S. has gone from creating about
one-fourth the global GDP to about one-third today. Our stock markets now account for roughly one-half of
global market capitalization, having appreciated nearly tenfold. American household net wealth has never
been higher. Japan's and Europe's net new job creation has been microscopic compared with ours. Our
hollowed-out military has become the best in human history.
But we cannot coast any longer on Reagan's legacy; we must renew and expand it, both at home and
overseas. Giants India and China are waking up economically. A growing number of countries are adopting
the flat tax and becoming more competitive. And as for national security, Islamic extremists seek to sap
Western civilization's morale, thereby enabling these fascists to reign supreme in Muslim countries while
non-Islamic states quiver in fear.
We need a renewed Reaganesque revolution. So which candidate is the most likely Reaganite reformer?
John Kerry? To ask the question is to answer it -- with gales of laughter. President Bush, on the other hand,
has a program as radical as Reagan's was.
On national security, Sen. Kerry would be Carter reincarnated. He would cut and run in Iraq. He would
engage in ineffectual diplomacy with regard to the imminent nuclearization of Iran. He would treat terrorism as
a law-enforcement problem, as if Osama bin Laden and his ilk were simply Islamic Tony Sopranos. Sen.
Kerry is unlikely to "pull the trigger" for America to fight a new, hot war, if such a war became necessary. A
commander in chief must have an overarching goal and sense of direction. Abraham Lincoln had it during the
Civil War. Despite severe, demoralizing setbacks and mistakes, Lincoln never lost sight of what the conflict
was all about and never wavered in his determined prosecution of it. In contrast, Bill Clinton skedaddled out
of Somalia after one hard firefight. Kerry would be a Clinton. He really believes that people like Jacques
Chirac and Gerhard Schroder possess superior, more sophisticated wisdom. One can only wonder what
folderol that French-Swiss boarding school pounded into young John Kerry's head decades ago.
President Bush, unlike Sen. Kerry, has embraced the Wilsonian dream of making the world "safe for
democracy." More, he is actually pushing the process in the Middle East. George Bush is no "realistic"
balance-of-power statesman -- he wants to remake societies just as we remade Japan and Germany after
World War II. Mistakes have been made in Iraq, including busting up Iraq's army last year. But President
Bush's goal is no less the revolutionary: enabling Iraq to become a liberal, more Western-like state. And Iraq
and Afghanistan are the beginning, as he envisions "free governments in the broader Middle East that will
fight terrorists, instead of harboring them."
Sen. Kerry is a trade protectionist who would disrupt one of the greatest wealth-creating, wealth-spreading
mechanisms since World War II. President Bush has learned the painful lesson of trade protectionism -- it
doesn't work economically or politically. That's why his administration worked overtime to get the global Doha
Round of trade talks back on track a few months ago.
On the domestic front, Sen. Kerry is very much the defender of a stagnant, liberal status quo. He would hike
taxes on small-businesspeople and upper income earners who don't have the assets of Teresa Heinz Kerry
to be able to load up on tax-free municipal bonds. Sen. Kerry and his cohorts can't seem to fathom that taxes
are a burden and a price. Increase the price of risk-taking and success, and you'll get less of them. The

--- Page 36 ---
Page 36 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
legendary quarterback John Elway could not have done what he did with a 50-pound football nor could Barry
Bonds with a 100-pound bat. The same holds for taxes and entrepreneurs.
Sen. Kerry wants more government intervention in health care and would move us toward the kind of systems
that are so glaringly failing in Canada and Europe. Social Security? Do nothing, except perhaps some day
raise taxes. Education? Do nothing except throw money at it and resist vouchers, curb charter schools and
oppose anything else the education-undermining National Education Association dislikes.
Sen. Kerry would also pander to fashionable and apocalyptic fantasies, such as global warming or the
ever-recurring notion that we are running out of oil. And to fulfill a fraction of his lavish campaign promises,
John Kerry would make generous-spending George Bush look like a piker.
On domestic issues President Bush is, again, the revolutionary, his vision encapsulated by the words
"ownership society." On Social Security, President Bush wants to allow workers, if they choose, to have a
portion of their payroll taxes go into their own personal retirement accounts. This would make the economy
stronger by creating more capital. Who is going to employ the money better -- investors or politicians? In this
instance, by turning a pay-as-you-go liability into an asset, the president would be taking a page from
Alexander Hamilton. Pull out a dollar bill and you are holding non-interest-bearing debt of the U.S.
government -- clearly a liability -- but it's also currency, something you can use to make investments or buy
goods and services. Social Security reform would undergo a Hamiltonian transformation -- from pure fiscal
drain to wealth creator. Every worker, even those earning minimum wage, could build up real capital.
The president has already signed into law Health Savings Accounts, an IRA-like device that will increasingly
put patients in charge of health-care spending instead of third parties. Again, who is going to get more value
from these health-care dollars -- patients or third parties?
On taxes, the president has made clear his intention to simplify this corruptingly complex code. A flat tax,
anyone?
President Bush also believes in tort reform. Trial Lawyers are laying waste to increasing swathes of American
society. These predators are now threatening our pharmaceutical industry. Sen. John Edwards wants us to
believe that he and his ilk are there to "help the people." What trial lawyers have done to workers
compensation programs in many states puts paid to that notion. Workers comp was once a no-fault system to
ensure that injured workers got medical coverage and received pay. Today, it is too often a feeding trough for
the plaintiff bar.
---
Two areas the president hasn't addressed are reforming the growth-destroying International Monetary Fund,
and bringing in, as Reagan wanted to but couldn't, a commodity or gold-based monetary system that would
stabilize the value of the dollar and give us even lower interest rates than we have today. But the Bush
agenda, nonetheless, is breathtaking in its sweep.
Alas, for a revolutionary, President Bush has been un-Reaganesque in communicating his bold ideas. His
recent fervor on the campaign trail concerning his foreign-policy vision came awfully late. The public should
have been hearing more of this months ago. Ditto the theme of the ownership society; here, too, the
groundwork was not well laid. But these are quibbles over tactics. President Bush's strategic vision is, as our
British allies would put it, "spot on."
Wherever you look, it is George Bush who is the Reagan revolutionary; John Kerry, the reactionary, with a
stand-pat-ness worthy of a 19th-century aristocrat.
---
Mr. Forbes is editor in chief of Forbes Magazine and president/CEO of Forbes Inc.

Japan's New Bills Might Scare Up Some Old Cash
Japan's New Bills Might Scare Up Some Old Cash
By Stanley White Dow Jones Newswires
646 words
27 October 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
B2D
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TOKYO -- The central bank's plan to introduce new paper currency next month could shine an unwanted
spotlight on cash hoarded in Japan's underground economy.
The Bank of Japan's main aim in issuing the new notes is to make life more difficult for counterfeiters. But it
also may encourage those holding large amounts of cash to shift that money into investments, as using large
amounts of old notes could attract unwanted attention after the new paper becomes the standard.
Notes bearing the old design will remain legal tender after the new versions are introduced. But some market
observers speculate that the central bank someday will have to set restrictions on existing notes and recall
them.
"It will become increasingly awkward to use the old notes as the new ones become more common, providing
a catalyst for the spillover of money from the underground economy into the real economy," says Takehiro
Sato, an economist at Morgan Stanley.
The underground economy refers to transactions that aren't reported to tax authorities and covers money
laundering, tax evasion and trade in illicit drugs. People involved in such deals prefer to use cash, as it
doesn't leave a document trail that could tip off tax collectors. Measuring the volume of such activity is difficult
because of its informal and clandestine nature, but it is generally accepted that Japan's underground
economy is much smaller than that in Europe or the U.S.
Takashi Kadokura, senior economist at Dai-Ichi Life Research Institute and an author of several books on the
subject, says the best estimates available put the size of Japan's underground economy at 4.5% of nominal
gross domestic product in 2002, compared with 10% for other developed countries.
Underground economic activity took a hit after Japan's asset-price bubble burst in the early 1990s, but it will
likely grow in coming years, he says.
"Over the next five years I expect Japan's underground economy to expand faster than the potential growth
rate for the legitimate economy, which is from 1.5% to 2%," Mr. Kadokura says. If the government increases
taxes to plug holes in its finances and rein in its growing debt, tax evasion will increase. A rise in
Internet-related crime also should fuel the underground economy, he says.
The central bank, for the first time since 1984, is changing the design of the country's 1,000-yen, 5,000-yen
and 10,000-yen notes (roughly $9, $46 and $92, respectively, at current exchange rates). The new notes,
which will make their debut Monday, come loaded with features such as holograms, ink that can be viewed
only from certain angles, and improved watermarks. These extras make the new bills strikingly different from
their predecessors.
There is about 81 trillion yen of bank notes in circulation. When the central bank last changed the notes'
design, it took about two years to complete the transition. This time the bank has said it could take longer.
Aside from tax evasion, Japanese have hoarded cash rather than deposit it at banks because of fears of
instability in Japan's financial system. But those worries are subsiding as banks reduce bad debts. Mr.
Kadokura pegs the value of hoarded cash in the April-through-June quarter at five trillion yen, while Morgan
Stanley's Mr. Sato says it was at least 17 trillion yen.
A move to unload the old bank notes could push money into gold, Swiss bank accounts or offshore tax
havens in places like Hong Kong, according to Mr. Kadokura. Precious metals offer the security of owning a
physical asset with underlying value while also making it easier for the buyers to hide their identity than if they
bought other assets, some experts say.

PARIS -- Finance Minister Nicolas Sarkozy backed proposals to overhaul a restrictive French retail law,
saying the changes would sustain this year's consumer-spending boom by enabling retailers and
manufacturers to cut prices.
Mr. Sarkozy endorsed a special committee's proposals for gradual change, rather than full-scale repeal, of a
law that prevents the sale of goods below their official cost price. He promised to act on the proposals before
he leaves office at the end of November.
A report recommends excluding promotional expenses from the cost price of products. Deducting those costs
means retailers would be able to reduce sale prices to consumers. Carrefour SA, the world's second-biggest
grocer by revenue after Wal-Mart Stores Inc. of the U.S., and French family-owned chain Leclerc have been
campaigning for such a change.
With the proposals calling for gradual legal amendments, observers say the changes will favor some big
retail-industry entities with pricing power, but won't revolutionize the sector by immediately sparking a price
war.
With France's industrial economy stalling, Mr. Sarkozy has sought to sustain a boom in consumer spending to
drive the euro zone's second-biggest economy to a faster pace of growth than Germany's and Italy's. Earlier
this year, he cajoled retailers and manufacturers into imposing a 2% price cut on a range of 4,000 household
staples.
The French retailing law was introduced in 1996 to protect small and midsize goods makers against the
financial muscle of nationwide retail chains. By banning goods from being sold below cost price, the law
prevented retailers from selling loss leaders in their stores and passing back the burden to manufacturers.
But the law didn't address "forward margins," in which consumer-goods makers give retailers financial
backing to promotional activity designed to boost sales of their goods. Added on to minimum-cost prices, that
practice has artificially inflated what consumers have been paying in stores for years, the report concluded.
By last year, the report said, forward margins accounted for 32% of average net prices paid by consumers.
-- Dow Jones Newswires
EU Says U.S. Export-Tax Fix Benefits Boeing
BRUSSELS -- The U.S. plan to end a trade dispute with the European Union over export-tax breaks may let
Boeing Co. continue the breaks for untold years, EU officials say, exacerbating trade disputes between the
two powers.
While American officials wait for the EU to sign off on the U.S. plan and end its tens of millions of dollars of
monthly punitive tariffs, EU officials are poring over the document's hundreds of pages to determine exactly
how much Boeing benefits and whether they can accept the plan.
The questions arise as the U.S. has sued the EU over government aid to Boeing's European rival, Airbus, a
unit of European Aeronautic Defense &Space Co., leading the EU to countersue over U.S. subsidies to
Boeing.

--- Page 40 ---
Page 40 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The World Trade Organization ruled last year that so-called Foreign Sales Corporations tax breaks in the
U.S. illegally benefited U.S. exporters, and authorized the EU sanctions. Congress adopted legislation that
ladles out new tax breaks to a wide range of U.S. businesses in return for doing away with the FSCs. The
phaseout of the FSCs will take until the beginning of 2007.
Because planes already ordered are grandfathered in, and such orders can take many years to fill,
Chicago-based Boeing is among companies that appear to receive the FSC benefits for far longer, EU
officials said. "There are provisions that maintain the tax breaks for certain companies beyond the end of the
transition period," said EU spokeswoman Arancha Gonzalez. That is a key complaint holding up the EU's
decision about dropping its sanctions, along with other late additions to the legislation the EU is examining.
EU sanctions so far have cost U.S. companies about 220 million euros ($274.7 million) since March. Boeing
was the biggest beneficiary of the FSC tax breaks, but the EU left Boeing off its sanctions list because many
airlines in Europe are customers of the company.
-- Juliane von Reppert-Bismarck and Scott Miller
---
ASIA/PACIFIC
Indonesian Government Study Backs Newmont
JAKARTA, Indonesia -- The Environment Ministry here said the water and air around Newmont Mining
Corp.'s Sulawesi gold mine aren't polluted, despite complaints of health problems by local residents that
have prompted Indonesian police to hold five company executives without charges for almost a month.
Newmont, the world's largest gold miner in terms of production and revenue, faces a $550 million lawsuit by
several villagers who live near its PT Newmont Minahasa Raya mine on the island of Sulawesi. The suit
blames the Denver company for causing tumors and other health problems that followed the mine's 1996
opening.
Newmont insists its operation complies with all Indonesian government guidelines. But the allegations have
triggered a police investigation into Newmont's practice of dumping treated waste rock into nearby Buyat Bay.
Five Newmont executives, including a U.S. citizen, have been detained in a move the U.S. government says
is "inappropriate" and illustrates the unpredictability of Indonesia's legal system -- a problem that has driven
foreign investors away in recent years.
But in the most detailed scientific study released so far, the Environment Ministry is releasing a report today
that shows levels of pollutants in the area's water, air and fish are well below Indonesian limits and global
standards. The study also says two rivers near the Newmont mine are free of mercury or arsenic pollution,
and adds that levels of mercury in fish taken from Buyat Bay are below World Health Organization guidelines.
The results back up a similar but less extensive study conducted in August by the WHO and Japan's National
Institute for Minamata Disease.
"This report represents complete vindication for Newmont and confirms that Newmont has told the truth, has
mined responsibly and has properly managed the environment of Buyat Bay," said Tom Enos, vice president
of Newmont's international operations.
Police officials handling the case couldn't be reached yesterday, but have said in the past that their own tests
show excessive levels of mercury and arsenic in water taken from Buyat Bay.
-- Timothy Mapes
---
THE AMERICAS
Canada Is Set to Raise Rates, at Least for Now
TORONTO -- Economists expect strong economic data will prompt Canada's central bank to raise interest
rates today and again in December, continuing the course it set last month.
All 13 economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires expect the Bank of Canada to raise its trendsetting
overnight target rate to 2.50% from its current 2.25%.

--- Page 41 ---
Page 41 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
They are far more divided, however, on what the bank will do in 2005. Whether the central bank continues to
raise rates depends in part on whether a strong Canadian dollar and sustained high oil prices start to curb
demand.
Canada's economy has turned in some strong statistics recently -- it created 43,200 jobs in September
compared with the 15,000 expected -- and central bankers worry that demand is nearing capacity, spurring
inflation.
While some economists expect the central bank to continue ratcheting up rates into next year, others are
watching the Canadian dollar. Canadian exporters proved surprisingly resilient in their ability to absorb the
currency's 20% appreciation in 2003, but the currency has started to rally robustly in the last few weeks and
could climb significantly higher. Such a move could prompt more caution at the bank in coming months,
economists said.
In addition, higher oil prices could cause significant slowing in the U.S. economy, a key source of demand for
Canada.
-- Dow Jones Newswires
