The Good News, Or the Bad News? --- U.S. Pre-Election Economy Is a Mixed Bag, With Fortunes Varying Even Within the Same Industrial and...
The Good News, Or the Bad News? --- U.S. Pre-Election Economy Is a Mixed Bag, With Fortunes
Varying Even Within the Same Industrial and Regional Sectors
3,658 words
18 October 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ONLY DAYS shy of the presidential election, the nation's economy is neither as weak nor as strong as either
candidate would suggest. Nor is its condition as clear as everyone would like.
For every rosy report, there is one tinged in gray, making it difficult to get a sense of whether the recovery,
which officially began in late 2001, is broad-based and sustainable. Gross-domestic-product growth has
slowed from a pace of 5% a year to roughly 3.5% since March -- a significant slowdown, but compared with a
10-year average growth rate of 3.3%, not that bad.
Housing remains strong, but prices are beginning to flag. Business spending on tech gear is up, but
telecommunications giant AT&T Corp. is laying off 7,000 workers. Aging baby boomers promise to be the
elixir for pharmaceutical companies long term, but short term those companies are struggling. Manufacturing
activity is expected to increase over the next three months, but the sector reported a loss of 18,000 jobs last
month.
Fortunes vary within industries and regions as well. Sales at clothing retailer American Eagle Outfitters Inc.
are up while sales at Limited Brands Inc. are down. In St. Louis, companies in the home-fixtures, furniture
and electronic-cable businesses are closing plants, resulting in the loss of as many as 1,300 workers, while
those that make windows and doors, small engines and home appliances are adding 1,100 new workers.
Oil-field jobs are being created in the Rocky Mountain states and mining jobs in West Virginia. The
automotive-dependent cities ringing the Great Lakes, from Detroit to Buffalo, N.Y., are dragging as General
Motors Corp. and Ford Motor Co. order production cuts. In the San Francisco Federal Reserve District,
growth is solid, thanks to lumber and tech.
While each sector of the economy has companies performing well and those not, noticeably absent among all
of them is a strong sense of optimism. Prudence has replaced exuberance. Companies have more cash than
they have had in decades but aren't spending and hiring as would be expected. After posting strong
job-creation figures in August, small-business owners reduced employment in September for only the second
time in the past 12 months.
The reasons, it seems, transcend individual sectors or regions. Events from hurricanes to terrorism affect how
consumers and chief executives spend. That makes it tougher to declare, with confidence, that the economy
is strong or weak.
-- Clare Ansberry
---
Following are snapshots by Wall Street Journal reporters of various sectors of the economy:
Technology
Business is much better than it has been for most of the past four years -- but not as good as executives and
investors expected, or hoped, it would be.
The good news: Business spending on tech gear rose 14.8% in the second quarter, compared with a year
earlier, faster than any time in the past 20 years except for a short period during the Internet bubble of the
late 1990s. For its most recent quarter, networking kingpin Cisco Systems Inc. reported revenue up 26% from
a year earlier. Likewise, increased Internet advertising spending is propelling strong financial results at
companies such as Yahoo Inc. and Google Inc.
But purchases of some tech items, such as business software, started slowing in June, and tech retailers
reported a sluggish back-to-school season. As revenue growth has slowed, tech companies cut back sharply

--- Page 43 ---
Page 43 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
on orders for semiconductors and other parts. Numerous chip makers, including Intel Corp., Advanced Micro
Devices Inc. and Broadcom Corp., lowered financial forecasts for the third quarter.
Telecommunications companies are hurting as spending on traditional local and long-distance service
continues to decline amid price wars and consumer defections to cell or Internet phones. Lagging sales of
telecom equipment hurt gear makers such as Nortel Networks Corp. and Lucent Technologies Inc. Corning
Inc., which makes fiber-optic telecommunications cable, said earlier this month that it would write off nearly
$3 billion in assets. The wireless business, on the other hand, had 4.6 million new customers signing up in
the second quarter, the best showing since the fourth quarter of 2001, according to Yankee Group.
-- Scott Thurm and Ken Brown
---
Autos
The car industry has see-sawed much of the year, and its short-term outlook remains cloudy. Demand for
U.S. light trucks and cars fell to a relatively weak 15.4 million-vehicle pace in June, bounced back to a 17.2
million vehicle pace in July and fell again in August. In September, model year end closeout deals pushed
sales to a 17.5 million vehicle pace, but industry executives caution that they expect October and November
will be less robust.
The robust second-half recovery that industry executives hoped for early in the year hasn't materialized,
prompting auto makers to order fourth-quarter production cuts. Those actions hit hardest in two presidential
battleground states, Ohio and Michigan, where auto-parts suppliers, already squeezed by rising prices for raw
materials like steel and plastic resins, are concentrated. The pressures recently have forced several large
suppliers to slash profit forecasts for the year and threaten more job cuts.
Auto makers still predict that U.S. vehicle sales will reach 17 million light trucks and cars for the year. But the
patterns of demand are uneven -- with the industry's market-share losers concentrated in Detroit. Rising
gasoline prices could cut consumer ardor for the big sport-utility vehicles that have propped up profits at
General Motors and Ford, while companies such as Toyota Motor Corp., DaimlerChrysler AG's Chrysler
Group, Nissan Motor Corp. and BMW AG have lifted sales this year thanks to popular new models, like the
Chrysler 300 C sedan.
Because of uncertainty about oil prices and jobs, "I think there is a chance the economy could be weaker in
the fourth quarter than it was in the first half of the year," Ford's chief U.S. economist, Jarlath Costello, said
during a conference call Oct. 1.
-- Joseph White
---
Energy
While booming oil and natural-gas prices meant good times in the 1980s for energy producers and
energy-rich communities, today's record-setting prices are providing only a modest boost for the energy
economy -- except for major oil companies' soaring profits. Moreover, the high gasoline and home-heating
prices are hitting Americans in the pocketbook and crimping retail sales.
For the past six months, employment in the oil-field and energy-exploration business turned a corner,
averaging 7.8% gains from a year earlier, according to the Department of Labor. Still, says Barton Smith, an
energy economist at the University of Houston, "the energy economy is still acting relatively conservatively"
because it doesn't want a repeat of the past, when it overbuilt during the good times, only to be hit hard when
prices dropped. Moreover, there is very little crude oil left to drill in the U.S., and the nation is importing
greater amounts of all types of energy, pressuring prices.
Still, confidence that natural-gas prices will remain high has led to investment in pipeline construction,
exploration and manufacturing of products such as specialized drill bits. A considerable percentage of the
new energy jobs are being added in the Rocky Mountain states, where new natural-gas finds are spurring
development. Meanwhile, Houston, a global energy capital, is adding jobs and sends thousands of workers
overseas, exporting industry know-how and importing fat paychecks.
Other sectors of the energy industry aren't doing so well. A power-plant building boom beginning in the late
1990s led to excess capacity that has created a large-scale financial bust and slammed the brakes on that
industry's growth.
-- Russell Gold

--- Page 44 ---
Page 44 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
---
Health Care
The U.S. health-care sector -- already one-seventh of the country's entire economy -- has traditionally been
sheltered from economic ebbs and flows. No longer.
With baby boomers aging, and obesity, diabetes and hypertension on the rise, the pharmaceutical industry
has robust long-term prospects. Short term, though, many companies are struggling with dry new-product
pipelines, imminent generic competition and slowing profit growth. Earlier this month, a generic version of
Pfizer Inc.'s $3 billion-a-year epilepsy pill Neurontin went on sale and is expected to cut deeply into the
company's sales of the drug.
The pharmaceutical industry is still reeling from the recent withdrawal of Merck &Co.'s Vioxx, after a large
study found the arthritis drug doubled the risk of heart attack and stroke. Safety questions are spreading to
similar drugs, such as Pfizer's blockbuster Celebrex, which could damp sales.
Other parts of the health-care sector are feeling the drag of a slow economic recovery. Some managed-care
companies are expected to be hurt by sluggish job growth, cutbacks in employee benefits and higher
premiums, all of which have limited the pool for new health-plan members. That zero-sum game has been
stoking more price competition in recent months, putting pressure on profit margins.
"Heretofore, health care has always been viewed as a noncyclical sector," says Robert Junkin, vice president
and large-cap equity-fund manager at John Hancock Funds in Boston. "But they're not as safe [from
economic cycles] as they used to be."
-- Vanessa Furhmans
---
Airlines
Any hope that some of the recent improvement in the U.S. economy would rub off on the airlines has
disappeared, largely due to skyrocketing fuel prices. Even low-cost carriers such as Southwest Airlines and
AirTran Holdings Inc.'s AirTran Airways, which use fuel-hedging contracts to damp the impact of rising fuel
prices, are now showing the strains. Meanwhile, carriers that have big networks in Florida and along the East
Coast, including Delta Air Lines and JetBlue Airways Corp., canceled thousands of flights because of the
hurricanes.
(MORE)
Industrywide third-quarter losses totaled $1.2 billion, according to J.P. Morgan airline analyst Jamie Baker.
That's only slightly better than the cumulative $1.5 billion in airline losses in last year's third quarter, blamed
on the aftereffects of the U.S. invasion of Iraq. Mr. Baker estimates that had oil prices remained at levels of
last year's third quarter, the industry would have turned in a $200 million profit for the quarter.
All this grim news is even worse for the major airlines that have struggled to change their cost structures
since the Sept. 11, 2001 attacks sent the travel industry into a financial tailspin. UAL Corp.'s United Airlines,
the second-largest U.S. airline, still is wrestling with a plan to emerge from bankruptcy-court protection. US
Airways, which has filed for Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection twice in two years, is trying to impose
concessions on its workers after failing to negotiate cuts with unions. Delta, which emerged from the
post-Sept. 11, 2001 period as one of the strongest carriers financially, is now on the ropes and could file for
bankruptcy-court protection within weeks. All three carriers also have huge worries about their pension
obligations.
Joe Leonard, chief executive of AirTran, says the airline industry needs a "significant reduction in capacity" to
return to profitability. That, and reasonable price increases that stick.
-- Evan Perez
---
Housing
Interest rates remain at historic lows and continue to fuel new-home sales. However, mixed signals in the
housing market keep alive the debate about whether home prices are headed downward or will keep
appreciating but at a slower pace.

--- Page 45 ---
Page 45 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The rate of sales for existing homes nationwide fell, albeit ever so slightly, and the median home price
remained essentially flat for two consecutive months. At the same time, the national median home price of
$190,100 was up 7.3% year to year, and the number of sales was up 2.3% at 6.5 million homes.
Affordability is becoming an issue on both coasts. Flattening sales in markets like Massachusetts suggest
that home buyers are either bristling at prices or can't afford to pay more. In Northern Virginia, average home
prices remained essentially flat at $415,521 in August. Part of the metropolitan-Washington area, Northern
Virginia has seen house prices appreciate more than 50% since 2001. Only 18% of Californians can afford to
purchase a median-priced home in the state, a level not seen since the early 1990s.
The dizzying appreciation of prices in California has helped big home builders deliver blockbuster earnings
and mask weakness in other markets like Denver and Texas. But prices in some key California markets have
leveled off in recent weeks. In fast-growing Riverside, where tract homes are replacing dairy farms, the
median price for existing homes slipped to $310,120 in August from $317,820 the month earlier. The drop in
Riverside comes after a 36% run-up in prices year to year.
-- Queena Sook Kim
---
Manufacturing
U.S. manufacturers are emerging from their most prolonged downturn since the Great Depression. In July,
factory output finally recovered enough to surpass its prerecession peak, set in June 2000, meaning the
sector is churning out more stuff now than it did at the top of the boom. Recent surveys by the Institute of
Supply Management show that manufacturers anticipate their order books, production and employment all
continuing to grow.
"Manufacturers are saying there has been a strong recovery and that they are being helped by investment
spending, not just in the U.S. but around the globe," says Nigel Gault, an economist at Global Insight, an
economic-forecasting firm in Lexington, Mass. "They are moving into a recovery phase, but they're still
digging themselves out of a very deep hole."
The recovery has been bloody and slow. It took 49 months for output to reach prerecession levels, compared
with cycles of the past that usually took about 26 months, says Daniel Meckstroth, an economist at the
Manufacturers Alliance/MAPI, a research group in Arlington, Va.
Tough times have pushed hundreds of weaker companies into bankruptcy or the arms of acquirers, while
global competition continues to pressure the sector from all sides. Soaring prices for oil, steel and other raw
materials also cloud the outlook. Manufacturers are finally spending more on new equipment, but they remain
hesitant to expand too much, fearing overcapacity.
Meanwhile, manufacturing's job picture remains a sore point. Factories shed 18,000 jobs last month,
according to the Labor Department, the first decline in two months. There are now 14.4 million manufacturing
workers in the U.S., down three million from July 2000, and most experts believe the sector will recover only a
small portion of those lost jobs. Manufacturers have benefited from rapid productivity growth in recent years,
which allows them to get by with fewer workers.
-- Timothy Aeppel
---
Retail
After a strong first half, the industry has a more uncertain outlook for the next few months. At high-end
department stores such as those run by Neiman Marcus Group Inc. and Nordstrom Inc., wealthy customers
continue to spend freely. For everyone else -- most of America's families, and the stores that serve them --
the picture is more worrisome.
September was the fourth straight month that retail sales, measured by sales at stores open at least a year,
were generally disappointing. According to Goldman Sachs, such sales eked out the smallest gain this year in
August, based on a weighted average, and the second-smallest increase in September.
To be sure, some retailers, such as Wal-Mart Stores Inc., continue to open new stores at a rapid pace. But
many companies aren't seeing robust sales for several reasons: The tax-cut stimulus from last year has worn
off. Wages have been growing slowly, particularly among lower-income households. Discounters say their
customers are drained by high gasoline prices, and elevated energy bills are expected to cause trouble this

--- Page 46 ---
Page 46 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
winter. Even the spring buzz about new women's fashions -- more colorful and ladylike than in past years --
has died down.
Consulting firm Retail Forward Inc., which estimates future shopper demand in a monthly index, predicts that
October's retail sales will be softer overall than September's, as middle-income and lower-income households
rein in their spending, offsetting improving confidence by upmarket consumers.
Most economists believe holiday spending will diverge along similar lines, producing a mixed picture.
Predictions for November and December sales combined, compared with the same period the previous year,
range from a 3% to a 7% gain. Still, last week Wal-Mart Chief Executive Lee Scott told analysts he was
optimistic about the holidays.
-- Amy Merrick
---
Advertising and Media
While there are signs of a mild advertising recovery in parts of print media, local radio and television markets
remain soft, except for those stations in presidential swing states. Auto companies have cut back
network-television ads, compounding a shift toward newer media like the Internet and cable TV. Moreover,
advertisers are putting more money into product placement, online promotions and offbeat vehicles, such as
street theater.
National TV advertising, which generally comprises corporate brand and image campaigns, may be a little
more robust than local advertising. In the late spring, when advertisers made their annual commitments for
the fall season, the roughly $9.5 billion in total ad spending committed was on par with last year, despite
growing advertiser concerns about the effectiveness of TV commercials.
Still, whether the commitments turn into spending depends on the networks' ability to deliver on ratings
guarantees, and on economic conditions. Lauren Rich Fine, who follows advertising for Merrill Lynch, pared
her 2004 forecast for U.S. advertising growth to 6.5% from 6.6%, excluding direct-mail ads. With 2005 lacking
a presidential election or an Olympics -- two traditional catalysts of ad spending -- the near future is less
clear.
In late September, both Colgate-Palmolive Co. and Unilever said they intended to increase spending on
marketing, even though that would hurt earnings. Nevertheless, ad-space buyers remain skeptical. Speaking
before a gathering of industry executives in early October, Jon Mandel, chairman of Grey Global Group Inc.'s
MediaCom U.S., a media-services firm, said he would put faith in such intentions only when he started to see
money actually flow.
-- Brian Steinberg and Martin Peers
---
Entertainment
The entertainment industry has had its fair share of big hits this year. In the movie business, Mel Gibson's
"The Passion of the Christ" kicked off the year with a surprise bang, pulling audiences back into movie
theaters after a tepid 2003. "Shrek 2" and "Spider-Man 2" followed with strong performances, each selling
more than $300 million of tickets in North America.
However, the all-important summer season saw audiences slip again. For the first nine months of the year,
movie-theater attendance was down 1.2%, according to box-office tracking firm Exhibitor Relations Co. By
raising ticket prices, movie theaters kept revenue growth in positive territory though: Overall revenue rose
2.4% to $7.25 billion. The Christmas period will be key in determining whether the industry can hold on to
box-office revenue gains. During November and December, Hollywood studios will fire a barrage of
big-budget movies into theaters. Among them: computer-animated features "The Incredibles" and "Polar
Express."
Hollywood is banking on the real star being the DVD, however. The silver disc is expected to rack up another
robust year, although there are concerns that its surge could peak soon as homes become saturated with
DVD players.
(MORE)
Signs of life, meanwhile, are returning to the piracy-ravaged music industry after a harrowing three-year slide.
By volume, album sales rose 5.8% in the U.S. year to date, according to Nielsen SoundScan, which tracks
music sales. For the first time in four years, two artists, Norah Jones and Usher, each sold more than one

--- Page 47 ---
Page 47 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
million copies of an album in a single week. On a global level, music sales continue to drop, but at a slowing
rate.
Stealing the show overall, the entertainment industry's fastest growth business, videogames, is gearing up for
another strong performance due partly to the arrival of two key games: Grand Theft Auto: San Andreas and
Halo 2. Nintendo Co. and Sony Corp. also will introduce hand-held game players. Growth is expected to
accelerate as new videogame consoles come on stream.
-- Merissa Marr
---
Food
Already pressured by retail consolidation, private-label brands and value-conscious consumers, food giants
raised prices for key products like coffee, cheese and cereal to help offset skyrocketing costs of milk, coffee
beans and soybeans. Prices for food at the supermarket rose 4% in the third quarter, according to an
American Farm Bureau Federation survey.
But such pricing moves have gotten increasingly difficult for food makers, who are squeezed on one side by
the rising power of retailers such as Wal-Mart Stores Inc. and on the other by finicky consumers looking for a
good deal.
Kraft Foods Inc., General Mills Inc. and Unilever have all issued earnings warnings in the past year, leading
many investors to question how much pricing power food makers have. The nation's biggest milk producer,
Dean Foods Co., lowered its earnings guidance last month for the third quarter and the full year. Dean's chief
executive, Gregg Engles, said that while dairy commodity prices fell in July and August, competitive
pressures were "more intense than we expected."
"Companies that have let price gaps widen between them and private label have paid a very dear price in
how swiftly market share has shifted to other brands," says Andrew Lazar, who follows food companies for
Lehman Brothers. Meanwhile, higher-end food purveyors, such as Starbucks Corp. and Whole Foods Market
Inc., have been doing fine, showing that there is a market for pricey goods if they are positioned correctly.
-- Sarah Ellison

Some manufacturers already have plans for spending their windfall. The bill also will help individuals,
especially from states with sales taxes but no state or local income tax.

The Fed's $53 Oil
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Fed's $53 Oil
258 words
8 October 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Federal Reserve Governor Ben Bernanke is speaking up again, and it may not be a coincidence that oil
prices also keep rising. Mr. Bernanke suggested yesterday that softer economic growth could trigger a pause
in the Fed's determination to end its easy money policy.
Bonds promptly fell out of bed, oil prices continued their rise through the $53-a-barrel barrier, and the dollar
price of gold closed above $419 an ounce, close to its recent highs. We suspect this has something to do
with markets fearing that the Fed may lose its focus on its core mission, which is supposed to be maintaining
price stability.
Commodities like oil are the canary in the coal mine of future inflation. Oil in particular has an inelastic
supply curve, as economists put it, meaning that as demand surges in a growing world economy, the price
tends to surge with it. Whatever the impact from Chinese demand or pipeline damage from hurricanes, the
Federal Reserve has also contributed to $53 oil.
It was about a year ago, after all, that Mr. Bernanke was saying that inflation had been conquered and
deflation was the problem. He and his colleagues were later surprised when the economy surged and
commodity prices began shooting up, and only then did they begin to change their historically low interest
rate policy. They have since seemed committed to making up for that mistake, but having Mr. Bernanke out in
public raising doubts about this resolve doesn't help the Fed's credibility.

America's New Jingoes
America's New Jingoes
By George Gilder
1,828 words
8 October 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
With markets at last recovering from the turn-of-the-century crash and the attacks of September 11, it is an
opportune time to debate America's future in a rapidly changing world economy. America's establishment of
liberal economists and media pundits, however, are joining in a cramped new nationalism that jeopardizes the
future of American technology and prosperity. Like reactionary jingoes of the past, they are priming John
Kerry with the delusional view that the U.S. and its workers are somehow victims of global trade and capital
movements. But as the presidential debates turn to domestic policy and economics, voters need to recognize
the realities of world economic transformation and the real threats to American dominance.
In a popular image, "Benedict Arnold CEOs" are seen to be offshoring factories and outsourcing jobs.
Once-prestigious economists such as Paul Samuelson and once-responsible analysts such as Paul Krugman
and once-sensible financial pundits such as Lou Dobbs are adducing twisted new theories of how free trade
is no longer a win-win proposition. The alleged victims of expanding trade and globalization run from
low-wage American workers to Third World environments, from aging American software engineers to overall
U.S competitiveness. Mr. Kerry is showing a disturbing receptivity to this alarming turn among his economic
allies and advisers.
---
With international trade expanding as a share of global GDP, these pundits want to revisit and politicize all
trade compacts in order to incorporate favored rules for ecological and labor policy, presumably lowering the
permitted exhalations of carbon dioxide by low wage workers abroad. They want to revive the entirely
spurious issue of dumping, a system of price traps and controls used only against foreign rivals outperforming
U.S. companies, usually using U.S. components.
Central to their concerns is the hugely beneficial emergence of China from barbarism and India from penury
to become crucial sources of skills and components for U.S. companies and by far the fastest growing
markets for high technology goods. But in a remarkably callous and destructive revulsion, these new
self-defeating nationalists of the left want to deprive Indian engineers of jobs serving American companies
and to harass China over spurious charges of currency "manipulation" and picayune technicalities of ever
more complex U.S. trade laws.
In the crucial area of technology, the new nationalists claim to want a futuristic industrial policy. But when
closely examined, their support for technology shuns most existing markets, energy sources, and technology
companies in favor of subsidies for fashionably flaky or far out technologies, such as nanotech and hydrogen
cars. Promises to achieve energy independence, for example, invoke the usual heliotropic mantras and
Quixotic tilts at windmills, while opposing any demonstrably effective means of energy production, such as
new drilling for oil and gas in Alaska or new generations of nuclear plants.
All these policies reflect an era when the U.S. was the global capitalist spearhead, dominant in technology
deployment, and our major rivals routinely crippled themselves with socialism. Through these decades, the
U.S. could afford to indulge in chemophobia, xenophobia, eco-legal self-abuse, rampant litigation, and
regulatory excess without gravely harming what remained the world's lowest taxed and freest major economy.
Today, however, our major rivals are China, Taiwan, South Korea, Japan, and other Asian tigers that are
more aggressively capitalist and more resourceful in deploying technology than we are. Preparing to join
them are Russia and its Baltic and Eastern European neighbors now competing with each other in lowering
tax rates to flat levels in the low teens. China has become a new Wild East of enterprise, with the heart of its
economy located in "free zones" with lower tax rates and regulations than Silicon Valley. In energy policy,
China is far more advanced than the U.S. and has announced plans to build 60 new nuclear power plants
over the next five years; Russia is unleashing petroleum venturers across its endless reaches of promising
territory.

--- Page 52 ---
Page 52 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In high technology, China today has more Internet connections and its free zones have higher bandwidth than
does the U.S. South Korea has 40 times more communications bandwidth per capita than we do. Japan is
also far ahead of us in broadband deployment. India is advancing rapidly, particularly in software, fueled by
graduates of its rigorous and competitive school system. Asian countries annually graduate 10 times more
electrical engineers than does the U.S., and the U.S. still feels free to send back to their homes foreign
engineers and scientists graduating from advanced education in the U.S.
At the same time that our rivals have turned sharply toward capitalism and technology, the U.S. intelligentsia
is turning toward a technophobic fear of useful chemicals (DDT, PCBs) and fervent opposition to
world-leading deployment of technology in the U.S. by such corporations as Wal-Mart and General Electric. In
passionate support for the Kyoto treaty, they arouse false fears of global warming (global temperatures today
stand just below the average of the last two millennia), and raise the hopes of foreign and U.S. bureaucrats
bent on usurping control of U.S. energy usage from our citizens.
At the same time that this movement offers reefs of proposals that would ensnare the U.S. economy in new
taxes and regulations, its economists accept a set of unduly pessimistic beliefs about current U.S.
performance. Not only is the U.S. wartime budgetary deficit strangely seen as evidence of runaway tax cuts
for the rich, but a growing trade gap is seen as evidence of a gullible America absorbing goods dumped from
abroad while outsourcing jobs to cheap labor overseas. Over and over, the new American jingoes depict the
U.S. as somehow a victim in the international economy.
Warning of a possible turn against the dollar by current holders of the currency, such establishment figures as
Robert Rubin and Pete Peterson, and their academic and journalistic vicars, urge tax hikes, devaluations,
trade restrictions, higher minimum wages for labor, as if the U.S. were a precarious Third World country to be
pushed into crisis by the International Monetary Fund.
Their case is a tangle of contradictions. We are to run down the dollar, creating inflation and inflation
premiums in interest rates, increasing the costs of all technology businesses that import crucial components
(nearly all), crashing our markets ourselves, in order to prevent foreign banks from selling our securities,
running down the dollar, and crashing our markets. Beat 'em to the punch. Hey, sounds smart, if the balance
of payments were a sign of failing U.S. competitiveness rather than of flourishing American growth. Smart
policy perhaps if the U.S. were likely long to remain the largest market for American technology companies.
But this is no longer the 19th century, when trade in goods dominated the balance of payments and a gap
was filled with gold shipments across treacherous seas. In the 21st century, capital movements traveling on
fiber lines at the speed of light precede and dominate goods movements, which are inhibited by all the
obstacles of international trade. By definition, a trade gap means a capital surplus. For the last several years,
foreign movements of capital to purchase long-term assets in the U.S. have exceeded the trade gap by
between $200 billion and $300 billion per year. Most of the trade gap is capital goods used by American
companies to compete internationally. Foreigners want to invest in the U.S. because we have the most
creative and entrepreneurial culture and by far the deepest and most liquid financial markets. By Walter
Wriston's Law, capital goes where it is welcome and stays where it is well treated. People send us money
because we welcome it and treat it well. The only way we can stop them is by slowing U.S. economic growth
and destroying our uniquely resourceful financial industries.
While regularly incurring trade gaps and budgetary deficits, our economy has grown since the early 1980s
from a level, depending on dollar valuation, between one-fifth and one-fourth of global GDP to close to
one-third of global GDP last year. During this upsurge entirely unexpected by the same economists now
advising Sen. Kerry, U.S. per capita GDP surged from 4.7 times per capita global GDP in 1980 to 6.5 times
per capita global GDP in 2003. The U.S. created some 36 million net new jobs at ever higher levels of
productivity and earnings, while Europe and Japan created scant employment at all outside of government
and entered a productivity slump that continues today. Meanwhile, the U.S. won the Cold War, and since
1990 its stock markets soared from less than one-third to roughly one-half of global market cap. The net
wealth of U.S. households in real terms trebled to all-time records ($45.9 trillion at last report). Debt has been
shrinking as a share of overall national assets, which now stand at a level near $80 trillion.
Throughout this period of expanding trade, catastrophist economists like Mr. Rubin, Mr. Krugman, Fred
Bergsten and Mr. Peterson have been predicting the same disastrous flight from the dollar that they would
cause by their policies. The remedy is always tax rate increases and spending cuts -- which always tend to
mean reductions in defense, the only kind of spending that Congress permits to be cut.
---
These proposals are silly and self-defeating. They reflect the continuing bankruptcy of demand-side
economics. Empirically, the supply-side engine of global growth revived by President Bush with crucial tax
rate reductions has outperformed all other countries. To adopt some panicky austerity regime now would
crash our competitiveness without achieving any significant benefit.

--- Page 53 ---
Page 53 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
(MORE)
The U.S. today stands at a crossroads. The key economic issue confronting the next president is whether to
embrace the policies of decline and sclerosis that afflict old Europe and have left generations of young people
unemployed; or whether to enlist with Asia in the supply-side policies of dynamism and growth that have
brought more human beings out of poverty than any other regimes in world history.
It should be an easy choice. The American left once displayed a real concern for poor people, but today they
exhibit merely a morbid envy of the rich. Once they supported American engagement in the world. Today,
they retreat to a timorous parochialism. Now it is President Bush who shows compassion for the world's poor
and confidence rather than timidity before the forces of global capitalism. It is Mr. Bush who is embracing
Asian dynamism rather than Eurosclerosis. For America, that is the winning side.
---
Mr. Gilder, a senior fellow at Seattle's Discovery Institute, is editor of the Gilder Technology Report.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Public Promises Are Nice-- If You Can Pay for Them" -- WSJ Oct.
29, 2004)

Guess Who's Coming to Europe...
Guess Who's Coming to Europe...
By Norman Stone
1,909 words
6 October 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Turkey in Europe? That question is going to be decided today, when the European Commission presents its
report on Turkey's candidature, and it looks as if the answer will be "yes." The country has been formally
associated with Europe by treaty since 1963, and though the application to join the European Union has been
repeatedly frustrated, the likelihood is that talks about accession will soon start. There will be jubilation in
Turkey -- from bankers who see more foreign investment for the fastest-growing economy in the continent, to
humble young guides in Cappadocia, who think the streets of Dortmund are paved with gold and who, to get
there hitherto, have had to marry whooping German divorcees twice their age.
Thinking Europeans are divided. There are those who see Europe as ending at the last Gothic cathedral, the
real clash of civilizations being between Latin and Orthodox. They would knock out Greece. Others take a
wider view, agreeing with the Americans that the European Union is just a civilian twin of NATO, which is
certainly where it started -- even the euro was first suggested by a Marshall Plan official.
To resolve the disagreement, the EU drew up, in 1993, a checklist of things that candidates would have to
display -- standard "End of History" stuff about political rights and a market economy. Turkey has been
meeting these "criteria" very well, even providing for Kurdish language lessons, following EU lectures as to
minority rights. Kurdish languages (there are seven) had not earlier been promoted, on the grounds that they
did not amount to a real education -- the attitude taken by the French over Breton; and, if this Scotsman may
say so, thank God, also by the makers of the Anglo-Scottish Union in the 18th century when it came to
Highland Gaelic. The lessons have been taken up by hardly more than a hundred romantic souls, Kurdish
parents being rather harder-headed in this matter than the bright-eyed lecturers from countries that simply do
not have linguistic minorities at all, having wiped them out, or, as with the Swedes, gone in for sterilization of
100,000 Lapps.
The negotiations will be tortuous and lengthy: devils are in the detail. Germans fear an influx of Anatolian
peasants, and a well-known historian, Hans-Ulrich Wehler, sounded the alarm: Would they ever assimilate?
Ironically, he himself wrote a trenchant article about Polish immigration to the industrial Ruhr over a century
ago, showing that it took four generations to become German, and blaming the Prussian state for its belittling
ways.
Religious freedom is another knotty matter: one man's way to heaven is another's sleepless night. But the
main lines of Turkey's road toward Europe are clear, and this is right and proper. Turkey is the only place
between Athens and Singapore that offers its people a decent life, and the Europeans would have to be fools
to let it go. With the foreign investment that usually comes with the initial Euro-boom, Turkey will be another
Spain, the European country (and former empire) she most resembles.
What is perhaps so odd about present developments is that it is a religious-minded government that is taking
Turkey into Europe. That government emerged with considerable luck, which consists, in the first instance, of
the utter ineptitude of its opponents. Most Turks, even if they observe some of the Islamic pieties, would
certainly not vote for an Islamic government, and the brief attempt at one such, in 1996-97, ended in ridicule.
Yet the coalitions that followed were a mating of carps and rabbits, as the Germans used to say. In 2001,
there was a crash, and they followed IMF (and European, especially German) prescriptions to sort it out. This
meant real austerity, with bankruptcies and serious unemployment. Farm subsidies were cut, and the pension
age was raised. Maybe the coalition government could have ridden out the resulting unpopularity but the
carps and rabbits started quarrelling and an early election was called -- which wiped them all out. The
beneficiary has been the present government, led by men who had learned from the blundering of their
erstwhile Islamic colleagues, and who claim that they are just creating a Muslim Turkish equivalent of the
Christian Democrats who have been so successful in much of Western Europe since 1949.
There is a rump Islamic party -- its posters show a little girl in a wimple with the legend, "My Mummy doesn't
have to work as a barmaid." Its rather grim candidates try to affect a JFK grin, modern-fashion, but it is not
convincing, and the party got nowhere in the last election. By contrast, the new party, which repudiates most

--- Page 55 ---
Page 55 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
of the literal-minded pieties, won well over a third of the vote, and thus, under the rules, has a strong majority
in parliament. True, the backwoodsmen sometimes make trouble. Recently, some of them were wanting
adultery to be a crime, just when all seemed to be going well for Turkey's eventually joining the EU.
Now, this was widely misunderstood as a criminalization of Turkish Kareninas and Bovarys. It was not. The
fact is that there has been enormous resentment in the provinces of girls known as "Natashas" -- blond, leggy
creatures from the former Soviet Union (the Russian embassy gets angry if you say they are Russian) who
have been driven into prostitution by events back home. They hit Turkey like a tidal-wave, and caused havoc.
God-fearing men, married off in the traditional fashion in their teens (as happened with the prime minister's
own son, though he was packed off to America), suddenly discovered these honey-pots, and in many cases
either dumped a wife who could not imagine what she had done wrong, or otherwise hollowed out the
marriage. In a traditional-minded society, where men, perhaps with a bit of straying, nevertheless keep
marriages going, the Natashas acted like a bomb. In small towns in Cappadocia, for instance, there can be as
many as 50 of them, and they cause an immense amount of misery. Sophisticated people in the big cities
may just laugh it off, but it is not funny in these provincial places, and so a criminalization of adultery was
briefly put into the law.
It may be that enemies of Turkey in Europe egged the chief opposition party into covert encouragement of the
law, in order to discredit the government and the application to join Europe -- conspiracies abound. But the
law itself, until it was discarded, reflected a concern among well-intentioned conservatives who think of the
family as the bedrock of society. No doubt the government proceeded somewhat clumsily, but its overall
position was not as cruel and senseless as some Europeans had immediately concluded.
Prime Minister Recep Tayyip Erdogan is a formidable politician, the most formidable since Turgut Ozal a
quarter-century ago. He came up the hard way from a district in Istanbul which still had open sewers, and
manages to control what is at bottom a very divided party. It greatly helps that he can play the European card,
and that he can profit from a post-crisis economic recovery that could easily develop into a Euro-boom.
One thing this government is doing is to attack seriously what had become the greatest problem Turkey had
to face -- inflation. For 30 years, it had gone remorselessly ahead, at about 80% per annum. A general,
perturbed about the possible loss of patriotism that might come with Turkey's joining Europe, said that it
would be a great insult to remove the head of Ataturk from the banknotes; a foreign office man answered that
putting the great man's face above a figure of 20 million was a bigger insult. Ten thousand on a banknote
might mean that minor virtues go, and fun can be had. A hundred thousand means blood on the streets -- a
Pinochet if you are lucky, a Lenin or a Hitler if you are not. The Turkish inflation was not altogether real, in
the sense that prices did not go up by much in dollars, and, after Ozal, Turks could just change their money
into greenbacks at a bank machine.
The system really just meant that the government took a sort of arbitrage profit, gaining in the time that
elapsed between the money's buying the goods, and the goods-men's putting it into dollars. The inflation
never became stratospheric, as might have been expected -- partly because, Turkey being a linchpin of the
world order, the IMF would come to the rescue, and partly because interest-rates were enormous (125% or
thereabouts). You could get cheap, long-term credit if you were an insider, and various politicians were
caught doing favors to their sisters and their cousins, whom they reckon up by dozens, and this contributed
much to the electoral success of the Erdogan party.
The previous coalition government had launched a program to bring the Turkish currency into line: budget
surplus, measures to encourage foreign investment and privatization, and cleaning up the banks. These had
become very lazy, with the gifts that the financial system handed to them in overnight repo rates. Some
collapsed; there have been mergers; the survivors have seen the sort of profitable openings that financial
collapses bring, and there is a universal sense, nowadays, of an economy that is moving -- capital investment
and productivity bounding up, foreign trade heading for Russia's, without any raw materials to speak of,
beyond tomatoes. The inflation has been contained for the past two years, and early next year the
bank-notes are to lose six noughts.
---
(MORE)
So: a Muslim-minded government, presiding over a glad morning in Europe. The professional (though not the
business) classes in Turkey do not trust it at all: What is its agenda? One of my graduate students says we
should set an exam question: "Economy, Politics, Islam: choose two." Head-scarved illiterate women in the
kitchen? Bearded cousins presiding over the utilities? So far, it has to be said, the government has been
exceedingly careful. After all, Turkish Islam had, from the beginning, a cosmopolitan side. Besides, this is
very far from being the first time that Turkey has had a government supportive of religion, the free market and
the NATO connection: Turgut Ozal led such a regime, and one of its targets, in the then anti-inflation
program, was the trade unions.

--- Page 56 ---
Page 56 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Resident foreigners -- there are a great many of us, from grand bankers and McKinsey people to English
schoolteachers and not a few Americans running away from cigarette persecution back home -- are in favor
of this government, so far. It is honest and efficient. True, we live mainly in the big cities, not in smaller places
where creeping Islamization is more likely; and true, too, that with foreign passports we can escape if the
answer to my student's question is "Islam and politics." But the fact is that this is a very good moment for
Turkey. A synthesis of Ataturkist republicanism and the Ottoman past is on the cards, brought about through
a Europe that is at last going to do the right thing.
---
Mr. Stone, former professor of history at Oxford, is a professor at Ankara's Bilkent University.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: In Joining the EU, Turkey Should `Privatize' Religion" -- WSJ Oct.
13, 2004)

Price of Gold Takes a Tumble After Dollar Stages Advance
Commodities Report
Price of Gold Takes a Tumble After Dollar Stages Advance
Dow Jones Newswires
413 words
5 October 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- A rise in the value of the dollar against the euro and other major currencies sparked selling
pressure by large speculative players in the gold-futures market.
Gold for December delivery on the New York Mercantile Exchange's Comex division fell $5.60 to $415.60 an
ounce.
Gold, which is priced in dollars, becomes more expensive for foreign buyers as the U.S. currency
strengthens, which in turn can decrease demand for the yellow metal.
"Dollar movement continues to be the main factor for the gold market," said Dave Meger, analyst at Alaron
Trading. Analysts also said many players had put on fresh long positions in the gold market ahead of the
weekend round of Group of Seven meetings in Washington.
"Last week, a lot of large funds and individuals placed bets on the results of the G-7," said Leonard Kaplan,
president of Prospector Asset Management. "There was some talk last week that the G-7 would make some
comments on the twin deficits of the U.S. and that perhaps China would unpeg the yuan to some extent.
"The G-7 said nothing and all those bets had to be unwound," said Mr. Kaplan, pointing to the losses seen
yesterday in gold futures.
Lower crude-oil prices also fed into the negative atmosphere for gold prices. In recent weeks, rising crude
prices, considered an inflationary economic factor, have supported gold, which is a traditional inflation
hedge.
Mr. Kaplan sees additional room for lower gold prices in the near term. "The gold market is really in a trading
range between $380 and $420. We tested the top of that range last Thursday and Friday, and it became clear
those prices were unsustainable."
In other commodity markets:
COFFEE: Futures on the New York Board of Trade fell on technically related selling pressure and large
speculative players exiting long positions. Coffee for December delivery fell 3.95 cents to 76.20 cents a
pound.
CRUDE OIL: Futures at the New York Mercantile Exchange fell, as easing fears of civil war in Nigeria's
oil-rich south offset deteriorating oil production in the Gulf of Mexico. The shallowness of the decline,
however, left traders unsure of whether the market was regrouping before retesting levels above $50 a barrel,
or teetering on the brink of further losses. Crude oil for November delivery fell 21 cents to $49.91 a barrel.

Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot ... And Not: Emerging Markets Bounced Back in September --- Surge in the Price of Oil...
Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot ... And Not: Emerging Markets Bounced Back
in September --- Surge in the Price of Oil, Commodities Aid Rebound; Japan Doesn't Fare as Well
By Craig Karmin
680 words
4 October 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A September surge in oil and other commodity prices helped many emerging markets bounce back. But
higher energy costs, and fresh concerns about Japan's economy, cooled down the once-hot Tokyo stock
market.
The Morgan Stanley Capital International emerging-markets index jumped 5% for the month in dollar terms,
lifting the index back into positive territory year-to-date. Stock markets in Russia, Indonesia, and Mexico all
enjoyed a big bounce in September.
Not coincidentally, these markets are oil producers. An 18% surge in the price of crude oil -- which hit record
nominal highs of $50-a-barrel last week -- made it the most attractive investment in September. This marked
the second time in three months that crude oil enjoyed a monthly gain of more than 15%.
The prices of many commodities, like gold and other precious metals, also continued their march higher. The
Dow Jones World Basic Resources Index rose 6%. These gains also helped boost the currencies of natural
resource-producing nations, such as the Canadian dollar. The currency rose 3.9% last month and is around
an 11-year high versus the U.S. dollar.
"The way to profit from a rebound in the global economy is resources and commodities," says Brian
Gendreau, an emerging-markets analyst at Heckman Global Advisors, a New York-based independent
research firm. "Markets that were direct or indirect plays on this theme outperformed for the month."
Stock markets in the developing world had been in sharp decline since the middle of the first quarter, when
risk appetite began to wane alongside heightened fears of rising U.S. interest rates. A surprise victory by the
opposition party in the Indian elections further rattled the markets, especially in Asia.
The opposite was true last month. The victory by Susilo Bambang Yudhoyono in Indonesia's first direct
presidential election fueled interest in that market, helping lift the MSCI Indonesian index 12% in dollar terms
just in September. In Brazil, where recent credit-rating upgrades attracted investors to Brazilian debt, the
MSCI Brazil index rose nearly 10% in dollar terms.
Rising oil prices also helped the MSCI Russia index gain 8% for the month, even though energy giant Yukos
has lost about two-thirds of its value over the past year amid a government probe of the company. Even India
continued to make up for lost ground after falling 17% in one day in May following the Congress Party's
victory. A more positive reassessment of the election results helped the Bombay Sensex rise 9% in
September.
At the other end, higher energy prices punished oil importing nations. Japan was among those hardest hit;
the Nikkei Stock Average finished the month with losses in nine of the final 10 trading days. The blue-chip
index slid into negative territory for the month with a 0.9% decline at a time when most foreign stock indexes
gained.
Other Japanese stock indexes did even worse. The Jasdaq Index of smaller Japanese stocks fell 6%, while
the Dow Jones Japan Financial Index also tumbled 6%. Fund managers said that these declines reflect
worse-than-expected gross domestic product numbers in Japan and fears that domestic demand had been
overstated.
"Expectations for Japan and its economic rebound got too high, so a lot of stocks got overvalued," says Eric
Ritter, a portfolio manager for Driehaus Capital Management in Chicago.
The dollar suffered modest losses, giving back some of the gains made earlier this year. But U.S. stock
indexes fared relatively well for September, which historically has been one of the toughest months of the
year. The Dow Jones Industrial Average was down 0.9%, falling in September for the sixth consecutive year.

--- Page 62 ---
Page 62 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But the Nasdaq Composite Index jumped 3.2% to break its streak of four straight down periods in September.
The Russell 2000 of U.S. small stocks rose 4.6%.

Dow Industrials Top 10100 Mark as Oil Price Declines
By E.S. Browning
375 words
30 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
OIL PRICES retreated for the first time in two weeks, sending stocks up for a second day and propelling the
Dow Jones Industrial Average back above 10100.
Stocks benefited from revised government data showing the economy grew at a 3.3% annual rate in the
second quarter, above the 2.8% initially estimated. And some money managers were buying stocks to round
out portfolios before the quarter ends today.
But the more-rapid economic growth was bad for bond prices, which fell on worries that the Federal Reserve
will have to accelerate its interest-rate increases to prevent inflation.
The Dow industrials rose 58.84 points, or 0.58%, to 10136.24, still down 2.9% for the third quarter and off 3%
for 2004. The industrials began the day with a decline. They turned up midmorning as oil prices began to fall,
following the release of Energy Department data showing stronger-than-expected U.S. crude-oil inventories
last week.
Oil futures, which were trading above $50 a barrel before the inventory news, finished at $49.51 on the New
York Mercantile Exchange, posting their first decline in 10 trading days. Reports that rebels in oil-producing
Nigeria had accepted a cease-fire also helped knock oil prices down.
Stocks also benefited from news that Wal-Mart Stores would expand its share buyback program.
Gold rose 50 cents to $413 an ounce, another one-month high.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.43%, or 4.74 points, to 1114.80, while the Nasdaq Composite
Index advanced 1.29%, or 24.07 points, to 1893.94.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.36 billion shares traded, 1,807 stocks rose and 1,481 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 22/32, or $6.875 for each $1,000 invested. The yield rose to
4.094%. The 30-year bond was down 1 3/32 to yield 4.867%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 110.90 yen, down from 111.37, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.2329 from $1.2319.

Stocks Revive, Shaking Off Oil-Price News
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks Revive, Shaking Off Oil-Price News
By E.S. Browning
644 words
29 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A DAY AFTER sagging below 10000, the Dow Jones Industrial Average rebounded with its strongest gain in
almost a month, shaking off news of $50 oil and declining consumer confidence.
Traders said the jump was partly a technical rebound after repeated declines. In addition, some money
managers were putting money to work before the quarter ends.
Traders also pointed to Caterpillar's announcement during the afternoon that sales would exceed its
expectations this year and next. Its chief executive also said the heavy-equipment maker is enjoying an
"unprecedented recovery" across its business.
Caterpillar, a component of the Dow industrials that is benefiting from resurgent demand for basic industrial
materials, rose 3.3%.
The industrial average rose 88.86 points, or 0.89%, to 10077.40, its strongest advance since Sept. 2, almost
four weeks ago. Other indexes also posted gains.
Even so, many of the widely followed indexes still are in the red for 2004 and are heading toward a decline for
the third quarter as well. The Dow industrials are down 3.6% on the year and have fallen a similar amount,
3.4%, this quarter.
"We bounced off the 10000 level, helped a bit by the Caterpillar guidance," said David Hegarty, head of stock
trading at Commerzbank Securities, a New York brokerage arm of Germany's Commerzbank. "Overall it was
quite a resilient performance," with strong late-day volume, he noted.
Still, he said it essentially was a technical rebound after repeated declines, making it uncertain whether the
advance will continue.
One small plus for stocks was that crude-oil futures fell back after surpassing $50 a barrel during the day on
the New York Mercantile Exchange. Crude finished with a gain of 26 cents at $49.90, another 21-year high. It
was oil's ninth consecutive session of gains, during which time it has risen $6.32 a barrel, or 14.5%. On an
inflation-adjusted basis, however, oil traded at more than $80 a barrel in the early 1980s.
Bond prices, on the other hand, gave back some of their recent gains, and the decline in price pushed yields
upward, as prices and yields move inversely. The yield of the benchmark 10-year Treasury note edged back
above 4%, to 4.012%.
The Conference Board reported that consumer confidence, which had been expected to rise in September,
instead fell, for a second consecutive month.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.59%, or 6.54 points, to 1110.06, down a bare 0.17% this
year.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many technology stocks have been weak for most of the year,
recovered 0.54%, or 9.99 points, to 1869.87, a decline of 6.7% on the year.
The dollar weakened against the euro but rose against the yen to its highest level in almost eight weeks. The
price of gold rose $3.40 an ounce to $412.50, a one-month high, on the Comex division of the Nymex.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 0.95 point, or
0.5%, to 189.19. Excluding U.S. stocks, the index rose 0.39%, or 0.57 point, to 148.46.
In major U.S. market action:

ASIA/PACIFIC
Indonesian Police Hold Newmont Mining Staff
JAKARTA, Indonesia -- Indonesian police detained employees, including two U.S. citizens, of Newmont
Mining Corp. over allegations the Denver company polluted water near a gold mine on the island of
Sulawesi.
After months of controversy and conflicting reports about pollution near Newmont's Buyat Bay mine, the
detentions are likely to deal another blow to Indonesia's mining sector. Mining, a pillar of Indonesia's
economy, has seen a sharp drop in fresh investment during the past few years because of conflicting
regulations and disputes between local authorities and the central government over how to supervise the
industry.
On Wednesday, Indonesian police detained Bill Long, a U.S. citizen who works as the site manager for PT
Newmont Minahasa Raya, along with three Indonesian employees. Yesterday, the police questioned Richard
Ness, the president-director of the 80%-owned Newmont subsidiary, and said they intended to detain him as
well. Under Indonesian law, the police can hold the men for as many as 20 days without charges.
"These people are accused of not taking proper care of the mining process and causing harm to people living
in the area," said police spokesman Paiman, who like many Indonesians has only one name. He said they
were detained so the investigation could be conducted "smoothly."
Newmont says levels of heavy metals in Buyat Bay are no higher than in most maritime environments and
conform with international standards. J. Christopher Anderson, Newmont's Denver-based senior executive for
external relations and social responsibility, said, "we're convinced that all of the scientific evidence is on our
side."
The police investigation began with claims by local residents of an increase in health problems and a drop in
fish populations since production at the mine began in 1996.
Newmont, the world's largest gold miner in terms of production and revenue, also faces a $550 million
lawsuit filed in August by a legal-aid group in an Indonesian court on behalf of several villagers.
-- Timothy Mapes
Australia Hears Two Warnings on Home Prices
SYDNEY, Australia -- Sounding a note of caution during the election campaign, the International Monetary
Fund said Australian home prices are in danger of a sharp fall with severe consequences for the wider
economy.
Comments by the IMF were accompanied by similar warnings from Australia's central bank that suggested
any further rate increases will be introduced with a deft hand.
The outlook for house prices and interest rates could again be a crucial factor in the Oct. 9 vote, with the
outcome likely to be decided in so-called mortgage-belt seats that have helped elect Prime Minister John
Howard's conservative government since 1996.
In its latest World Economic Outlook report, the IMF said the run-up in Australian house prices has been
among the fastest in the world and defies explanation on the basis of fundamentals. "In cases where house
prices may have exceeded fundamentals, which may include Australia, Ireland, Spain and the U.K. . . . there

--- Page 69 ---
Page 69 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
is a danger that higher interest rates could trigger a much larger downward adjustment in house prices, with
considerably more severe consequences for real activity," the IMF said.
Australian real-estate prices have more than doubled since the mid-1990s, with mortgage loans swelling
household debt to about 150% of household income.
Economic management, national security and Australia's involvement in the Iraq war have been touchstone
issues in the six-week campaign by Mr. Howard's coalition of urban and rural lawmakers and the center-left
Labor Party led by Mark Latham.

EUROPE
Euro's Expansion Sullied by Bad Greek Data
BRUSSELS -- The revelation that Greece has been submitting faulty budget numbers dealt a blow to the
reliability of some European Union statistics and cast a cloud of uncertainty over the adoption of the euro by
newly admitted EU members.
The EU statistics agency Eurostat said since 2000 Greece has overestimated tax revenue, booked more EU
aid money than it received and underestimated spending on defense and interest payments. As a result,
Greece also underreported closely watched budget-deficit figures, putting the gap at 1.7% of gross domestic
product in 2003, when the actual deficit was 4.6%. EU rules require that deficits not exceed 3% of GDP, a
condition for adopting the euro.
The situation shows how little power the EU has to police members' spending. While the current
embarrassment could force some tightening, analysts said governments are likely to continue cutting corners,
undermining the common currency and forcing governments to pay higher interest rates to service their
debts.
EU officials pledged tighter monitoring. Eurostat director Michel Vanden Abeele plans to seek more staff and
more power to audit accounts.
Now, Eurostat can only consult with finance ministries. Only "a few officials" visited Athens "for two or three
days" in the past few years to check accounts, a Eurostat spokesman said. The statistics agency received the
correct numbers earlier this month only because a new Greek government is eager to disclose the failings of
previous administrations.
The controversy is likely to increase scrutiny of new EU entrants seeking to reduce budget deficits so they are
eligible to adopt the euro. Six of the 10 new members, largely from Eastern and Central Europe, had deficits
exceeding the 3% limit in 2003, including the region's three biggest economies: the Czech Republic, at
12.9%, Hungary, at 6.2%, and Poland, at 3.9%.
-- Dow Jones Newswires
Spain's Budget Aims to Spend With Discipline
MADRID -- Spain's six-month-old Socialist government is expected to approve today a draft budget for 2005
that aims to raise social spending while maintaining the fiscal discipline that was the trademark of the
previous center-right administration.
But pushing the draft budget through Parliament promises to be Spanish Finance Minister Pedro Solbes's
biggest challenge this year as he seeks to juggle the conflicting interests of financial markets, regional parties
and leftist allies in Parliament.
Under Socialist rule in the early 1990s, Spain suffered a period of huge budget deficits, rampant inflation and
sluggish economic growth. The country bounced back and experienced eight years of buoyant growth during
the two terms of Jose Maria Aznar, the former leader of the center-right Popular Party. Under Mr. Aznar,
Spain handily outpaced average European growth rates and became one of the first countries in the
European Union to present a balanced budget in 2003.
Mr. Solbes needs to prove to financial markets that his Socialists won't wreck Mr. Aznar's legacy by again
running big budget deficits. Spain's economy, the fourth-largest in the euro zone, is one of the EU's leading
growth engines. But the main driver of its growth, a construction and housing boom, may be on the verge of
slowing down, some economists say.

--- Page 70 ---
Page 70 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
One concern is that Mr. Solbes might not win full support for his budget unless he caves in to the spending
demands of the government's political partners. Efforts to control overall state spending also are hindered by
the growing economic influence of Spain's 17 regions, which currently account for 46.3% of total state
expenditures, compared with 33.6% in 1996.
The 2005 draft budget will feature a 25% jump in spending on research and development -- an increase
aimed at boosting Spain's low labor productivity and diversifying its economy -- and spending increases of
7.3% for education, 33% for housing and 6.5% for pensions.
It is unclear which items will be cut to balance the books.
-- Carlta Vitzthum

What's an Analyst to Do?
What's an Analyst to Do?
By Brian S. Wesbury
1,170 words
24 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Many economists believe that markets contain enough relevant information to make forecasts or manage
economic policy. But the markets' message is not always easy to divine. In recent weeks, for example, stocks
and bonds have been marching to different drummers.
Since Aug. 12, the Dow is up 2% and the Nasdaq is up 8%, signaling a positive view of the economy. But the
10-year Treasury yield, as can be seen in the chart nearby, has plummeted from 4.9% in May, to below 4%
this week, suggesting that growth may be weak. So what's an analyst to do? How can these two markets be
reconciled?
Some economists suggest that these market moves are logical. They argue that the Fed has contained
inflationary pressures by hiking interest rates three times, without harming the economy. Voila! Low bond
yields, a narrowing yield spread and rising stock prices.
But this theory ignores the continued increase in commodity prices (including gold) and the drop in the value
of the dollar that has occurred in the past month. If we assume that inflation has stabilized at say 2%, then
commodity prices should no longer be rising at double-digit annualized rates. But they are. Moreover, at
1.75%, the federal-funds rate is still below inflation. Both history and economic theory suggest that this is too
low.
If the U.S. economy really needs negative real interest rates to continue growing, then there must be some
serious underlying problem. And if that were the case, it is hard to imagine that stock investors would be
bullish.
Others say that rising oil prices, rather than causing inflation, are creating a drag on the economy. The
Economist magazine puts it this way: "The high oil price is a tax . . . [that] can be passed on to consumers in
higher prices, or lower growth, or both. The [bond] market seems to have decided that it is being passed on
only in the form of lower growth."
There are two problems with this theory. First, it ignores rising stock prices. Secondly, rising oil prices would
affect the growth path of inflation-adjusted, or real GDP, not nominal GDP -- which includes both real growth
and inflation. Milton Friedman taught us that monetary policy impacts the growth rate of nominal GDP.
Robert Mundell, Arthur Laffer, the late Bob Bartley (longtime editor of this newspaper) and others taught us
that it is fiscal policy that determines the split between real growth and inflation.
As a result, if rising oil prices really act like a tax increase, real growth should slow, while inflation rises.
Weaker real growth would pull interest rates down as the return to capital fell, but the increase in inflation
would push them up to compensate for lost purchasing power. Nominal GDP, which is determined by Fed
policy, would be unaffected. The net impact on bonds should be neutral.
Another argument for why bond yields are falling has to do with foreign purchases. According to this view,
dollars held by China, Japan and other Asian nations, as well as recycled petro-dollars, are finding their way
back into the U.S. bond market.
Foreign official assets, held at the Federal Reserve in the form of U.S. Treasury securities, increased by $85
billion in 2002, $167 billion in 2003, and $244 billion in the 12 months ending in June 2004. Today, they stand
at nearly $1 trillion, more than 20% of all outstanding Treasury bonds, a record high. The last time this
occurred was in the late 1970s. Foreign central bank holdings of U.S. Treasury securities held by the Fed
rose to 19.3% of outstanding federal debt in 1979 from 5.7% in 1970. At that time, bond yields were also slow
to adjust to higher inflation rates. As a result, this theory has some appeal.
Nonetheless, it still does not explain the volatile moves in Treasury bond yields of the past two years. The
data show no evidence that reduced foreign buying caused the sharp increase in bond yields that the U.S.

--- Page 74 ---
Page 74 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
experienced in June and July 2003, or March and April 2004. Nor does it show that accelerated buying
caused yields to fall.
Theoretically, bond yields should move with changes in nominal GDP. During the 1990s, the 10-year
Treasury yield was on average 1.25% above the rate of growth in nominal GDP. Declining inflation over this
period slowly brought nominal GDP growth down, and as this occurred, long-term U.S. bond yields also fell,
even though real GDP growth and real interest rates stayed high.
But lately this has changed. As nominal GDP growth accelerated from 3.2% in 2001 to 7.0% (4.7% real
growth, 2.3% inflation) during the year ended in the second quarter, 10-year Treasury bond yields fell from
5.0% to 4.0%. Bond yields are now significantly below nominal GDP growth for the first time since the late
1970s.
To theorize that foreign buying is powerful enough to change the relationship between long-term bond yields
and nominal GDP growth is a stretch. The most powerful force in determining bond yields is the Fed. On
average, between 1975 and 1979, the federal-funds rate was held 1% below CPI inflation -- one of the most
accommodative monetary policy postures in history.
When the Fed holds rates this low, it drags the entire yield curve down. Investors become involved in the
"carry trade," by borrowing at short-term rates and lending at long-term rates in order to profit from the steep
yield curve. In addition, holders of large mortgage portfolios are forced to hedge. The result can be artificially
low interest rates, such as the 3.1%, 10-year yield in June 2003.
But this was just an artifact of Fed policy, not a forecast of slower growth. The economy had already begun to
accelerate upon the passage of the tax cut in May and real GDP expanded at a 7.4% rate in the third quarter.
Bond yields hit another low in March of this year, and then spiked, before declining again, a pattern not
followed by the economy.
Many of the theories used to explain the recent decline in long-term bond yields break down under close
scrutiny. The best explanation continues to be excessively accommodative Fed policy.
With nominal GDP growth accelerating, gold prices above $410 an ounce, the real federal-funds rate below
zero, and commodity prices rising sharply, it is clear that Fed policy remains very loose. It is this policy
stance, not some imminent economic collapse that is holding down long-rates. As we saw in 2003 and again
earlier this year, no matter how many different theories are bandied about, once bond yields shake loose of
the Fed, they rise sharply. There is no reason to suspect that this time will be any different.
---
Mr. Wesbury is chief economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens &Thompson, Inc., in Chicago.
