Bookshelf: The Biggest Rags to Riches Story Ever
Bookshelf:
LEISURE &ARTS
Bookshelf: The Biggest Rags to Riches Story Ever
By Brink Lindsey
936 words
23 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D10
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AN EMPIRE OF WEALTH
By John Steele Gordon
(HarperCollins Publishers, 460 pages, $26.95)
Since the earliest days of European discovery, America has inspired dreams of fabulous wealth and gain. "It
is a veritable Cockaigne," Columbus wrote during his third voyage, likening the New World to the medieval
legend of gluttonous plenty. The conquistadores followed close on his heels in a wild scramble for gold,
treasure and even eternal youth. The British settlement of North America, meanwhile, got its start as a
profit-seeking commercial venture.
In the U.S., the dreams of opulence came true. Americans today enjoy a level of material abundance that
sets the standard for the world's aspirations -- and soars beyond the wildest imaginings of centuries past.
How this came about is the subject of John Steele Gordon's superb "An Empire of Wealth." With clarity and
good sense, Mr. Gordon tells the story of America's transformation from uncharted wilderness to economic
colossus. Along the way, he offers well-wrought set pieces on the people, technologies and industries that
propelled the race to riches.
The best are those in which Mr. Gordon dusts off episodes now only dimly remembered. He recaptures, for
example, the wild audacity of building the Erie Canal, whose projected cost was equal to three-quarters of the
entire federal budget at the time. "It is a splendid project, and may be executed a century hence," Thomas
Jefferson told the canal's tireless champion, DeWitt Clinton. "It is little short of madness to think of it at this
day."
And then there is Samuel Slater, the industrial pirate honored by Andrew Jackson as the "Father of American
Manufactures." An apprentice at one of England's new textile mills, Slater aimed to open a mill of his own in
the U.S. In 1789, he circumvented the British ban on exporting textile machines by committing every detail of
their design to memory and sneaking out of the country disguised as a farm laborer.
Another highlight is the story of Frederic Tudor, the forgotten pioneer of a forgotten industry -- the ice trade.
Tudor concocted the idea, mocked by his contemporaries, of shipping New England ice to warmer climes; he
then hit upon sawdust, previously a bothersome waste product of the lumber industry, as the ideal insulating
material. By 1847, ships out of Boston were transporting 23,000 tons of ice a year to foreign ports and almost
52,000 tons to the Southern U.S.
Mr. Gordon enlivens his narrative with an assortment of little-known facts, figures and asides. As late as
1790, he observes, slaves made up 5.5% of New York's population. The New York Stock Exchange's
practice of giving prices in eighths of a dollar, discontinued only in 1999, was a vestige of the old practice of
cutting up Spanish dollars into "pieces of eight." On the initial run of the first U.S. commercial train,
passengers brought writing materials to refute the popular theory that the human brain could not function at
such speeds. Not that technological marvels were always welcome. In 1844, 63-year-old Philip Hone wrote:
"Railroads, steamers, packets, race against time and beat it hollow . . . .Oh, for the good old days of heavy
post coaches and speed at the rate of six miles an hour!" According to Mr. Gordon, Hone's lament is the first
recorded reference to those mythical and ever-advancing "good old days."
Mr. Gordon pays particular attention to the development of the nation's financial system. His discussions of
the Erie Railroad takeover battles and of the Credit Mobilier scandal, when railroad executives tried to bribe
members of Congress, remind us that Enron-style shenanigans are nothing new -- and that today's rogues
are pikers by historical standards. Mr. Gordon devotes special attention to wartime finance. Following

--- Page 76 ---
Page 76 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Cicero's maxim that "the sinews of war are infinite money," he notes that, during the Civil War, the Union was
able to raise two-thirds of its revenues by borrowing and an additional 21% by taxing. The Confederacy, by
contrast, raised only a total of 46% of its revenues by such means, resorting to the printing press for the rest
and precipitating a ruinous inflation.
Somewhat simplistically, Mr. Gordon blames the 19th century's boom-and-bust bipolarism on the absence of
a central bank. Yet he acknowledges the Fed's catastrophically restrictive response to the 1929 crash as
well as its loose-money culpability in the inflation of the 1960s and '70s. Why, then, are we to assume that
central banking in the prior century would have proved a panacea?
Mr. Gordon's grasp of basic economic principles is admirably solid. He comes down strongly in favor of free
trade, sound money and freely moving prices. He is skeptical of government restrictions on competition,
however well-intentioned.
What is missing from his account, or at least muffled, is the social and political tumult caused by America's
economic dynamism. The rise and fall of industries and regions, the convulsions that attended
industrialization and mass immigration, the revolution in values catalyzed by widespread affluence, the
never-ending struggle over dividing the pie -- all have rocked the empire of wealth. Mr. Gordon is not blind to
such turbulence, but he plays it down. In his chronicle, upward and onward is the dominant theme. The full
story is rather more interesting.
---
Mr. Lindsey is vice president for research at the Cato Institute and author of "Against the Dead Hand: The
Uncertain Struggle for Global Capitalism."

Page 77 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
An African First-World Country
An African First-World Country
By F. W. de Klerk
924 words
23 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Some observers believe that the critical period for emergent democracies is not their first decade, but the 10
years that follow. So what are the prospects for South Africa during its second democratic decade?
The first 10 years were marked by some remarkable successes. Apart from consolidating democracy, the
ANC government implemented economic policies that have reduced inflation and interest rates to the
lowest figures in more than two decades. South Africa has recorded 10 years of uninterrupted economic
growth, averaging about 3% per annum. This is not the 5% to 7% that it needs to address its social problems,
but it is by no means a bad performance in a period of global volatility and uncertainty. South Africa has also
proved that it can compete effectively in global markets. It has increased its manufactured exports to 58%
from 36% of the total, and now earns more from the export of BMWs, Mercedes and Volkswagens than from
gold. Tourism is booming, and the country is an attractive destinations for visitors from all over the world.
Despite all this there are a number of serious concerns for the next decade. The first is AIDS, which has
reduced life expectancy from 63 years in 1990 to only 47 years now. Over the next 10 years, more than five
million South Africans will die of AIDS and leave behind them some two million orphans.
Apart from -- and perhaps linked to -- AIDS is poverty and inequality. Ironically, under the traditionally socialist
ANC, South Africa has become an even less equal society than it was 10 years ago. Since 1995 the GINI
coefficient (by which zero is absolute equality and 1.0 is absolute inequality) has risen to 0.63 from 0.6. There
is an increasing cleavage between its emerging multiracial middle class and unionized labor elite on the one
hand and a growing black underclass on the other. Although the government has built more than a million
new homes, and although it has increased welfare allowances to children and pensioners, almost half the
population -- mostly black -- lives below the poverty line.
Unemployment has increased among black South Africans to 46.6% in 2002 from 36.2% in 1995. Its roots
are complex, and include retrenchments necessitated by competition in globalized markets; the lack of skilled
workers and managers; and sluggish domestic and foreign investment. Yet a central cause is undoubtedly
the government's rigid labor laws, that have driven the cost of labor well above that of South Africa's
competitors in other emerging economies.
The other half of the population has prospered. Strong and vocal trade unions have protected the interests of
their members, only 2% of whom live below the poverty line. The middle class has grown rapidly as blacks
have moved into middle and senior posts in government and in the private sector. The middle class now
comprises 7.5 million (about 17% of the population). This includes 3.5 million blacks (10% of the black
population); 3.25 million whites (60% of the white population); half a million coloreds, or people of mixed
origin (15% of their population); and 250,000 Indians (25% of the Indian population).
The government is acutely aware of the threat posed by poverty -- and particularly by the continuing highly
visible inequality. Despite impressive black advances spurred by affirmative action, whites still hold most of
the best jobs in the private sector and own most of the assets, including 80% of farmland and 65% of equity
on the Johannesburg Securities Exchange. Blacks own less than 5% and the rest is owned by foreign
interests, including former South African companies that have listed overseas since 1994.
South Africa's next decade is therefore destined to be dominated by the ANC government's efforts to address
the continuing inequality, primarily through its black economic empowerment programs. It has set ambitious
targets for black ownership of land and equity, and for more equitable black representation at all levels of
management. The success of this process will, in turn, be determined by the following factors:
First, South Africa needs real transformation that will substantially address the needs of the poorest -- rather
than further enrich the emerging black elite and middle class. The government must eliminate all barriers to
job creation by relaxing its own rigid labor approach.

--- Page 78 ---
Page 78 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Second, transformation must take place within the basic rules governing the globalized economy. The
government is fully aware of the need to maintain property rights, economic freedoms and basic standards of
administration and management.
Third, transformation must be implemented in a way that does not undermine national unity or lead to
intercommunity friction. The constitutional transformation worked well precisely because it was the result of
negotiations and compromises between parties that represented the great majority of blacks and whites.
However, whites have not been sufficiently consulted with regard to the principles underlying black economic
empowerment and feel increasingly alienated.
South Africans need to talk to one another about the challenges of the second decade, just as they did when
they reached agreement on their new democracy. The stakes are very high. The country's ability to reach a
national consensus on transformation will be a key factor in achieving success -- and in determining whether
South Africa will succeed in realizing President Thabo Mbeki's dream of becoming the first African country to
join the ranks of the First World.
---
Mr. de Klerk is a former president of South Africa and a Nobel Peace laureate in 1993.

Stocks, Bonds Rally On Fed's Assessment Of Nation's Economy
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks, Bonds Rally On Fed's Assessment Of Nation's Economy
By E.S. Browning
683 words
22 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS AND BONDS hit new milestones after the Federal Reserve raised its target short-term rate as
expected and offered an upbeat assessment of the economy.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index and the Nasdaq Composite Index finished at their highest levels in
more than two months. The bond rally also extended, sending the yield of the 10-year Treasury note, which
moves opposite to price, down to 4.047% -- its lowest finish since April 1.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 40.04 points, or 0.39%, to 10244.93.
Crude-oil futures jumped 75 cents to a one-month high of $47.10 a barrel on the New York Mercantile
Exchange, but investors shrugged off that news and focused on the Fed.
"The markets put an enormous amount of faith and credibility in the Federal Reserve, and when the Fed says
things are progressing," said Andy Brooks, head of stock trading at Baltimore mutual-fund firm T. Rowe Price,
"that is very reassuring." The Fed comment was a special relief, since a spate of earnings warnings from
prominent companies such as Intel and Colgate-Palmolive have put stock investors through "a rough couple
of weeks," Mr. Brooks said.
The Dow industrial average was held back by weakness in component stocks such as Citigroup, Altria and
3M. The Dow still hasn't returned to the two-month high of 10342.79 hit on Sept. 7. But the sister-index Dow
Jones Transportation Average hit another five-year high of 3271.22, up 0.89%, or 28.89 points. And the Dow
Jones Utility Average hit a two-year high of 295.85, up 0.39%, or 1.15 points.
In the statement accompanying its decision, the Fed offered an upbeat picture, saying "growth appears to
have regained some traction," and that "inflation expectations have eased." It said the pace of further rate
increases would continue to be "measured."
The statement reinforced hopes that the economy will sustain moderate growth without serious inflation or
sharp interest-rate increases through the end of this year.
Still, the Fed rate increase was the third since the end of June, and historically, stocks often have suffered
after the Fed raised rates for a third time. But even though the Fed's short-term target rate is up 75% from its
46-year low of 1%, it remains unusually low and well below consumer inflation.
A variety of banks including KeyCorp and Wells Fargo quickly raised their prime lending rates, which govern
a variety of consumer and business loans, to 4.75% from 4.5%.
The Fed doesn't hold another interest-rate policy meeting until Nov. 10, just after the presidential election.
Investors widely expect the Fed to raise its target rate another quarter-point, to 2%, either then or at the
following meeting, in December. They expect that, at some point, the central bank will continue raising rates
next year. Housing starts were stronger than expected in August.
The S&P 500 rose 0.63%, or 7.10 points, to 1129.30, now up 1.6% this year. The 1130 level has been one at
which sellers emerged in the past, and traders will be closely watching whether the index now can break
through that level.
The Nasdaq Composite Index advanced 0.69%, or 13.11 points, to 1921.18. Technology stocks have been
recovering from a summer swoon, but the tech-heavy index still is down 4% this year.
Gold rose $3 to $408.30 an ounce, a three-week high. The dollar hit a one-month low against the euro.
In major U.S. market action:

against the dollar to $1.2338 from $1.2168.

Kofi's Law
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Kofi's Law
606 words
20 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A20
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Last we checked, U.N. chief Kofi Annan was promising to help the U.S. rebuild Iraq. But pressed by a BBC
interviewer last week, the Secretary-General stated flat out that the liberation of Iraq was "illegal" and a
violation of the U.N. Charter. He had already opined that "there should have been a second resolution"
authorizing the invasion, and that "I hope we do not see another Iraq-type operation for a long time."
These thoughts could certainly stand a little parsing. Mr. Annan seems to be saying that the only way force
can be used legitimately in the modern world is with the unanimous permission of the U.N. Security Council.
So perhaps we should remind him of some recent history.
For example, there was that splendidly legitimate U.N. operation in Bosnia, where its blue-helmeted
peacekeepers watched with indifference as Serbian soldiers rounded up for slaughter thousands of Muslim
men in the so-called U.N. "safe haven" of Srebrenica. Or Rwanda in 1994, where Mr. Annan -- then head of
the U.N. peacekeeping office -- shrugged off panicked warning calls from the U.N. commander on the ground,
thereby allowing the slaughter of 800,000.
And if liberating Iraq was wrong, Mr. Annan must also believe it was wrong for NATO to have intervened in
Kosovo, where Russia once again prevented Security Council unanimity. How about the recent French
intervention in the Ivory Coast, which the Security Council got around to blessing only after it was a fait
accompli? And notwithstanding the latest U.N. promises, what if Gallic and Chinese oil interests block
international action in Sudan, allowing the continued attacks on Darfurians? It would appear, on this
evidence, that Security Council unanimity isn't exactly the gold standard of legitimacy, much less of morality.
And what's this business about a "second" Iraq resolution? U.N. Resolution 1441 was the 17th resolution
demanding that Saddam verifiably disarm, behave with some modicum of respect for the rights of his own
citizens, and otherwise comply with conditions of the ceasefire following the end of the 1991 Gulf War. From
firing at American planes patrolling the no-fly zones, to widespread sanctions busting, to a banned long-range
missile program, the Iraqi dictator was in undeniable breach in March 2003 of the terms under which his
regime was spared back in 1991. In other words, there was never any legal need for even Resolution 1441.
This is the same Kofi Annan, by the way, who said after saving Saddam from a U.S. armada in 1998 that
"You can do a lot with diplomacy, but with diplomacy backed up by force you can get a lot more done." But in
large part thanks to such diplomatic interventions by Mr. Annan on Saddam's behalf, by 2003 the dictator
apparently believed that this "force" was always going to be an illusion. He thought he'd slip the noose one
more time.
The Secretary-General's latest posturing is far from harmless. The U.N. has been given the lead role in
organizing the elections in Iraq scheduled for January. But Mr. Annan's "illegal" comments, which have been
replayed across the Arab world, have given an added feeling of legitimacy to every jihadist hoping to disrupt
the vote.
His comments also suggest that Mr. Annan belongs in the same category as France and Russia in never
intending the "serious consequences" threatened by Resolution 1441. We wonder: Could the corrupt Oil for
Food program and all the revenues it generated for the U.N. have anything to do with it?

Hurricane Ivan Lashes Southeast --- Twenty Deaths Cited in U.S.; Insured Losses Could Reach $7 Billion in the Region
Hurricane Ivan Lashes Southeast --- Twenty Deaths Cited in U.S.; Insured Losses Could Reach $7
Billion in the Region
By Nicole Harris in Atlanta, Dan Morse in Milton, Fla., and Scott Kilman in Chicago
1,184 words
17 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A6
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Hurricane Ivan cut a destructive swath across four Southeastern states, spawning deadly tornadoes, toppling
beach houses into the Gulf of Mexico and leaving hundreds of thousands of people without power.
At least 20 deaths were blamed on Ivan, on top of the 68 people killed during Ivan's destructive trek through
the Caribbean. The storm could rank as the second-costliest U.S. hurricane, behind Hurricane Andrew, which
in 1992 killed 26 people in Florida and caused insured losses of $20.3 billion, adjusted for inflation.
Risk Management Solutions, a Newark, Calif., company that assists insurers in reducing exposure to large
catastrophes, predicted insured losses of $2 billion to $7 billion. Hurricane Charley inflicted about $6.8 billion
in insured losses after roaring through Florida last month, and Frances caused an estimated $2 billion to $4
billion after it hit Sept. 5.
After barreling ashore before dawn yesterday near the resort town of Gulf Shores, Ala., with winds of about
130 miles an hour, a weakening Ivan then spread lashing winds and rain as it cut north-northeast across
Alabama. At its worst, the storm spread hurricane-strength winds of at least 75 mph over an area roughly the
size of Maine. By late yesterday, Ivan was a tropical storm with maximum sustained winds of 60 mph, but
some areas in its projected path still were expected to get more than 15 inches of rain.
More than 443,000 power customers in 12 Florida counties lost power as a result of the storm, according to
state officials, and Southern Co.'s Alabama Power unit said people in areas hit hardest by Ivan will have no
power for at least a week. The outages came as Florida utility workers still are scrambling to restore service
to some customers who lost power when Frances barreled across the state.
Meanwhile, El Paso Corp. said a 26-inch natural-gas pipeline in the Gulf of Mexico is leaking and will need to
be repaired. The leaking pipeline moves a relatively small amount of natural gas, so market watchers don't
expect much impact on pricing. Three floating drilling rigs that broke loose from their moorings during the
storm were found yesterday. Transocean Inc.'s football-field-sized Deepwater Nautilus had drifted 70 miles
northeast of its original location.
Hurricane Jeanne, the other potentially menacing storm churning in the Atlantic Ocean, was downgraded to a
tropical storm yesterday afternoon as it passed over the Dominican Republic but could regain hurricane
strength before approaching Georgia and South Carolina early next week, according to the National Weather
Service.
Ivan battered Alabama cotton and peanut farms, and Charley and Frances bruised Florida's citrus industry,
but the U.S. is sitting on such big supplies of these commodities that economists don't expect prices to rise
significantly.
Michael Swanson, an economist at banking titan Wells Fargo &Co., Minneapolis, said yesterday the
hurricanes over the past month haven't done enough damage for him to change his forecast for food-price
inflation to climb 3.35% the remainder of this year, and to climb a further 3.15% for 2005.
Cotton prices plunged 5% yesterday because Ivan didn't cause nearly as much damage as traders had
thought possible. As the massive storm approached land Wednesday night from the Gulf of Mexico, it veered
east and missed the Mississippi Delta's cotton-growing region.
Ivan has probably destroyed at least some of the cotton crop in Alabama and western Georgia. The crop is
highly vulnerable at this time of year because the plant is nearing the harvest stage, when the white fluffy
cotton boll is exposed to elements.

--- Page 93 ---
Page 93 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But other cotton-growing states, such as Texas, are expecting such big yields that U.S. cotton supplies will
soar this year. Last week, the U.S. Agriculture Department predicted that U.S. farmers will harvest a record
20.9 million bales of cotton.
Hospitals and their patients have been especially vulnerable to Ivan and its predecessors. Many hospitals
have been forced to cancel elective surgeries, and in some instances patients have been moved. Admissions
are also down since millions of residents have evacuated parts of Florida and the Southeast.
HCA Inc.'s Gulf Coast Hospital in Lee County, Fla., has been closed since Hurricane Charley ripped through
Southwest Florida last month. The roof was ripped off the emergency room. More than 40 patients and 16
babies were transferred to the Southwest Regional Medical Center.
Health Management Associates Inc., of Naples, Fla. said on Monday that the hurricanes could affect earnings
in the third quarter. HMA's 14 hospitals in Florida account for 25% of its operations. Joel Ray, an analyst at
Wachovia Securities in Richmond, Va., said HMA, HCA and Tenet Healthcare Corp. have the most hospitals
at risk. He estimated that evacuations prompted by the storms could reduce patient volumes for several
weeks by 10% to 20%. That could translate into a drop in revenue for the quarter of 1% to 2.5%, Mr. Ray
said.
In Pensacola, Fla., hospital workers and patients at Baptist Healthcare were happy to have survived
Wednesday night. Ivan shattered many windows and water poured in. Sparks flew outside as power lines
came down. Then the power went out. A backup generator provided lighting, but there was no drinking water.
Around 2 a.m. yesterday morning, a glass wall along the cardiac-care unit blew in; other windows shattered.
Fortunately, many patients had already been moved into the hallway to get away from windows. In the midst
of the chaos, a baby was born in the hospital.
At HMA-owned Santa Rosa Medical Center, in Milton, Fla., Pete Gandy, the chief executive officer, made the
decision Tuesday not to evacuate the 129-bed hospital.
He ordered that all hospital workers come in early, and suggested that they bring family members with them
and plan to stay through the storm. About 50 children of staff spent the night Wednesday night, playing
games and sleeping in the hallways on the floor.
Inside the building, patients were moved away from glass windows, and the highest floor of the four-story
structure was emptied. The hospital also put out the word to local physicians that only seriously ill or injured
patients would be admitted until the storm passed.
By 9 p.m. Wednesday, the hospital had 42 patients as Ivan rolled ashore with winds at 125 mph. The next
morning the poststorm victims began arriving. They lined up 10-deep with lacerations, hypertension triggered
by fears during the night and other minor injuries.
Throughout the storm, even as reports were coming in of tremendous damage in nearby areas, the hospital's
staff stayed on.
"The houses were flattened and they kept working," Mr. Gandy said. "the test of a hospital is proven during a
crisis like this."
---
Daniel Machalaba, Russell Gold and Paul Davies contributed to this article.

Shares of Home Builders Nail Down an Advance
Small-Stock Focus
Shares of Home Builders Nail Down an Advance
By Cynthia Schreiber Dow Jones Newswires
707 words
17 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Home builders helped small-capitalization stocks outperform the overall market, but trucking
shares were stragglers.
Meritage jumped $5.46, or 7.6%, to $77.18 on the New York Stock Exchange. The single-family home builder
issued a strong profit outlook for 2005 and said third-quarter demand for its homes remains "very good."
Champion Enterprises gained 40 cents, or 3.9%, to 10.75 and Beazer Homes USA advanced 2.11, or 2%, to
105.73, both on the Big Board.
Countering those gains, Swift Transportation fell 1.99, or 11%, to 16.28. The trucking firm warned that its
third-quarter earnings will miss expectations, partly due to high fuel costs. Also in the trucking group, Werner
Enterprises slipped 45 cents, or 2.4%, to 18.20 and Arkansas Best shed 40 cents, or 1.1%, to 35.47.
Traders said a report showing mild inflation in August also helped to lift stocks.
"Valuations are pretty good and the economy is doing OK," noted Gordon Johnson, portfolio manager of
Armada Small-Cap Core Fund. "You can have a nice little November-December pop."
The Russell 2000 Index jumped 6.02, or 1.1%, to 574.54, its highest level in 11 weeks. The S&P SmallCap
600 Index climbed 2.82, or 0.98%, to 290.82, also an 11-week high. The Nasdaq Composite Index of
large-cap and small-cap stocks advanced 7.56, or 0.4%, to 1904.08.
The gains came in light trading as some investors took time off to celebrate Rosh Hashana. Trading volume
on the Nasdaq was 1.32 billion shares. Advancers outnumbered decliners, 1,954 to 1,076.
Some small stocks managed to resist declines in the medical-device group after stock downgrades of large
caps Mentor and Guidant. ArthroCare leapt 1.67, or 6.2%, to 28.74 and Viasys Healthcare rose 14 cents, or
0.9%, to 16.50 on the NYSE.
Specialty retailers advanced after large cap Best Buy boosted its quarterly dividend. Sports Authority gained
86 cents, or 3.8%, to 23.36 and Linens 'N Things rose 42 cents, or 1.7%, to 25.15, both on the Big Board.
Guitar Center gained 84 cents, or 1.9%, to 45.49.
Gold futures settled slightly lower, as did some stocks related to the precious metal. Coeur D'Alene Mines
slipped 6 cents, or 1.7%, to 3.57 on the NYSE and Bema Gold lost 4 cents, or 1.6%, to 2.39 on the American
Stock Exchange.
Netopia plummeted 89 cents, or 31%, to 1.95. The Emeryville, Calif., developer of broadband and wireless
products and services said an ongoing audit revealed revenue-recognition issues that would require a
restatement of results between June 2002 and March 2004. The company also said its auditor, KPMG,
resigned.
Martha Stewart Living Omnimedia rose 77 cents, or 6.8%, to 12.03 on the Big Board, a session after founder
Martha Stewart said she wants to begin serving her prison sentence as soon as possible. The move was
designed to end the uncertainty surrounding the media and home-products company.
Lawson Software dropped 77 cents, or 13%, to 5.36. The provider of business-application software said it
expects a net loss for its fiscal first quarter, compared with a net profit a year earlier.
Shares of EFJ climbed 67 cents, or 11%, to 6.78 after the wireless-telecommunications company boosted its
2004 outlook, citing "increased sales opportunities" for homeland-security communication products.

--- Page 95 ---
Page 95 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
American Science &Engineering advanced 3.25, or 13%, to 28.50 on the Amex. The company said it
received a $23.2 million order from the U.S. government for its X-ray screening systems.
Trident Microsystems sank 2.71, or 20%, to 10.63. C.E. Unterberg, Towbin downgraded its investment rating
on the stock to "market perform" from "buy," citing concerns about the company's capital structure.
NitroMed rose 2.60, or 13%, to 22.29. The Lexington, Mass., company said late-stage data on its heart-failure
drug will be presented at the American Heart Association's annual meeting in November.

Commodities Enter Investment Mainstream
Going Global
Commodities Enter Investment Mainstream
By Michael R. Sesit
989 words
9 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Paris -- HEDGE FUNDS AND other speculators have taken some criticism for the soaring price of oil and
other commodities.
But amid the din of the blame game, a broader and more-significant trend in the global investment community
has largely been overlooked: Mainstream investors such as pension funds, insurance companies and
university endowments -- even Harvard's -- are pumping more money into commodities.
These long-term institutional investors hold, on average, investments in energy, industrial and precious
metals, livestock, agriculture and other commodities that are twice those of hedge funds, estimates Jeffrey
Currie, head of commodities research at a London office of Wall Street securities firm Goldman Sachs Group
Inc.
Often, those investments aren't directly in physical commodities themselves but in commodity-related and
energy-related companies and in indexes linked to the price of commodities.
Market analysts conservatively figure that roughly 150 institutions have so-called passive investments pegged
to the Goldman Sachs Commodity Index, up from fewer than 50 in 2000. Goldman estimates that more than
$25 billion is tied to the index, compared with $8 billion four years ago. American International Group Inc.
estimates that an additional $8 billion to $10 billion in investments is pegged to the Dow Jones-AIG
Commodity Index, up from about $200 million a few years ago. (Dow Jones &Co. is also publisher of The
Wall Street Journal.)
"Commodities have begun to emerge as a discrete asset class in their own right," says Stefan Weiser,
London-based European head of investor sales for the Goldman index.
Of course, some commodities and metals, especially gold, have long had a dedicated following. But such
investors typically have been few in number and have long been on the fringes of the global investment
community. Until recently.
Part of commodities' recent appeal has to do with a bull market fueled by strong demand, especially from
economies such as China's, and an underinvestment in production in the past 10 to 15 years. From its lows in
mid-February 1999, the Goldman index is up 183% through Tuesday. "Commodities will be a bull market into
early the next decade," predicts Felix Zulauf, principal partner of Zulauf Asset Management. In July 2003, the
Zug, Switzerland, firm launched the Gondwana natural-resource fund. With more than $605 million in assets,
the fund is now closed to new investment.
Commodities' status as an acceptable investment for mainstream institutions could get a further boost from a
recently published paper: "Facts and Fantasies about Commodity Futures" by Gary Gorton of the University
of Pennsylvania's Wharton School and K. Geert Rouwenhorst of the Yale School of Management at Yale
University. The two academics conclude that during the past 45 years, commodity futures have had roughly
the same return as stocks with less risk, have way outperformed bonds and are a better hedge against
inflation than either stocks or bonds.
"As this sinks in and as commodities do well, trustees, directors, portfolio managers, fiduciaries and others
will no longer be able to dismiss commodities out of hand," says James Rogers, an independent investor and
co-founder with financier George Soros of Quantum Fund. The Gorton-Rouwenhorst paper, predicts Mr.
Rogers, "is going to change completely the investing world over the next decade."
The rush into commodities, in fact, already is well under way.
Take the Ontario Teachers' Pension Plan. More than 6% of its 79 billion Canadian dollars, or US$61 billion, in
assets is invested in commodity-related investments. Its biggest equity investment is in Nexen Inc., a

--- Page 103 ---
Page 103 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Canadian oil-and-gas concern. Its second biggest is in Fording Canadian Coal Trust. In partnership with
Sherritt International Corp., Ontario Teachers now owns 85% of Canada's coal reserves. Early this year, the
fund also acquired a 50% stake in 25 U.S. power-generation facilities.
"We believe that demand for energy will increase in the years ahead, and this strategic investment in the
energy sector will help us to pay many teachers' pensions in the future," Robert Bertram, Ontario's executive
vice president for investments, recently told plan members in a Webcast.
At the start of the decade, Europe's two biggest pension funds had no commodity investments. Stichting
Pensioenfonds ABP, the 157 billion euro ($190 billion) fund for Dutch government and educational-system
employees, got into commodities in 2001 and has reached its target of having 2.5% of its assets, or close to
$5 billion, in commodities. A year earlier, the 55.7 billion euro PGGM fund for Holland's health-and-welfare
sector waded into commodities. It now has 4.3%, or roughly $3 billion, invested in them.
Harvard University has been investing in commodities for more than a decade, and its internal benchmark
calls for allocating 13% of the university's $19.3 billion endowment to oil, gas and other commodities. That's
just two percentage points less than the weighting assigned to U.S. stocks and two points more than the
allocation to U.S. bonds. Unlike many pension funds that passively follow a benchmark, Harvard actively
manages its commodity investments, and 10 percentage points of the 13 allocated to commodities are
invested in timber.
"Commodities, we think, are a diversifying asset class with no correlation -- and in some cases, negative
correlation -- with other asset classes," says Jack Meyer, president and chief executive officer of Harvard
Management Co. in Boston. In other words, commodities don't tend to rise and fall in lockstep with stocks and
bonds, something that is also borne out in Harvard's performance. During the five years ended June 30, the
Standard &Poor's 500-stock index averaged minus 2.2% a year. Over the same period, Harvard's average
annual return from commodities was roughly 16%.
On the other hand, commodity prices tend to rise with inflation. And that, says Michel Meijs, a spokesman for
ABP, "is very important for a pension fund."

Blue Chips Gain On a Decrease In Crude Prices --- End of Convention Contributes To Dow Industrials' 0.8% Gain; Bonds Advance After Pullback
Thursday's Markets
Blue Chips Gain On a Decrease In Crude Prices --- End of Convention Contributes To Dow Industrials'
0.8% Gain; Bonds Advance After Pullback
By E.S. Browning
659 words
8 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS HIT A two-month high as investors dragged back to work from the Labor Day weekend, as summer
waned and a new trading season began.
Stock-trading volume rose after being held down last week by the Republican convention, but it remained
below average. Some investors remained on vacation, or remained nervous about the outlook. Still, a
continuing decline in oil prices, together with relief that the convention ended without significant trouble, were
enough to move the market ahead.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 82.59 points, or 0.80%, to 10342.79, now down only 1% on the year.
The blue chips pushed above 10300 for the first time since July 1.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.69%, or 7.67 points, to 1121.30, now up almost 1% on
the year. It, too, is at a two-month high, and is just above the 1120 level that some traders have been
watching nervously because it has been a point where people sold stocks in the past.
Treasury bonds also advanced, resuming their gains after their sharp pullback late last week, amid signs that
economic growth remains moderate. Bond investors will be closely watching Friday's release of August
wholesale price figures, which could indicate whether inflation is turning into a problem that could hurt bonds
by forcing interest rates higher.
With the Olympics and the conventions now history, some investors are concerned now about the third
anniversary of the 9/11 attacks, coming on Saturday. But many already are focusing on the November
election.
The days after Labor Day historically are a period when investors re-examine their expectations for the
remainder of the year and for the coming year. September often has been a difficult month for stocks, but in
years when the incumbent president is expected to win re-election, stocks typically rally ahead of the election.
Although many money managers remain uncertain, some are beginning to bet that a pre-election rally will
take place this year.
"Everyone is looking to the election as a big inflection point," said Tim Heekin, director of trading at brokerage
firm Thomas Weisel Partners in San Francisco. "Some institutions have put money to work ahead of that,
although some are waiting for the vote," he said, adding that some of the recent gains could be due to money
managers betting on a pre-election rally.
Oil prices fell after Saudi Arabia lowered its prices and the president of the Organization of Petroleum
Exporting Countries said production remained well above demand. Investors were heartened that Hurricane
Frances spared oil platforms in the Gulf of Mexico.
Investors will be closely scrutinizing Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan's testimony to the House
Budget Committee today for any signals on his plans for interest rates.
Fed policy makers have boosted their target short-term rate twice this year to 1.5%, and they hold three more
meetings before year's end. Interest-rate futures indicate that investors expect the Fed to boost its target rate
twice more, to 2%, before year's end, including a quarter-point increase on Sept. 21. Investors also are eager
to know whether Mr. Greenspan thinks the economy is emerging from the "soft spot" he has said it was
experiencing.
Technology stocks continued to suffer from Intel Corp.'s disappointing sales forecast last week, and chip
makers declined despite the market advance. The tech-laden Nasdaq Composite Index, however, rose
0.76%, or 14.08 points, to 1858.56, still down 7% on the year.

--- Page 105 ---
Page 105 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Gold fell, as did the dollar against the euro and yen. The 10-year Treasury note rose 10/32, or $3.125 for
each $1,000 invested. The yield, which moves inversely to price, fell to 4.246%.

Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...and Not: Wall Street Took August Vacation --- Global Indexes Followed, Logging...
Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...and Not: Wall Street Took August Vacation
--- Global Indexes Followed, Logging Little Movement; Russia Proved an Exception
By Peter A. McKay
538 words
7 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
One of the exceptions to the low-key activity has been the Russian stock market, which saw the Russian
Traded Index rise 12%, or 110.96 points, to 1028.87 since July, despite continuing legal troubles at the oil
giant OAO Yukos. Swiss and Chinese shares also registered decent gains, and a recent dip in oil prices after
record highs bodes well for stocks and the world economy in the near future, many analysts say.
Crude oil futures have risen 19 cents since the end of July, to $43.99 a barrel at the New York Mercantile
Exchange, but are off 11% from a record intraday high near $50 in mid-August. On Friday, prices fell 7 cents
after Iraq resumed use of a pipeline that had been attacked by insurgents.
Francis Gannon, senior portfolio manager at SunAmerica Mutual Funds, says the oil turnaround and the
respectable, if lackluster, performance of most sectors of the U.S. stock market lately are surprisingly upbeat
signals.
"If you look back at the last three months, we've had the Iraqi turnover, the political conventions and the
Olympics all go off pretty smoothly," says Mr. Gannon, referring to many investors' nervousness about
terrorist attacks at such events. "To have the market essentially flat at this point, after having those worries
going into the summer, is pretty impressive."
Outside Russia and Switzerland, most European markets have registered modest gains. Sweden's OMX
Index rose 2.1%, or 14.06 points, to 697.36. France's CAC-40 index edged up 0.7%, or 25.93 points, to
3673.03.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average and Standard &Poor's 500-stock index have registered modest gains
since July 30, each up about 1%. The Nasdaq Composite Index was off 2.3%, or 42.88 points, at 1844.48.
However, a profit warning by semiconductor giant Intel Corp. may hamper U.S. tech stocks again, which
would in turn also weigh on Japanese shares. Sure enough, Japan's stock market resumed sliding last week,
and since the end of July, the Nikkei 225 Stock average is down 0.7%, or 81.41 points, to 11244.37.
For the most part, gold has moved in sympathy with crude lately, as fluctuations in the price of crude
increase or ease investors' nervousness about other financial markets. The U.S. dollar's relative weakness
also has boosted gold, which is often used as a hedge against "paper" assets because it is perceived as a
tangible store of value.
Gold futures have risen 2.4%, or $9.50, to $400.50 a troy ounce since the end of July on Nymex's Comex
division. By contrast, the dollar is off 0.9% against the Japanese currency, at 110.14 yen. The euro is 0.4%
higher against the dollar, at $1.2066.
The Reuters-CRB Futures Index, a broad indicator of raw materials prices, has increased 2.1%, or 5.72
points, to 273.50 since July. A continued rise in the index could spur global inflation fears.

One American's Olympic Odyssey
LEISURE &ARTS
One American's Olympic Odyssey
By Peter Cipollone
1,279 words
2 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D16
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Athens -- I still do not want to sleep. It has been about a week since my crewmates and I won the Olympic
gold medal in rowing's premier event, the eight with coxswain, and I am afraid to close my eyes. I might miss
something from this utterly surreal experience.
About one month ago, our squad arrived in Athens to acclimatize for the Olympics. Despite the rumors and
media frenzy over Greece's halting preparations, our landing could not have gone more smoothly. Within two
hours of touching down, we were all registered and well fed, food being perhaps the essential component of
Olympian happiness. We then collected our USA Olympic uniforms, a Christmas-like frenzy where people
hand you scads of cool gear emblazoned with "USA" and the five rings all over the place.
We agreed not to attend the Games' opening ceremonies. It was too close to the day of our heats, where our
crew had drawn the No. 1-seeded Canadians. And we did not want to risk a 4 a.m. traffic jam, or getting
caught in a terrorist incident. Instead, our whole squad of about 60 people watched it on a big screen. Once
again, the Athenian execution was flawless. The ceremonies were on time, spectacular and thankfully brief.
The crowd did not boo the USA athletes, but instead grew silent as they marched in.
Racing began with a preliminary heat on Aug. 15. We would need a world-beating performance to defeat
heavily favored Canada, winners of the 2002 and 2003 world championships. Our preparation was simple
and spare. As we concluded our warm-up, stroke man Bryan Volpenhein turned around and spoke to the
crew: "Guys, it's just a boat race."
We got off the start line cleanly in a close third place, with the Italians and Canadians just edging us at the
500-meter mark. Now it was our turn. We dropped the Italians and kept the Canadians very close by, until the
last 350 meters of the race, when we sprinted. With each stroke, we narrowed their lead. With about 10
strokes remaining (there are about 220 strokes in the race), we took the lead and held it to the finish line. A
few minutes after the race, we found out that it had been a truly epic battle. We and Canada had shattered
the world record by almost three seconds. Our winning time of 5:19.85 got us into the gold-medal final; the
Canadians, having lost by 0.61 seconds, would have to race again in order to make the final.
The day of our final began like every other day in Greece, cloudless, breezy and hot. As we walked toward
the boathouse, we saw our parents and families standing behind a low fence, waiting for us to come by. Our
coach, Mike Teti, had arranged this crossing of paths as part of his pre-race plan. There were quick hugs,
and quiet exhortations of "We love you" and "Go get 'em." There is something calming about seeing your
parents before approaching a daunting task.
For our final preparation, Coach Teti brought in Coach Ted Nash, a 10-time Olympian and Olympic legend.
His message was simple: "Winning this race will make you brothers. It will change your life forever." Ted has
a gold medal from Rome 1960. He knows this firsthand.
Our lineup, from bow to stern, was Jason Read, Wyatt Allen, Christian Ahrens, Joey Hansen, Matt Deakin,
Dan "The Hurricane" Beery, Beau Hoopman, Bryan and me. As coxswain, I would steer, control the pace,
and act as the eyes of the oarsmen, who were facing backward. We put our boat in the water, and headed for
the start pontoons. At 10:30 a.m., it was nearly 100 degrees. Among the five other crews in the final, some
were snorting, barking and yelling encouragement at their boat mates. We were silent. There was not much
to say.
The starter's horn sounded. This was our one chance at it. We had a blistering first 20 strokes and somewhat
unexpectedly took the early lead. The rhythm within the crew felt unearthly, like a perfect golf stroke, repeated
47 times per minute and with bone-shaking power. I was holding on tighter than I ever have, just to avoid
getting tossed overboard. After the first 20 strokes, we shifted gears from our starting pace of 47 strokes per
minute, down to our base cadence of 37, focusing on our power and rhythm.

--- Page 108 ---
Page 108 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the body of the race, we had planned to wrest the lead away from the front-runners, but our start had taken
care of that. It was time to build an insurmountable margin. Our shell was moving so smoothly, as if we were
on ice skates flying down the course. I called for more power and we skipped town on our opposition. By the
halfway point, we had a more than three-second lead over the field.
As we came toward the finish, we knew that our competition would not yield so easily. We ratcheted up the
pace still more while keeping a close eye on the trailing crews. At 350 meters remaining, I yelled, "We are
one minute away from Olympic gold!" The crew responded and the crowd roared in the stands. Barring an
error, no one would catch us.
And then it happened. For a split second, Bryan and I looked at one another as if to ask, "Is it real?" It was.
What followed was a frenzy of joy, relief and unfiltered emotion. The bow man, Jason, stood up to cheer and
promptly fell into the lake. The Hurricane slumped back in his seat, unable to muster even a yell. We
whooped, barked, hugged and splashed. For Bryan, Christian and myself, the crew's veterans of Sydney
2000, our bitter memories were supplanted with sweet redemption. We had won the gold, breaking a 40-year
dry spell that stretched back to Tokyo 1964.
We spent the next week celebrating -- together, with our friends, families and the people walking the streets
of Athens. To see a child's eyes go wide at the sight of a gold medal is a joy we could once only imagine, but
now will never forget. We took photos, signed autographs, danced with the women's soccer team and chatted
up Katie Couric and Al Roker. Sleep was a waste of time. The next moment was far too full of potential.
At the closing ceremonies, we marched in mixed groups of athletes from different countries. We were with
Uzbekistani wrestlers wearing snakeskin shoes and Mongolians in pointed hats. We could not speak to one
another, but, man, could we all laugh together. We walked around the Olympic stadium waving and rallying
the spectators. And there was one most welcome difference. At the sight of our USA jackets and flags, the
crowd did not go silent. They cheered and waved and reached out across the barrier to touch us. It was hard
not to get a little misty.
Looking back over this season, there are some lessons I will take with me all the way. From the Greeks, we
learned that determination easily trumps the banter of cynics. We remembered, thanks to Bryan, that it is just
a boat race, something we have all done a thousand times, and done very well. When given chances to be
gracious and to show the world that Americans have good hearts, we seized each one.
---
Mr. Cipollone is an assistant director of Dow Jones and Reuters' Factiva product development group.

On Health Care, `Ownership' Is More Than a Talking Point
Business World
On Health Care, `Ownership' Is More Than a Talking Point
By Holman W. Jenkins Jr.
1,073 words
1 September 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Alan Greenspan nicely teed up the "ownership society," presumed theme of George Bush's convention
speech Thursday night. Mr. Bush will rightly emphasize the positive, but he'll be offering a way to deal with
the pending crash of the welfare state, topic of the Fed chairman's widely noted talk last Friday.
Social Security reform, if Mr. Bush mentions it, would be a big kahuna in the eyes of the media. But,
curiously, a potentially bigger kahuna is already in force, in the form of tax-shielded "health savings
accounts," authorized for the masses in last year's Medicare bill.
The idea is to put the consumer in charge of most routine and everyday spending decisions, returning
insurance to its proper role as protection against unpredictable, "catastrophic" expenses. The aim here, at
long last, is to tackle the perverse tax incentive at the heart of our health-care woes -- the massive tax
handout to employer-provided health insurance.
Mr. Bush has been selling HSAs on the stump for half a year, but nobody apparently listens to the president.
HSAs went unmentioned at the Democratic convention, where acknowledging the dilemmas of unconstrained
health-care inflation, let alone the potential solution, would violate the free-lunchism of the moment. Indeed,
in a delicious episode in House Speaker Denny Hastert's new autobiography, he has Hillary Clinton opposing
the Republican approach because Americans are "too greedy" to be trusted with their own spending
decisions.
Right. Critics also said only the young, healthy and wealthy would opt for HSAs as just another way to shelter
their incomes from taxation. Yet two early promoters of the accounts, eHealthInsurance and Assurant, found
this year that more than one-third of their applicants were previously uninsured, two-thirds were over 40, and
many were at the lower end of the income scale.
This is surprising only to those who never understood why the tax code was the problem in the first place.
Notice that the typical family policy doled out by companies to their employees represents a total price-tag of
about $9,086 a year. If you're in the top tax bracket, the effective after-tax cost to you is about $5,500. If
you're in the working-poor bracket (i.e. pay no federal income tax), it's $9,086.
In fact, it's doubtful that such an insurance product would even exist in the marketplace in the absence of a
massive tax subsidy, given the built-in incentives that naturally drive costs out of sight. Certainly you wouldn't
buy gold-plated, first-dollar health insurance if you faced the full tab alone.
Yet even some who know better -- say, corporate America -- would rather lobby the government for more
handouts than call for a restructuring of the tax incentive that causes all the trouble. The Big Three, for
example, have had a fun time convincing a certain large, national newspaper that, because their health
spending amounts to $1,400 per car, they're at a disadvantage to Japanese automakers, who don't pay for
their employees' health care.
Oh yes they do. We all on average pay for what we consume with what we earn. That's true of health care,
whether premiums are paid to a private insurer or taxes are paid to support a government-run system.
The Big Three have the same problem we all have: a tax code that waters and fertilizes a great deal of
unnecessary, inefficient spending by beneficiaries that payers are at a loss to control. Fourteen hundred
bucks per car -- that's roughly commensurate with health care's share of total GDP. No problem here. But
consider that GM's health spending amounts to $14,000 per current employee and is growing at, say, 12% a
year.
That translates into $1,680 per year that workers are taking home in additional compensation but don't know
it and don't feel it, because the extra money is being hoovered up to maintain existing benefit levels.

--- Page 110 ---
Page 110 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Putting a lid on health-care inflation, which means putting a lid on the tax distortion that feeds it, would
assuage a lot of social dissatisfactions about stagnant pay and "jobless" prosperity. Benefits managers have
cottoned on and talk, without irony, of a "revolution" in health-care finance. JP Morgan and Cigna just teamed
up to start offering employers an HSA-style insurance policy coupled with a debit card workers can use for
their out-of-pocket spending.
Yet the idea is absent from Democratic thinking because Democratic economists and policy advisers have
gotten the word that talking about individual incentives is somehow "Republican," thus verboten in party
policy documents.
No serious person doubts that our overreliance on third-party payment is the problem that will be solved -- or
will lead to a government-run, single-payer system that controls costs by denying care. In our information-rich
economy, the medical industry doesn't even publish price lists. Is this not downright weird and a sign change
is desperately needed? (The exception is cosmetic surgery, where, as health economist John Goodman
points out, consumers pay out-of-pocket and competition has meant prices are flat or falling).
Some in the Bush camp were prepared to go deeper than even the HSA kludge, totally revamping the tax
system. The idea was to help Americans shift their expectations: No longer will they send their tax money to
government and hope government will take care of them in old age. Now they will have ownership of the
assets that will take care of them in old age.
Hope for such boldness on Thursday night probably vanished the moment it became clear John Kerry was
going backward in the polls. It may be just as well. The HSA revolution suggests that simply offering
taxpayers a better choice may be the stealthy way to reform entitlements (and let's admit that the tax
deductibility of employer health care is a giant middle-class entitlement) without frightening swing voters with
any Big Bang-like proposals.
---
Mr. Jenkins edits "Political Diary," the editorial page's daily e-mail newsletter with commentary, analysis and
gossip on election-year politics. With John Fund. Subscribe at www.politicaldiary.com.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Health Savings Accounts Need Guidance, Protection" -- WSJ Sept.
7, 2004)

Business and Finance
483 words
30 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal

A1
English

GREENSPAN WARNED that the U.S. may have promised retirees more in Social Security and Medicare

Taste -- Review &Outlook: Rankings That Rankle
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Taste -- Review &Outlook: Rankings That Rankle
490 words
27 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
W13
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Some awards, like the Princeton Review's recent recognition of SUNY Albany as America's "top party
school," clearly aren't worth having. But these days even good news gets a bad rap in the academy. Just look
at how some of the winners are responding to this year's U.S. News &World Report college rankings.
No sooner did it emerge that Princeton was tied with Harvard for America's best college than the Princeton
P.R. office issued a statement sniffing that rankings "cannot capture the distinctiveness of any institution."
This was no odd sentiment. Harvard didn't even deign to offer comment. But when it won first place last year,
its director of undergraduate admissions reacted coolly: "It's always flattering to be appreciated," she told the
Crimson. "But there are a lot of better reasons for choosing a college."
Others object to the whole idea of ranking. Take Mount Holyoke President Joanne V. Creighton, who wrote
this for USA Today in 2001: "Not only should we refuse to give lip service to this specious and oversimplified
labeling of our institutions, we should resist labeling our students with numbers, too. There are insidious
parallels between the bogus ranking of colleges and universities by U.S. News and the ranking of students by
their SAT scores. "
Insidious, indeed. The academy is increasingly reluctant to acknowledge distinctions in merit. This plague of
indecision is yielding larger numbers of co-valedictorians and co-salutatorians and often puts students in the
dark about how they really stack up against their peers. Grade inflation hasn't helped. "We're all different"
has somehow morphed, within the protective confines of the ivory tower, into "we're all equally good."
Not that the U.S. News weightings represent any kind of gold standard. Under any system, the exact place of
each school is hard to defend. Any two that are adjacent on the list can be regarded, for all intents and
purposes, as equally good. Still, when you look at schools 10 slots apart, you'll start to notice a difference.
Critics love to note that the best place for a budding physicist may not be ideal for an artist. They're right, of
course -- but these aren't the kids plunking down their $9.95 for the U.S. News rankings. Most high-school
seniors don't know what they want to do with their lives. They're often looking for something generic: the best
place to go to stretch their minds and find their interests.
The popularity of the rankings tells us customers want more information about what they will be getting in
return for the tens of thousands of dollars a college education costs. If the U.S. News rankings are really that
much worse than other metrics, academic leaders ought to convene their best and brightest to give us a
better system.

Industrial Average Hits Six-Week High On Drop in Oil Prices
Wednesday's Markets
Industrial Average Hits Six-Week High On Drop in Oil Prices
By E.S. Browning
555 words
26 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average hit its highest level since July 14, as oil prices fell for a fourth
consecutive trading session.
Amid hopes that oil's relentless climb may be over, short-term traders stopped betting on stock declines and
began wagering that the economy will find its legs and resume the steady gains that Federal Reserve
Chairman Alan Greenspan and most economists have been predicting.
The industrial average, languishing at 9814 less than two weeks ago, rose 83.11 points, or 0.82%, to
10181.74. It has risen in seven of the past nine sessions, but still is down 2.6% since the year began.
A view is spreading that the headwinds buffeting the economy finally may weaken now, letting corporate
decision-makers and consumers alike to breathe easier and spend more money, said Jay Suskind, director of
trading at brokerage firm Ryan Beck in Florham Park, N.J. "We have gotten through the Democratic
convention, we are almost through the Olympics, and if we can get through the Republican National
Convention next week, then maybe we can focus on fundamentals again."
The day's actual economic news was disappointing, but the oil news was so encouraging that traders looked
past the other data. New-home sales fell 6.4% last month. Orders for durable goods -- big-ticket items such
as appliances and machinery -- rose in July, but that was due almost entirely to a one-time jump in airplane
orders. Otherwise, durable-goods orders were almost flat.
All of that was overshadowed by the sight of oil futures plunging 3.85%, or $1.74, to $43.47 a barrel, the
biggest one-day decline since June 2, almost three months ago. Crude oil is at a three-week low, pushed
down by selling by investors unwinding bets on higher oil prices. Oil has fallen 10.7% in four sessions, since
ending at a 21-year high of $48.70 last Thursday. Gasoline prices also fell.
Treasury bonds rose, as the soft economic news and declining oil prices spurred hopes that the Federal
Reserve will raise target short-term interest rates only gradually. The yield of the 10-year Treasury note,
which falls when its price rises, fell to 4.27%, nearing the four-month low of 4.19% hit Aug. 17.
Technology stocks, which would benefit from a spurt of corporate investment, led the gains, with the Nasdaq
Composite Index rising 1.30%, or 23.83 points, to 1860.72, still down 7% this year. The broad Standard
&Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.80%, or 8.77 points, to 1104.96, now down just 0.6% this year.
Gold rose $4.90 to $407.90 an ounce.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.19 billion shares traded, 2,334 stocks rose and 999 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 2/32, or 62.5 cents for each $1,000 invested. The 30-year
bond was up 8/32 to yield 5.050%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 110.14 yen, up from 109.63, while the euro rose against
the dollar to $1.2086 from $1.2083.

Downturn Made States and Cities More Dependent on Property Taxes
Downturn Made States and Cities More Dependent on Property Taxes
By Ray A. Smith
1,271 words
24 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A new analysis shows that cash-strapped state and local governments have increasingly come to depend on
property taxes to fill revenue shortfalls as other sources of income soured.
Though not an unexpected trend during economic downturns, the reliance on property taxes has lasted well
beyond the end of the recession of 2001. Economists say it is has been of greater depth, and likely will have
more lasting consequences, than it did during prior downturns.
In good part, that's because the soaring housing market has lifted the median price of single-family homes by
15%, making property taxes attractive to state and municipal governments facing spending increases.
Meanwhile, states have grown more reluctant to lift income or sales taxes in recent years for fear of political
retribution, economists say. In some cases, they are even prevented from doing so by laws passed during the
1990s boom.
"The local property tax is one tax the local [authorities] can use to offset declines in state money," says
William Fischel, a professor of economics at Dartmouth College. "To some extent, if the state is sending
municipalities or even school districts less money because they're in fiscally difficult times, the one tax the
local [authorities] have some discretion over raising is the property tax."
Though municipalities can offset rising property values by changing their millage rates, or the rates at which
taxes are levied on properties, many strapped municipalities chose not to do so in recent years. "Ultimately,
what determines whether property taxes go up is the overall budget for the taxing district," says Matt Gardner,
state-tax-policy director with the Institute on Taxation and Economic Policy in Washington, D.C.
The new analysis, prepared for The Wall Street Journal, shows that property taxes have risen rapidly since
2001 as a share of state and local tax revenue, while income taxes fell and sales taxes rose only slightly.
Property taxes represented a 28.2% share of state and local revenue in the first quarter of 2004, up from
25.5% in the first quarter of 2001.
During that span, income taxes fell to 19.4% of state and local revenue collections from 22.4%, while sales
taxes as a proportion rose to 32.2% from 31.4%.
The analysis was done by Robert Tannenwald, assistant vice president and economist at the Federal
Reserve Bank of Boston, using data from the federal Bureau of Economic Analysis.
Overall, between 2001 and 2003, property taxes, which are used to fund everything from police and fire
departments to schools and recreational services, rose an average of more than 10% nationwide, estimates
Deloitte &Touche LLP's Property Tax Services Group.
The increased state and local reliance on property taxes over the past few years marked a reversal from the
trend of the mid to late 1990s. "Over time, there had been a trend for property taxes to play a smaller role in
overall tax collections than income or sales taxes," says Judy Zelio, principal at the National Conference of
State Legislatures' fiscal-affairs program. But in recessions, property taxes tend to creep back up as a
percentage, as happened during the recession of the early 1990s.
The recent rise in property taxes has spurred a political backlash in some areas. In Maine and parts of New
Jersey, Ohio and Texas, residents and sometimes city officials have succeeded in getting property-tax
reduction initiatives on the Nov. 2 ballot. But economists warn that such limits could potentially squeeze state
and local governments even more in a future downturn -- especially if rising interest rates cause the
scorching housing market to cool off, forcing housing prices lower.
"If you get ballot measures that put caps on rates and they pass, that could have a significant impact on state
and local governments," says Robert Lynch, a research associate with the Economic Policy Institute in
Washington and economics professor at Washington College in Chestertown, Md. Governments forced to

--- Page 120 ---
Page 120 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
restrict property taxes could face major revenue problems during a future downturn, and thus may face
service cuts, he warns.
Many factors contributed to the increase in property tax revenues that began during the recent recession,
economists say. The suddenness of the recession, and the stock-market collapse in 2000, rapidly decimated
income and sales tax revenues. Confronted with severe budget problems, states were forced to make drastic
budget cuts and, to a lesser extent, increase fees and drain their reserves.
Meanwhile, after years of steady growth as a result of the economic boom of the mid- to late-1990s, state
income and sales-tax collections were hit hard during the downturn. That also hurt local governments. State
tax revenue declined 8% in fiscal year 2002, the first time such revenue had declined in 10 years -- during the
recession of the early 1990s, according to the Nelson A. Rockefeller Institute of Government, the
public-policy-research arm of the State University of New York. It fell a further 3.4% in fiscal 2003.
Some of the decline in tax revenue was due directly to the weak economy, specifically job losses and
reduced spending. The stock-market downturn also hurt. "The drop of 2001 had an especially large impact,
because capital gains had become as much as 15% of the overall income tax base over the course of the
boom of the 1990s, and then almost dried up," says Harley Duncan, executive director for the Federation of
Tax Administrators, a Washington D.C.-based organization that provides services to state tax authorities and
administrators.
In that environment, the strong housing market proved to be the real potential gold mine to state and local
governments. The median price of an existing single-family home rose 15% between 2001 and 2003 to
$170,000. Declining commercial bases, rising health-care and pension costs, and expanding tax breaks for
business and elderly and low-income homeowners all played a role in pushing up property-tax bills for those
without such exemptions.
So did soaring expenses. According to the National League of Cities in Washington, D.C., 62% of cities said
they had increased public-safety spending, much of it due to homeland security, in the past year; 38% said
they had increased capital spending on infrastructure; and 10% said they had increased spending on
education. Four out of five cities and towns reported being less able to meet their financial needs in 2003 than
in the previous year and expected to be less able to meet those needs in 2004, citing declines in sales,
income and tourist tax revenues and cuts in federal and state aid, among other things.
Economists note that property-tax increases are not irreversible. Mr. Duncan points out that they are set each
year by votes of local governing boards, which makes them easier to change than the acts of state or federal
legislatures.
At the moment, states seem to be recovering from their fiscal problems. State tax revenue, for example,
increased 6.7% during the second quarter, the end of the 2004 fiscal year for 46 states, according to
preliminary data by the Rockefeller Institute. But that may not quickly result in increased aid to cities or
reduced property taxes. "State tax revenue is definitely growing, but it has a long ways to go to reclaim the
levels that it reached before the 2001 recession," says Nicholas W. Jenny, a senior policy analyst in the
Institute's fiscal-studies program.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Prudent Revenue Strategy: Taxes Tied to Real Estate" -- WSJ Sept.
2, 2004)

Gold Surges to a 12-Day High As Dollar Slides on Jobs Data
Commodities Report
Gold Surges to a 12-Day High As Dollar Slides on Jobs Data
By Gavin Maguire Dow Jones Newswires
474 words
9 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold futures on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange jumped to a
12-day high of $404 an ounce Friday, reacting to the decline in the dollar against its rivals after a
disappointing U.S. employment report.
The nearby-month benchmark price rose $7.50 to $399.80 an ounce, while the most-active December
contract rose $7.30 to $402.10 an ounce.
The report indicated that U.S. employers slowed hiring rates substantially in July and sparked fresh fears that
the economic recovery may be slowing sharply. That triggered a bailout in the U.S. currency by international
investors who sought refuge in nondollar-correlating assets such as gold.
December-contract prices on Comex consequently jumped from below $395 to the $400-$401 region within
minutes of the data's release and then embarked on a gentler climb to the $404 high by midmorning as fund
interest persisted.
Activity then slowed as traders digested the likely repercussions of the employment news on Federal Reserve
interest-rate policy at the coming monetary-policy meeting.
Should the Federal Reserve's accompanying statement on its rate decision tomorrow intimate that it is likely
to take its time raising interest rates for fear of slowing economic growth further, gold stands to benefit from
renewed fund interest over the near to medium term, gold dealers said. In that event, the $405, $407 and
$410 levels are targeted to the upside, they said.
But, if the latest data disappointment is deemed a "blip" and other sectors of the economy demonstrate signs
of strength, speculators' and investors' interest may retain a more bullish stance toward the dollar at the
expense of gold. Should that be the case, a slight easing in gold back below the $400 mark toward the
$395-$397 region might be seen, the gold dealers said.
In other commodities markets Friday:
CRUDE OIL: September futures prices on Nymex for crude oil reached a fresh high of $44.77 a barrel early
Friday in overnight trade, then fell to $43.95 by the end of pit trading Friday afternoon, down 46 cents on the
day. Traders attributed the retreat to book-squaring ahead of the weekend but said they still expect oil prices
to march higher amid strong demand, little spare production capacity and fear of supply disruptions.
COCOA: Futures prices for cocoa fell at the New York Board of Trade amid traders' concern that the past
month's rally could be near its end. Heavy fund selling pushed the September contract down $93 to $1,610 a
metric ton. Traders said the recent upward move was exaggerated as there was little commercial participation
and the long-term fundamentals were only slightly positive.

C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
PARIS -- The Paris stock exchange's operator, Euronext SA, has decided to close France's gold market until
further notice because there aren't enough operators to make it worthwhile.
Euronext said Friday that the move follows a decision by Credit du Nord, a unit of Societe Generale SA, to
cease trading in physical gold.
Market insiders say the writing had been on the wall for years. The market has waned since the 1980s, when
inflation was rampant and jitters over the local political scene had French residents scrabbling for an
investment haven.

English
