THE AMERICAS
World Bank to Continue Financing Oil Projects
WASHINGTON -- The World Bank's shareholders rejected a call to phase out financing of oil projects, opting
instead to let the bank choose its projects more carefully to ensure they reduce poverty and don't harm the
environment.
The decision closed the book on a three-year review of the bank's financial support for the extraction of oil,
coal, gold and natural gas in poor nations. It amounted to a repudiation of the reforms proposed by an
outside expert hired by the bank to conduct that study. The expert, Emil Salim, had called for an end to
oil-related loans by 2008, among other things.
The bank's 24-member board of directors decided unanimously to permit the bank to continue to make
oil-related loans, said Rashad Kaldany, director of the bank's department of oil, gas, mining and chemicals.
The bank's managers have said they intend to be "more selective" with such projects, with "greater focus on
the needs of poor people and a stronger emphasis promoting environmentally and socially sustainable
development."
Mr. Kaldany said the bank's directors decided that constituted a "balanced" alternative to the proposals made
by Mr. Salim, a former Indonesian environment minister. "There was very broad consensus that we should
remain engaged [in the oil sector] -- that we do add value through our involvement," Mr. Kaldany said.
The bank lends about $500 million to $600 million a year to support projects related to oil, gas and mining --
about 3% of its entire portfolio, according to Mr. Kaldany.
But critics contend the bank's participation has allowed gigantic oil-extraction projects -- such as the $3 billion
Chad-Cameroon pipeline -- to go forward. Often, the critics say, such projects cause social disruptions and
environmental harm while doing little to reduce poverty.
-- Dow Jones Newswires
---
ASIA/PACIFIC
Oil Companies Quit China Pipeline Project
SINGAPORE -- A consortium comprising Royal Dutch/Shell Group, Exxon Mobil Corp. and Gazprom OAO
decided to pull out of PetroChina Co.'s $5.25 billion West-East natural-gas project in China.
A Shell official said the consortium decided to pull out of the pipeline project because it was unable to find
"common ground" with PetroChina. Shell is the lead negotiator with PetroChina. PetroChina couldn't be
reached for comment.
According to an initial agreement with PetroChina, the three companies in the consortium each have a 15%
stake in the project, with PetroChina holding 50% and Sinopec Corp. holding 5%. The consortium, however,
hasn't provided any funding for the project.
The negotiations have been deadlocked since early this year on a number of issues, including the percentage
of the rate of return on the investment and downstream market development.

--- Page 136 ---
Page 136 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
When announced two years ago, the project -- a 2,500-mile pipeline stretching from China's far west to its
eastern coast -- seemed to mark the close of an era in which Beijing viewed its vast gas and oil fields as the
crown jewels of its socialist economy, off limits to foreigners.
China-based industry officials said the consortium's withdrawal from the project will hurt the confidence of
foreign companies in investing in China's energy sector in the long run, even though the move will have little
impact on the operation of the pipeline.
The Shell official said, however, that its energy business in China will continue to grow, despite the failure to
reach an accord on the pipeline project. "We expect that the total funding for the Shell share of projects in
China during 2004 will amount to $1 billion, the highest ever in a single year," he said.
The West-East gas pipeline links gas reserves in the Ordos and Tarim basins in western China and markets
in eastern China around Shanghai. PetroChina started commercial operation of the first section of the
pipeline in January and completed construction of the second section yesterday.
PetroChina aims to increase the gas supply from the pipeline to eastern China to five billion cubic meters next
year, far lower than the pipeline's designed capacity of 12 billion cubic meters a year, mostly to household
and commercial users.
-- Xu Yihe, Dow Jones Newswires
---
EUROPE
Russian Duma Supports Overhaul of Benefits
MOSCOW -- Russia's pro-Kremlin Parliament backed a bill that would replace benefits such as subsidized
transportation and medicine with cash payments, dismantling remnants of the Soviet welfare state and
affecting millions of vulnerable citizens.
The proposed legislation is part of the unpopular and potentially painful overhauls President Vladimir Putin
has promised to tackle during his second term. His goal is to slash bureaucracy and corruption. Opponents
say it is a cheap ploy to save money at the expense of society's most vulnerable. It has sparked criticism
across Russia, with nearly daily protests in Moscow last week and several rallies held in dozens of Russia's
far-flung regions during the past month.
The State Duma supported the bill yesterday and was expected to pass it in a final vote tomorrow. The bill
then must be approved by the upper house, which also is obedient to the Kremlin, before it goes to Mr. Putin
for his signature.
Advocates of the government-backed bill say substituting cash for benefits will make aid more accurately
targeted -- arguing, for example, that public transportation is scarce in rural areas and supplies of subsidized
medicines are short. They also say it will put people less at the mercy of the country's laborious bureaucracy.
Opponents of the bill -- which affects more than 30 million of the neediest Russians, more than one-fifth of the
population -- say the proposed payments, which start at $5 a month, will be eaten away quickly by inflation
and won't be paid in full by regional authorities.
Duma speaker Boris Gryzlov, of the main pro-Kremlin party, United Russia, said he "was deeply convinced"
that the situation for citizens now entitled to benefits "will significantly improve" with the new law.
Lawmakers outside United Russia blasted the bill. "Scientists first experiment on animals. But our
government is experimenting on people, on the whole country," said Gennady Seleznyov, an ex-Communist
and former Duma speaker who now is an independent lawmaker.
-- Associated Press
IMF Growth Forecast for Euro Zone Brightens
WASHINGTON -- The International Monetary Fund raised its projection for economic growth in the euro zone
this year, but warned that structural reforms are needed to keep recovery on track.
The IMF raised its forecast for real gross-domestic-product growth by 0.3 percentage point, to 2%, according
to a summary of its annual review of the region's economy. The upgrade from projections published in April,
prompted by strong demand for European exports, came despite expectations for unemployment to remain
elevated and domestic demand to recover slowly.

--- Page 137 ---
Page 137 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"A buoyant global economy and bullish financial markets have prompted a cyclical rebound of growth, while
the outlook for medium-term price stability remains favorable," the IMF said.
"A recovery is in the works, but in the staff's view, it is not in the bag," Michael Deppler, the IMF's European
department director, told reporters.
A renewed appreciation of the euro, which gained 9.4% in real terms against major trading partners in 2003,
is a downside risk to the economy, the IMF said.
It also expressed worry that "reform fatigue" has set in for many European governments, with progress in
labor and budget overhauls falling far short of what is needed to prepare for a rapidly aging work force.
-- Dow Jones Newswires

Forex View: Dollar May Face Some Pressures From Terror Alert
Currency Trading
Forex View: Dollar May Face Some Pressures From Terror Alert
By Grainne McCarthy and Tom Barkley Dow Jones Newswires
426 words
2 August 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar could come under some pressure early this week after the U.S. warned of possible terrorist attacks
against "iconic" financial institutions in New York City, Washington and Newark N.J.
After taking the unprecedented step of naming buildings because of the specificity of intelligence reports,
Homeland Security Secretary Tom Ridge said yesterday the government's threat levels for financial
institutions would be raised to orange, or high alert. The government named the Citigroup building and the
New York Stock Exchange in New York, the International Monetary Fund and World Bank buildings in
Washington, and the Prudential building in Newark.
"If there is a reaction, it will be dollar negative," said Marc Chandler, chief currency strategist at HSBC in New
York, although he added that the reaction may prove short-lived.
Mr. Chandler said the Swiss franc, a typical safe harbor in times of global instability, could see some flows,
while gold -- another safe-harbor play -- may also see some buying. U.S. Treasurys should benefit from
investors parking their funds in the safest of global investments.
Ever since the Sept. 11, 2001, attacks on New York and Washington, the dollar has tended to suffer in times
of terrorism fears, not least due to the role of the U.S. at the center of global efforts to counter terrorism.
As more time passed, however, investors and traders became less rattled by rumors and more focused on
quantifiable economic news and Fed policy. This week, the dollar will likely look more to such economic data
also, with heavyweight U.S. reports, including the July employment report, liable to mitigate the impact of the
terror threats.
Many short-term investors have taken their bets against the dollar off the table following a period in which
short-dollar positions in the speculative community had reached extreme level. That left the dollar more
capable of building on its substantial gains last week.
Having reached multimonth highs against several major currencies the day before -- including a dip in the
euro below $1.20 for the first time in two months -- the dollar ended the week in familiar territory.
Late Friday, the euro was trading at $1.2024, little changed from $1.2031 late Thursday in New York. The
dollar slid to 111.20 yen from 112.17 yen and was trading at 1.2811 Swiss francs from 1.2827 Swiss francs.
The pound climbed to $1.8211 from $1.8163 Thursday.

month's average, at 1.52 billion shares.
"Maybe the market is starting to get some footing here," said Warren Epstein, head trader at Kabrik Trading
in New York. "It is possible that the market is starting to digest the earnings and realize that they aren't so
bad," he said. He added that traders won't stop worrying until they see the gains continue for a while.
Crude oil fell to $42.75 a barrel from Wednesday's 21-year high of $42.90. JDS Uniphase issued a strong
quarterly sales projection, and Aetna released a better-than-expected annual profit forecast.
A quarterly measure of labor costs rose 0.9%, as expected, helping calm inflation fears.
The dollar hit another two-month high against the yen and a one-month high against the euro. Gold declined.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.46%, or 5.01 points, to 1100.43, while the Nasdaq
Composite Index advanced 1.23%, or 22.80 points, to 1881.06.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.55%, or 0.79 point, to 144.57.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, 2,325 stocks rose and 949 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 3/32, or $9.375 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, fell to 4.575%. The 30-year bond was up 7/32 to yield 5.299%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 112.17 yen, up from 111.69, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2031 from $1.2046.

OIL PRICES LEAPT past $43 a barrel before settling at a Nymex high of $42.90 on fears that a dispute
between Moscow and Yukos could jeopardize Russian crude shipments. Yukos's CEO said a court order
seeking to block asset sales could be interpreted as forcing the oil giant to stop pumping, but added that the
company doesn't plan to halt production.
Stocks fell in morning trading on the surge in oil prices but later rallied. The Dow industrials finished up 31.93
at 10117.07.
---
IBM expects to lay off fewer U.S. employees this year because of work being transferred overseas, as the
company reacts to concerns about outsourcing.
---
Boeing swung to a $607 million profit, buoyed by defense sales, a better outlook for commercial-aircraft
deliveries and a tax refund.
---
Durable-goods orders rose a modest 0.7% in June. The Fed reported solid economic growth, despite a
slowdown in some regions.
Inventories jumped last quarter, but they aren't expected to prompt a slowdown in output.
---
Time Warner's net fell 27% due to tough year-ago comparisons. The company disclosed an accounting probe
of AOL Europe.
---
Comcast swung to a profit as revenue rose 10%, but shares fell on concern about competition from telephone
and satellite companies.
---
Sony's net surged, aided by affiliates and chip sales. Canon, Sharp and JVC had strong results; Pioneer's
earnings fell.
---
DaimlerChrysler plans to name Eckhard Cordes to head Mercedes, putting him in the running to succeed
Jurgen Schrempp as CEO.
---
Honda's net rose 12% as cost cutting and higher Asian sales offset falling North American revenue and a
weaker dollar.
---

Cooper agreed to buy Ocular Sciences for $1.2 billion in cash and stock to create one of the biggest U.S.
contact-lens makers.

The FTC conditionally approved Sanofi's takeover of Aventis. The commission also cleared the merger of
Sony Music and BMG.
---
Unilever said profit rose 33%, helped by a more favorable tax rate, but warned that weak consumer
confidence is hurting sales.
---
CSX and Norfolk Southern reported mixed profits. But both railroads saw a surge in freight volume, a key
economic indicator.

Conoco's earnings surged 75%, boosted by rising prices for oil and natural gas and improved margins at its

Coral Energy will pay $30 million to settle charges it attempted to manipulate natural-gas deals.

The SEC is probing Wonder Bread maker Interstate over its workers' compensation reserves.

Stocks Drop as Chip Group Sags
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Drop as Chip Group Sags
By E.S. Browning
716 words
22 July 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
EASY COME, easy go.
The celebration of Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan's upbeat comments lasted one day, as major
stock indexes wiped out all of Tuesday's gains and more. Stocks showed life in the morning, but by day's end
the Nasdaq Composite Index had limped to its lowest finish since October. The Dow Jones Industrial Average
and Standard &Poor's 500-stock index finished at their lowest points since May. The Dow industrials' one-day
drop of 102.94 points, or 1.01%, was the sharpest since May 17, leaving the index at 10046.13, dangerously
close to the 10000 mark. The industrials have fallen 3.9% this year.
Technology stocks, especially computer-chip makers, led the declines amid worries that future earnings
growth won't justify the stocks' prices. The chip group sagged 3.7%, while the Nasdaq composite, filled with
tech stocks, fell 2.23%, or 42.70 points, to 1874.37, its sharpest percentage pullback since March and its
biggest point drop since July 6. It is down 6.4% on the year.
A catalyst for the declines was the sight of stocks such as Motorola and Texas Instruments, whose strong
earnings reports after Tuesday's close powered the market ahead at the opening bell, suddenly plunging as
the day wore on. Other stocks followed.
The problem was uncertainty about the future, and traders said the worries fed on themselves. Some
mentioned worries about the prospects for profit-margin growth at Motorola, while others noted that Texas
Instruments' chip orders fell 2% in the second quarter from the first quarter. Fears spread that growth among
chip makers and tech companies in general will slow.
"Motorola kind of rolled over and the rest of the market followed," said Robert Harrington, co-head of U.S.
stock trading at UBS's brokerage arm. "The earnings were OK, but people are worried about growth. Mr.
Greenspan said he isn't worried about the [economy's] growth slowdown in June, but the market may be
more worried than he is. If you are going to see slowing economic growth, then earnings growth has probably
peaked."
Also worrisome to traders, volume was the heaviest since May, with 1.68 billion shares trading on the New
York Stock Exchange in a day of widespread declines.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index broke below its closely watched average value of the past 200
days, falling 1.33%, or 14.79 points, to 1093.88, down 1.6% on the year. It had been holding near its 200-day

trading hours, when eBay reported strong earnings gains, but didn't offer a strong-enough forecast to please
investors. The stock fell 5.5% in after-hours trading.
With nearly one-third of the companies in the S&P 500 now reporting second-quarter earnings, gains have
averaged more than 27%, close to the peak of 28% hit in last year's fourth quarter, according to Thomson

apparently, no longer is enough.
Gold declined for a third day, falling 1% to $397.10 an ounce, below $400 for the first time since July 6.
Bonds fell again but the dollar advanced.

STOCKS GOT an afternoon boost from Alan Greenspan, and got more good news after regular trading
ended as Microsoft announced a surprise dividend payout.
With Federal Reserve Chairman Greenspan on Capitol Hill forecasting continuing economic growth and
controlled inflation, the Dow Jones Industrial Average broke a four-day losing streak, rising 55.01 points, or
0.55%, to 10149.07. Reflecting how weak the market has been of late, that was the blue chips' biggest gain
this month and it still left the Dow industrials down almost 3% on the year.
All three major indexes, in fact, posted their biggest gains this month, with the beleaguered Nasdaq
Composite Index rising 1.76%, its strongest advance since June 7.
Although the Federal Reserve boosted its inflation forecast for the year and slightly reduced its projection for
economic growth, investors welcomed Mr. Greenspan's comments. He told the Senate Banking Committee
that Fed interest-rate increases are "likely to be measured" as long as inflation pressures don't pick up.
"People continue to believe that he is going to keep the interest-rate increases in check," said Bob Basel,
senior trader at Citigroup's New York brokerage arm. "We will be able to deal with the small increases, rather
than have a big increase or a surprise increase."
After regular trading, investors got good news from both Microsoft and Motorola. Motorola forecast strong
sales for the current quarter and Microsoft announced a $30 billion stock repurchase, as well as a $32 billion
one-time dividend and a doubling of its annual dividend to 32 cents a share.
In after-hours trading, Motorola rose 5.6% and Microsoft was up 5.3%.
The dollar rose sharply on Mr. Greenspan's upbeat comments about the economy, while oil and gold both
declined. Treasury bonds, whose yields had been pulling back on hopes that a weak economy would reduce
the need for interest-rate increases, reacted poorly to the Fed Chairman's remarks. The yield of the
benchmark 10-year Treasury note suffered its biggest jump in three weeks.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.71%, or 7.77 points, to 1108.67, still down 0.3% this
year. The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many technology stocks led the declines this summer, rebounded
33.24 points to 1917.07, down 4.3% for the year.
Optimists saw the day's rebound as a sign that the market is coming out of the month's doldrums, but
pessimists said stocks were overdue for a technical bounce and that there still is no guarantee that the gains
will be sustained.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.54%, or 0.81 point, to 148.04.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.44 billion shares traded, 1,938 stocks rose and 1,337 fell.

the dollar to $1.2328 from $1.2438.

Truckin': Sector Counters Another Down Web Day

Affluent Advantage: So Far, Economic Recovery Tilts To Highest-Income Americans --- They Gain More, Spend More; With Job Market Rising, Will...
Affluent Advantage: So Far, Economic Recovery Tilts To Highest-Income Americans --- They Gain
More, Spend More; With Job Market Rising, Will Others Feel Rebound? --- Mr. Williams Waits for a
Raise
By Jon E. Hilsenrath and Sholnn Freeman
2,428 words
20 July 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
TOTAL DIVIDEND PAYMENTS to investors have risen 11%, or the equivalent of $3 billion, since the end of
2002. A page-one article yesterday incorrectly stated that dividend payments have risen 11% to $3 billion
since the end of 2002.
(WSJ July 21, 2004)
(END)
Joshua Berry and Ricky Williams, both Houstonians, have seen two very different economic recoveries.
Mr. Berry, an entrepreneur, has profited handsomely from the stock market, in the real-estate boom and by
selling a business. Mr. Williams, an airline baggage handler, has been waiting since 2001 for a pay raise.
With the U.S. economy expanding and the labor market improving, it isn't clear how well the Democrats'
message of a divided America will resonate with voters this fall. But many economists believe the economic
recovery has indeed taken two tracks, exemplified by the experiences of these two Texas residents.
Upper-income families, who pay the most in taxes and reaped the largest gains from the tax cuts President
Bush championed, drove a surge of consumer spending a year ago that helped to rev up the recovery.
Wealthier households also have been big beneficiaries of the stronger stock market, higher corporate profits,
bigger dividend payments and the boom in housing.
Lower- and middle-income households have benefited from some of these trends, but not nearly as much.
For them, paychecks and day-to-day living expenses have a much bigger effect. Many have been squeezed,
with wages under pressure and with gasoline and food prices higher. The resulting two-tier recovery is
showing up in vivid detail in the way Americans are spending money.
Hotel revenue was up 11% in the first five months of 2004 at luxury and upscale chains, but up just 3% at
economy chains, according to Smith Travel Research, a market-research firm. At the five-star Broadmoor
Hotel in Colorado Springs, Colo., $600-a-night lakeside suites are sold out every day through mid-October.
At high-end Bulgari stores, meanwhile, consumers are gobbling up $5,000 Astrale gold and diamond
"cocktail" rings made for the right hand, a spokeswoman says. The Italian company's U.S. revenue was up
22% in the first quarter. Neiman Marcus Group Inc., flourishing on sales of pricey items like $500 Manolo
Blahnik shoes, had a 13.5% year-over-year sales rise at stores open at least a year.
By contrast, such "same store" sales at Wal-Mart Stores Inc., retailer for the masses, were up just 2.2% in
June. Wal-Mart believes higher gasoline costs are pinching its customers. At Payless ShoeSource Inc., which
sells items like $10.99 pumps, June same-store sales were 1% below a year earlier.
A similar pattern shows up in cars. Luxury brands like BMW, Cadillac and Lexus saw double-digit U.S. sales
increases in June from a year earlier. Sales of lower-tier brands such as Dodge, Pontiac and Mercury either
declined or grew in the low single digits.
"To date, the [recovery's] primary beneficiaries have been upper-income households," concludes Dean Maki,
a J.P. Morgan Chase (and former Federal Reserve) economist who has studied the ways that changes in
wealth affect spending. In research he sent to clients this month, Mr. Maki said, "Two of the main factors
supporting spending over the past year, tax cuts and increases in [stock] wealth, have sharply benefited
upper income households relative to others."

--- Page 157 ---
Page 157 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The good times upper-income Americans are enjoying represent a bounce-back from the hit that many of the
wealthiest took after bonus income dried up in 2001 and 2002 and stock options went sour. For example,
Wall Street compensation was up 16% in the first quarter from a year earlier, after having fallen from
stratospheric levels the three previous years, according to the Securities Industry Association.
Longer-term issues are also at work. Wage and income disparities between the rich and poor have generally
been widening for nearly 20 years. In 1980, the top 10% of households in income accounted for 33% of total
household income, according to economist Emmanuel Saez at University of California, Berkeley. By 2000,
that had risen to 44%. The figures exclude capital gains. Mr. Saez says the concentration of income at the top
dropped during the recession but has probably started picking up again.
Some economists believe the gap is driven wider by technological change and by the economy's increasing
openness to global competition. Technology rewards skilled workers, and competition has generally punished
the unskilled, who are susceptible to the movement of work overseas. Both factors have come into play in
recent years as technology-driven productivity surged and the U.S. trade deficit widened.
Meanwhile, the U.S. has seen a big increase in the sheer number of affluent families. In 2002, nearly 16
million U.S. households had annual incomes of more than $100,000, up from a little more than five million 20
years ago, in inflation-adjusted terms.
For a sense of the divide, contrast the recovery experiences of Mr. Berry, a businessman who earns a
six-figure income, and Mr. Williams, the baggage handler, who makes around $20 an hour for Southwest
Airlines. Both have been shopping this month at the River Oaks Chrysler-Jeep car dealership in Houston.
Mr. Berry, 34 years old, is president of a nurse staffing business called ShiftBay.com. Last year, he and some
partners sold a medical-supply business. Mr. Berry says that together they saved more than $100,000 in
taxes, thanks to a reduction last year in the federal income-tax rate on long-term capital gains.
Mr. Berry also is in the process of selling his house, which he says has appreciated by almost $100,000 over
four years. And he says that while he lost a lot of money in the stock-market downturn that began four years
ago, he has enjoyed hefty gains since the market turned up about 15 months ago. Mr. Berry is choosing
between sticking with Chrysler (he now drives a Jeep Grand Cherokee) or trading up to a Cadillac, BMW or
Mercedes-Benz.
Mr. Williams, 52, hasn't benefited from the boom in the price of houses because he doesn't own one. His pay
hasn't budged since 2001, although he is expecting a raise this month. Within a year, he expects his hourly
pay to rise to about $24.
Mr. Williams's car lease (he, too, drives a Grand Cherokee) will be up in October, and he has been
scrambling to come up with a down payment for a new Chrysler PT Cruiser. He was still $1,800 short last
week. A Chrysler salesman was able to make up part of the difference with an additional $1,000 rebate
targeted at returning lease customers, on top of $4,000 in manufacturer's incentives already on the table.
"With the economy the way it is, I've had to rob Peter to pay Paul, and then sometimes rob them both," Mr.
Williams says.
The perception of a fast-lane/slow-lane recovery has become a central political issue. This year's stronger job
market has led Democrats to shift their emphasis: away from the argument that Bush policies have failed to
produce jobs and toward the idea that the expansion's fruits haven't been widely shared.
"They're telling people this is the best economy we've had," Sen. John Kerry mockingly told a riverbank crowd
last Thursday evening in Charleston, W.Va., drawing jeers. "What does it mean when you don't have any
health care at all?" Hands started popping up throughout the audience, as Mr. Kerry paused to point to each
one. "Too many people in Washington have no sense at all about what's happening," he said. His running
mate, Sen. John Edwards, speaks of "Two Americas," one "that does the work, another America that reaps
the rewards."
Bush critics have argued that the economy is producing jobs mainly in low-paying industries like restaurants
and temporary work. Mr. Bush counters that his opponents have been pessimistically distorting the economic
statistics, ignoring the gains. The Bush campaign cites data from the Bureau of Labor Statistics showing that
in the past year, there has been more net employment growth in occupations with above-average pay than in
occupations with below-average pay.
Campaigning in Wisconsin last week, Mr. Bush spoke of a local family of six who benefited from elements of
his tax package aimed at lower- and middle-income families, such as the child tax credit. "Oh, some of the
sophisticates will say that $2,700 doesn't matter to the Muellers: `It doesn't sound like a lot to me,' " Mr. Bush
said. "It is a lot to them. That's what counts."

--- Page 158 ---
Page 158 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Polls suggest that Americans aren't giving the president full credit for an economic recovery, and the class
divide in this recovery may help explain that. A Wall Street Journal/NBC News poll in late June found that
45% of Americans approved of his handling of the economy, while 49% didn't.
Mr. Maki of J.P. Morgan Chase estimates that in terms of dollars saved, the top 20% of households by
income got 77% of the benefit of the 2003 tax cuts, and roughly 50% of the 2001 tax cuts. And of stocks held
by households, roughly 75% are owned by the top 20% of those households. That made them prime
beneficiaries of last year's stock-market rally, although also big sufferers from the stock carnage from 2000 to
2002.
The affluent also benefit more from stock dividends, on which the federal income-tax rate was cut last year
retroactive to the start of 2003. Total dividend payments have risen 11% to $3 billion since the end of 2002,
estimates Berkeley's Mr. Saez. Higher-income households also are larger beneficiaries of the surge in
corporate earnings, which helps to drive dividend and stock returns. The level of corporate profits has risen
42% since the last recession, which ended in the final quarter of 2001. Wage and salary income is up just
6.3% in that time. Meanwhile, housing values have appreciated fastest in the most affluent regions during the
past three years, according to research by Fiserv CSW Inc., which tracks home prices.
Many economists say the lopsided recovery is now at a critical juncture. The impetus from new tax cuts has
largely passed, and the stock market has lost momentum, two factors that could slow the pace of
higher-income people's spending in the months ahead. As a result, the time has come for the recovery either
to broaden out to more-modest income groups -- or possibly lose momentum.
(MORE)
The late 1990s showed that lower-wage workers benefit when unemployment falls, as the tighter labor market
helps underpin wages across income categories. With the job market improving, there is a chance this could
happen again, but the outlook is still highly uncertain. Payroll employment has increased by 1.5 million since
last August. And some companies that cater to the mass market say they have noticed the turnaround. "We
had a terrific Fourth of July weekend," says Wayne Wielgus, a senior vice president at Choice Hotels
International Inc., which serves low- and middle-income travelers with brands like EconoLodge and Comfort
Inn.
But some economists worry that the early stage of the recovery for low- to middle-income families is being
squeezed by continuing pressure on wages and purchasing power. Average hourly earnings have risen at
just a 1.9% annual rate since the job market started improving notably last August. Meanwhile, the
consumer-price index -- driven by higher food and gasoline prices -- has risen at a 3.3% annual pace. The
average worker's purchasing power, in other words, has declined even as more people have been finding
jobs since August.
Weekly earnings for production workers and nonsupervisors at service companies, adjusted for inflation,
were down 2.6% in June from a year earlier. This slip might be transitory, and it wasn't anywhere near the
drops of 5% to 7.5% registered in the late 1970s and early 1980s. Still, it was the largest decline since 1991,
and it is a shift from the late 1990s and even the 2001 recession, when real wages were increasing.
As a result, after rising last year, the University of Michigan's consumer confidence index for lower-income
households is off 12% so far this year. Confidence among the affluent is lower as well, but by a smaller 6.7%.
The recovering job market and an easing of food- and gasoline-price increases could reverse some of today's
pressures. But these aren't the only issues hanging over lower-income households. Many are also highly
exposed to rising interest rates, says Mark Zandi, chief economist at Economy.com, because these
households were more likely to take out adjustable-rate mortgages to squeeze into an ever-pricier housing
market. For those who don't own homes, the chances of buying have become more remote as house prices
have soared. "Lower- and middle-income groups are going to remain under significant pressure," Mr. Zandi
says.
Many in this group are also getting squeezed as health-care costs rise and companies seek to shift the
burden to workers. From 2000 to 2003, employees' average annual out-of-pocket expenses for family medical
premiums rose 49% to $2,412, according to an employer survey by Kaiser Family Foundation, a nonprofit
research group in Menlo Park, Calif.
Becky Salas, a 32-year-old vocational nurse in San Antonio, says her family is still pinching pennies, even
though she believes an economic recovery is taking hold now. Two years ago, she and her husband stopped
using credit cards. Expensive toys for her children, movies at theaters and meals at McDonald's also are out.
"Easily we could spend $20 at McDonald's for just one meal," Ms. Salas says. And "we can go fly a kite,
instead of going to an expensive theater where the kids are going to yell and scream and won't enjoy it
anyway."

Curbing Foreign Investment --- Philippine Constitution Derails Development of Certain Sectors
International
Curbing Foreign Investment --- Philippine Constitution Derails Development of Certain Sectors
By James Hookway
994 words
19 July 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A9
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Tampakan, Philippines -- A TEAM OF FILIPINO and Australian miners struck gold several years ago near
this drowsy village high in the mountains of the southern Philippines. Better yet, they also found so much
copper that the site someday could become one of the largest copper mines in Asia.
But in January -- just days after President Gloria Macapagal Arroyo approved a plan to hasten development
of the country's mining industry -- Tampakan mine developer Sagittarius Mining Inc. was dealt an unexpected
blow: The Supreme Court said the project was illegal because it contravened the Philippines' constitution.
"We were shocked and stunned," says Tony Robbins, acting managing director of Sagittarius's Australian
partner, Indophil Resources NL. "Nobody knew what had happened."
What happened was that Indophil and its local partners discovered the hurdles to doing business in
developing countries that are shackled to revanchist economic regulation. In Latin America, for instance,
incoming governments frequently rewrite constitutions to micromanage economic and social policy. The
Philippines, meanwhile, is tied to a 1987 constitution written mainly by nationalist foes of former dictator
Ferdinand Marcos.
The charter explicitly excludes foreign investment from many sectors, while limiting foreign participation in
others. Over the years, that has enabled courts to intervene repeatedly for reasons the investors could hardly
have foreseen. In the 1990s, for example, Malaysian investors were prevented from redeveloping the Manila
Hotel -- World War II hotel headquarters of Gen. Douglas MacArthur -- because the Supreme Court ruled it
was part of the Philippines' national heritage; only Filipinos could manage and own the hotel.
Mining, in particular, has been curtailed. By some estimates, this chain of islands contains the world's
fifth-largest deposits of minerals. The government hoped to attract foreign investment in mining when a new
law passed in 1995, letting foreign companies own as much as 100% of Philippine mining ventures. But legal
challenges to the constitutionality of the 1995 law have stalled liberalization. And without foreign capital, many
potential sites remain untouched.
Philippine environmental campaigners have used the constitution to challenge the few mining projects that
get off the drawing board. Indeed, in 1996, after a toxic chemical spill at a separate majority Philippine-owned
mining venture, environmental lobbyists filed the Supreme Court suit that ultimately derailed the Tampakan
project.
The court's decision dismayed Philippine officials eager to attract foreign capital to stimulate the country's
wobbly economy. The country's central bank said foreign direct investment fell to $319 million in 2003 from
$1.8 billion a year earlier, as international investors began shunning the Philippines, partly because of its
unpredictable court decisions.
The government has formally appealed to the Supreme Court to reconsider its Tampakan decision. Lawyers
say Indophil holds 40% of Sagittarius's equity, which is in line with the constitutional limit for foreign stakes in
mining ventures. They also note that the constitution allows foreign financial or technical assistance in
developing mineral sites.
If the court changes its mind, Horacio Ramos, Philippines' Mines and Geosciences Bureau director, expects a
surge of new investment to expand annual mineral production to $4 billion within several years. That would
trail Indonesia; mining contributed about $18 billion to its economy in 2002. The Philippines annually mines
about $500 million of minerals -- mainly copper, nickel and chromium.
Ms. Arroyo, elected to a fresh six-year term in May, recognizes sluggish mining development is a drag on the
economy. She recently promised to try to amend the constitution to allow greater foreign participation in

--- Page 161 ---
Page 161 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
capital-intensive industries. But the last president who tried to amend the constitution, Joseph Estrada, faced
huge street protests when he tried to roll back legal curbs on foreign investment in 2000.
Framed after Mr. Marcos's fall in 1986, the current constitution was designed to prevent a rise of future
dictators. But the constitutional drafting committee also wrote in protectionist economic restrictions. Among
them: Foreign-owned factories can't own the land they are built on, and foreign investors can't own shares in
media businesses. In many industries, including mining, foreigners are limited to a 40% equity share.
Ms. Arroyo hasn't set a clear timetable for constitutional change. Some opposition politicians may choose to
support her. "There are so many things we can make easier for foreign investors, and amending aspects of
the constitution is one of them," says Sen. Edgardo Angara, a leading opposition lawmaker.
Investors, too, are concerned. "Banks can't fund projects with uncertain ownership, so investors can't get past
first base," says Robin Widdup, managing director of Lion Selection Group, based in Melbourne, Australia,
which has invested in mines around the world, including ventures in the Philippines.
Not all foreign-funded mining activity has halted in the Philippines. Australia's Lafayette Mining said last week
it had signed a contract to begin work at a combined gold-, copper-, zinc- and silver-mining site. It would be
the first foreign-funded mine to begin operations in the Philippines since 1968.
The biggest headache from the mining impasse may belong to the government and its hard-pressed
economic managers. Manila already has promised compensation to investors affected by the Supreme
Court's decision -- something the country's stretched coffers can ill-afford.
Mr. Ramos says that if the court upholds its Tampakan decision, it could adversely affect other exploration
and production contracts the government has signed. Chief among them, he says, is the Malampaya
natural-gas project in the South China Sea, developed and operated by the Royal Dutch/Shell Group and
ChevronTexaco Corp., which each own 45% stakes in the venture. The project provides one-third of the fuel
requirement for the Philippines' electric-power generators. A Shell official declined to comment on the issue.
"I was going to retire this year," says Mr. Ramos, a veteran in the Philippines' mineral industry. "But now it
looks like I will have to postpone my plans."

That Old Devil Inflation Lurks in the Shadows
Global View
That Old Devil Inflation Lurks in the Shadows
By George Melloan
1,053 words
13 July 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Friday will bring a new reading of the Consumer Price Index (CPI), telling Americans what the statisticians
believe is happening to the cost of living. But whatever the index shows, it will only confirm what intuition has
already conveyed to most of us. Americans sense what the cost of living is doing to them every time they pay
their monthly bills, stop at the gas pump or go through the supermarket check-out line.
The news, even in a statistical sense, hasn't been good in recent months. The latest year-on-year
comparison, for the three months ended in May, showed consumer prices rising at a troubling 5.5% annual
rate, the highest since 1990. A 5% inflation rate is not at all as negligible as it looks at first glance; it can
wipe out half the purchasing power of a dollar in 15 years.
Other warning signals are flying. Gold, that classical measure of a currency's worth, pushed above $400 an
ounce last week, well above its year-ago levels. The dollar remains weak against the euro and other
currencies. Real estate prices are going through the roof in some areas.
It was in the face of this that the Fed edged its key federal funds rate up a quarter of a percentage point on
June 30. Gradualism is the order of the day at the Fed and no doubt the governors are hoping that analysts
are correct in betting that Friday's figures will show a slower rise in the CPI. The economy slowed significantly
in June, which might well have curbed price rises.
But is the Fed -- or the market, for that matter -- reading the tea leaves correctly? And did the Fed act too
little and too late with its puny rate increase two weeks ago?
One of the jobs of a central bank is to try to stamp out the "inflation expectations" that have a way of
insinuating themselves into the human psyche. Once they take hold, they are pro-cyclical, as the economists
say. That means they give further impetus to the price increases. People switch from saving to spending, to
beat higher costs, thus artificially expanding demand and pushing prices up even faster. Investors shy away
from long-term obligations out of fear that the value and returns of their investments will be wiped out by the
currency's declining value.
Such a psychology may be fueling the rapid rise in home sales, as buyers try to lock in low mortgage rates
before they go up. That demand of course factors into prices as well, as buyers swallow higher home costs
because the rates for mortgage financing are so low.
If the Fed has a tendency to err on the side of inflation, it is partly because it has what is called a "dual
mandate" from Congress. It is supposed to be concerned not only about price stability, but also about
employment. At a time when prices appear to be under control, it has a tendency to pump liquidity into the
economy to try to stimulate economic growth. That's pretty much where the Fed was last winter, when
Democrats and the media were tub-thumping about a "jobless recovery." That the seemingly low job growth
was largely a statistical mirage didn't alter the politics.
What seemed prudent last winter looks less so today. There is a lag between the creation of liquidity and the
time when excess liquidity, if it exists, shows up in consumer prices. Economists variously estimate how long
it takes, and indeed the lags may vary. But quite likely the spring bump upwards of the CPI reflected the
monetary policies of months before. Thus, the Fed's decision on a modest tightening June 30.
It can be said on the Fed's behalf that it achieved the second part of its mandate, job growth. Or it seems that
way. In the first half of the year, over a million new jobs were created, and despite the slowdown in June,
economists are predicting a similar pace through the rest of year. If those predictions prove out, it will of
course be good news for George W. Bush. Presidents feel they have a long-standing entitlement to take
credit for a strong economy, whatever the reason. And indeed, Mr. Bush's tax cuts may well have been a
bigger factor in generating the recovery than the Fed's easy money policy.

--- Page 167 ---
Page 167 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But if the Fed has misjudged on inflation, that would of course be bad news for Mr. Bush. If rising prices are
taking a deeper bite out of American paychecks in November, the Kerry-Edwards team will be perfectly
capable of turning that into an election issue. Presidents get the credit for bad news, too.
Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan is well aware of the dangers ahead as he tries to steer monetary policy
away from the inflation rocks without crashing the economic recovery. Using a golfing term, he has
commented that it won't be a "gimme putt." The CPI numbers this week will be a key indicator of whether the
Fed will raise the fed funds rate another notch in early August. They may also indicate whether the Fed erred
in not being vigorous enough with the June 30 increase, which seems to have had little effect on any sector of
the real economy.
Despite the stellar Greenspan record dating back to the Reagan era, few central bankers are gifted with
infallibility. Some of the Fed governors, judging from their writings and speeches, seem to be remarkably
nonchalant about inflation. Indeed, some apparently still think there is such a thing as a Phillips Curve -- that
hoary theory, discredited by the stagflation of the 1970s, that inflation is a necessary adjunct to full
employment.
Fortunately, Chairman Greenspan has been mostly successful in keeping such ideas from guiding Fed policy.
He has focused, as any good central banker should, on keeping the currency as stable as it is possible to do
when you are managing something of no more substance than pieces of paper that supposedly represent
value. The process works as long as the holders of those bills believe in their worth. But once they lose that
confidence, watch out!

Page 168 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Dow Industrials Edge Up, but Chip Stocks Hurt Nasdaq
Monday's Markets
Dow Industrials Edge Up, but Chip Stocks Hurt Nasdaq
By E.S. Browning
454 words
13 July 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A GLOOMY Merrill Lynch report sent computer-chip stocks to a two-month low, but falling oil prices gave
other stocks a slight boost.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average edged up 25 points, or 0.25%, to 10238.22, still down 2% on the year.
Amid jitters about second-quarter earnings news due in the next two weeks, New York Stock Exchange
volume was the year's third-lightest, 1.11 billion shares.
Worries about soft earnings and slowing economic growth hurt stocks, but are making bond investors hope
that the Federal Reserve will hold back on interest-rate increases. Talk is spreading that the Fed might leave
rates alone at its next policy meeting, in August. Treasury bonds rose in value, and the yield of the 10-year
Treasury note, which falls when the price rises, fell to 4.449%, the lowest since April.
Stocks slumped after Merrill Lynch cut its investment rating for chip makers to underweight, citing soft
demand.
Oil prices, which had pushed above $40 a barrel in the morning, sagged to $39.50 in midafternoon, which
helped stocks. Traders cited a late-day decline in gasoline prices and news that the Bush administration
wants to open national forests to more commercial activity, which they thought could include oil drilling.
"We were really in the doldrums and the oil news really turned the market around," said Todd Leone, head of
listed trading at brokerage house SG Cowen.
The chip sector still declined, pulling technology stocks lower, but the oil news narrowed the decline. The
Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.48%, or 9.41 points, to 1936.92, off more than 3% this year.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, which like the Dow was down for most of the session, finished
ahead 0.14%, or 1.54 points, at 1114.35, once again up for the year -- by a slim 0.22%.
Gold rose to a three-month high of $408.10 an ounce.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.19%, or 0.28 point, to 150.22.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the Big Board, 1,852 stocks rose and 1,413 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 4/32, or $1.25 for each $1,000 invested. The 30-year bond
was up 6/32 to yield 5.201%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 108.25 yen, down from 108.33, while the euro weakened
against the dollar to $1.2410 from $1.2412.

American Way: Some Take It, Some Leave It --- Fannie Says U.S. Housing Finance Is Program Envied by the World; Italy, Among Others, Sees...
American Way: Some Take It, Some Leave It --- Fannie Says U.S. Housing Finance Is Program Envied
by the World; Italy, Among Others, Sees Flaws
By James R. Hagerty
1,348 words
12 July 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Brussels -- EXECUTIVES AT U.S. mortgage giant Fannie Mae like to tell their critics that the American
method of financing home loans is the "envy of the world."
That would be news to Bruno De Gasperis, head of the international department at the Italian Bankers
Association in Rome, who attended a conference of housing officials and mortgage lenders from around the
world here last month. He describes America's housing-finance system -- built around the
government-backed companies Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac -- as "an old-fashioned approach."
For years, Fannie and Freddie were seen as a global gold standard. Fannie has advised dozens of countries
on mortgage finance, in places such as Hong Kong and Turkey. But housing officials and bankers outside the
U.S. more recently have tuned into the American political squabble over how to tighten regulation of Fannie
and Freddie. Some are concluding that U.S. backing for Fannie and Freddie is an unjustified government
subsidy, largely enjoyed by people who don't need it, and a potential time bomb. If Fannie or Freddie ever
blows up, U.S. taxpayers probably would have to pay for the wreckage.
"That's not a contingent liability that the British taxpayer wants to take on," sniffs Adrian Coles, director
general of Britain's Building Societies Association, representing mutually owned savings institutions. The
British Treasury recently asked a university professor, David Miles, to study that country's mortgage market.
Among his conclusions: There is no need for a British Fannie Mae.
Colombia and Mexico are setting up programs to encourage the flow of funds into mortgage lending, but both
are deliberately limiting the involvement of government agencies. Officials of the European Union have given
a cool reception to a proposal that they should set up a European version of Fannie Mae. At the conference
here, the home-loan system that received the most praise was that of Denmark.
None of this means America has lost all bragging rights in the housing-finance realm. Many countries would
like to adopt at least certain aspects of the American system of channeling funds from the bond markets into
home loans. The highly automated U.S. mortgage industry, which processed $3.8 trillion of new mortgages
last year, has helped push the country's homeownership rate to nearly 69% from about 64% two decades
ago. The Bush administration wants to push the rate still higher because, it says, homeownership makes
neighborhoods more stable and allows low-income people to amass wealth.
Fannie and Freddie are owned by private shareholders but benefit from privileges granted by their
Congressional charters. Because investors see them as backed by the U.S. government, the two companies
can borrow money cheaply in the so-called agency bond market. They use those funds to buy mortgages
from banks and other lenders, which then can afford to make more loans.
One flaw in the U.S. system is that Fannie and Freddie serve masters with conflicting agendas. Their
shareholders expect them to churn out ever-larger profits; the government wants them to focus on financing
homes for poor people, even if that hurts profitability.
South Korea has borrowed heavily from the U.S. model but rejected the mixture of private owners and
public-policy duties. Korea Housing Finance Corp., established in March to develop bond-market financing for
home loans, is entirely state-owned. That was the original U.S. model: Fannie Mae was created in 1938 as a
government agency and partially privatized 30 years later.
In Mexico, a state-owned company, Sociedad Hipotecaria Federal, or SHF, recently began encouraging
"securitization," the bundling of home loans into securities that can be sold to investors. SHF will provide
limited guarantees, when needed, to boost the ratings on mortgage-backed securities to triple-A. Unlike
Fannie and Freddie, it won't buy large amounts of mortgages or mortgage securities for its own portfolio.
Holding a large portfolio of mortgages is risky because their value can fluctuate wildly with swings in interest

--- Page 172 ---
Page 172 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
rates. "The Mexican government cannot allow itself to have such a large contingent liability," says Guillermo
Babatz Torres, director general of SHF.
Colombia's government is limiting its role in mortgage finance to providing guarantees for mortgage-backed
securities that provide financing for low-income borrowers. The country is relying on a new private-sector
company to acquire mortgage loans and turn them into securities. One advantage of the Colombian plan: It
didn't require the creation of a government agency that might be difficult to close down if it outlives its original
purpose.
In Italy, the bankers association is setting up a privately owned computer service that would help small banks
sell mortgage loans to bigger ones. That would free the small lenders to make more loans than they can hold
on their books.
The European Union, whose 25 members have a mishmash of mortgage systems, is studying how it might
create more harmony in this area. Rob Thomas, an independent British economist who formerly worked for
UBS AG and the Bank of England, has drawn up a proposal for the EU to back an agency loosely based on
Fannie Mae. But Mr. Thomas says the agency should be owned by the mortgage banks that use its services,
not by investors seeking the highest possible return on their money. Under the control of its customers, he
reasons, "this institution wouldn't get too big for its boots."
Mr. Thomas admits that EU officials so far have shown little enthusiasm for his ideas. The European
Mortgage Federation, an organization of mortgage bankers, has rejected Mr. Thomas's plan as an
unwarranted "distortion" of competition.
Fannie Mae officials concede that no system is right for every country. But they say some of the European
resistance to the U.S. model comes from bankers who fear it would mean tougher competition.
Could Denmark be the model? It has a long track record: The country's first specialized mortgage bank was
founded in 1797, two years after a fire destroyed much of Copenhagen, creating an acute need for loans to
finance new homes.
Today, Danish mortgage banks obtain money for lending by issuing bonds whose terms correspond to those
of the home loans. For instance, if a bank makes a 30-year mortgage loan of two million kroner (about
$333,000), it typically will sell the same amount of 30-year bonds. If the loan is repaid early, the bonds will be
paid back early, too. This method helps the banks keep their cost of funds closely in line with the interest
payments they receive from borrowers. The Danish government's role is confined to regulating the market.
Soula Proxenos, who heads a small team at Fannie Mae that offers advice to other nations for a fee,
describes the Danish model as "ingenious." But she says it would be hard for developing countries, lacking
Denmark's legal structure and thriving bond market, to emulate. She adds that plenty of foreigners still are
eager to learn about the U.S. system. "In the housing-finance space," Ms. Proxenos says, "there is no
question that the U.S. is in the lead."
---

The U.S. has by far the largest residential mortgage market in the world,
with nearly $8 trillion in loans outstanding. But Spain and Ireland have
higher homeownership rates, partly reflecting cultural norms and land

CVRD Has Investors Fretting --- Acquisition Would Risk Exposure to Commodities' Fluctuations
World Stock Markets
CVRD Has Investors Fretting --- Acquisition Would Risk Exposure to Commodities' Fluctuations
By Geraldo Samor
1,102 words
9 July 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Rio de Janeiro -- OLD BRAZILIAN soccer wisdom says that the coach should never change a winning team.
So when word leaked that Brazil's Cia. Vale do Rio Doce, better known as CVRD, was considering a bid for
Canada's Noranda Inc., investors cried foul, sending the Brazilian stock down sharply. CVRD, the world's
largest iron-ore company and one of the most widely held emerging-market stocks, is off some 21% year to
date.
What scares them: the prospect that CVRD will transform itself from a stable, pure iron-ore play into a
diversified metals company subject to the whims and fluctuations of the commodities markets. While iron-ore
prices are hashed out at yearly negotiations between mining companies and buyers, metal prices are traded
daily on commodities exchanges.
For the past few years, investors have been buying CVRD shares -- which trade on the New York Stock
Exchange as American depositary receipts -- to seize on Asia's growing demand for iron ore and steel
products. As the price of iron ore soared, CVRD doubled in price over the previous year to reach a record
market value of $23.4 billion in early January. The stock has since fallen to around $48 from $62, in part on
concern that a successful bid for Noranda would make CVRD too much like slower-growth mining companies
such as Anglo-Australian conglomerate BHP Billiton or London-based Rio Tinto PLC.
"We love CVRD as it is," says Jean van de Walle, who oversees Latin American equities at Alliance Capital in
New York. "It is such a nice, clean play. They are the filet mignon of the [mining] business right now."
CVRD and at least two other mining companies have made bids for part or all of Noranda, in which Canadian
asset-management company Brascan Corp. has a 42% stake. According to news reports, the other bidders
are Anglo-Swiss mining company Xstrata PLC and state-owned China Minmetals Nonferrous Metals Co.
Analysts' most conservative estimates pin Noranda's price tag at around $3 billion for the Brascan stake
alone. A bid for the whole company could run higher than $5 billion.
CVRD has declined to comment on its bid.
Defenders of the planned acquisition say their case is clear-cut. Buying Noranda would allow CVRD to
diversify its portfolio of metals in one fell swoop. The company still derives two-thirds of its revenue from iron
ore, while Noranda is big on copper, nickel, aluminum and zinc. And by having more of its revenue in a
foreign currency, CVRD would be able to pay lower interest rates on its bank loans and bond issues.
But diversification isn't important in and of itself, argues Marcelo Kayath, head of Latin American equities at
Credit Suisse First Boston. Mr. Kayath says investors can do their own diversification, buying shares of
different mining companies, instead of having CVRD management do it for them.
CVRD is already diversifying with projects of its own. Just last week, the company inaugurated the first of its
five copper mines, a $400 million investment. Copper production generally has gold as a byproduct. The
company is also looking into exploring a nickel mine in the north of Brazil and is investing some $850 million
through 2006 to expand its production of bauxite and alumina -- raw materials for aluminum.
Then there is the timing issue. With commodity prices near an all-time high and China trying to slow down its
economy, there are more investors who think the commodity rally is over for the time being than those who
think otherwise. That means CVRD could be writing a big check for Noranda -- and be in for a major
disappointment in the medium term.
A bid for Noranda, and the challenges of integration if the bid succeeds, would be a major test for Roger
Agnelli, CVRD's energetic, politically savvy chief executive officer. Investors are wary of a deal because the
45-year-old Mr. Agnelli is untested in acquisitions of this magnitude.

--- Page 175 ---
Page 175 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Since he became CEO in July 2001, Mr. Agnelli has spent $2.36 billion on a number of acquisitions and sold
assets worth $1.52 billion.
Some investors worry that Mr. Agnelli likes the excitement of big deals and may lose the discipline he has
demonstrated so far. These investors hope that a bidding war will break out and that CVRD will ultimately
lose.
---

Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index fell 0.46%, or 0.87, to 189.63. Excluding the U.S., the index rose
0.03%, or 0.05, to 149.43.
In TOKYO, the Nikkei Stock Average ended down 0.6%, or 62.63 points, at 11322.23. NTT DoCoMo rose
0.5% to 189,000 yen on news it obtained the largest number of net new subscribers among mobile-phone
companies in June, beating rival KDDI, which had led user growth in recent months. KDDI fell 1.6% to
600,000 yen.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 Index advanced 0.5%, or 22.7 points, to 4381.1. Diageo, the world's largest liquor
group, fell 1.7% to 712.5 pence after the company said trading in the second half of its financial year was
broadly in line with expectations.
In FRANKFURT, the Xetra DAX Index gained 0.1%, or 3.90 points, to 3934.48. RWE climbed 2.6% to 40.80

In TORONTO, stocks fell in lackluster trading. Industrials fell sharply as railroads were hit by a bearish report
on the industry from Credit Suisse First Boston. Canadian Pacific Railway fell 0.75 to 32.48 Canadian dollars
and Canadian National Railway lost 1.63 to 55.70.

Taste -- Houses of Worship: Christian Teens? Not Very
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Taste -- Houses of Worship: Christian Teens? Not Very
By Dale Buss
864 words
9 July 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
W13
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
When I'm teaching Sunday school, I'm encouraged by what I hear from the teenagers at my evangelical
Christian church in suburban Detroit. They seem to understand -- and, more important, to believe -- the
bedrock tenets that will help them hew to orthodoxy throughout their lives and make them salt and light in the
world.
But the hard numbers say otherwise. It turns out that, while they may profess the faith and indeed love Jesus,
the vast majority of Christian teenagers in this country actually hold beliefs fundamentally antithetical to the
creed. The forces of moral relativism and "tolerance" have gotten to them in a big way. In fact, some leaders
believe that mushy doctrine among the younger generation ranks as the No. 1 crisis facing American
Christendom today.
About one-third of American teenagers claim they're "born again" believers, according to data gathered over
the past few years by Barna Research Group, the gold standard in data about the U.S. Protestant church,
and 88% of teens say they are Christians. About 60% believe that "the Bible is totally accurate in all of its
teachings." And 56% feel that their religious faith is very important in their life.
Yet, Barna says, slightly more than half of all U.S. teens also believe that Jesus committed sins while he was
on earth. About 60% agree that enough good works will earn them a place in heaven, in part reflecting a
Catholic view, but also flouting Protestantism's central theme of salvation only by grace. About two-thirds say
that Satan is just a symbol of evil, not really a living being. Only 6% of all teens believe that there are moral
absolutes -- and, most troubling to evangelical leaders, only 9% of self-described born-again teens believe
that moral truth is absolute.
"When you ask even Christian kids, `How can you say A is true as well as B, which is the antithesis of A?',
their typical response is, `I'm not sure how it works, but it works for me,'" says George Barna, president of the
Ventura, Calif.-based research company. "It's personal, pragmatic and fairly superficial."
Some commentators produce even more startling statistics on the doctrinal drift of America's youth.
Ninety-one percent of born-again teenagers surveyed a few years ago proclaimed that there is no such thing
as absolute truth, says the Rev. Josh McDowell, a Dallas-based evangelist and author. More alarmingly, that
number had risen quickly and steadily from just 52% of committed Christian kids in 1992 who denied the
existence of absolute truth. A slight majority of professing Christian kids, Mr. McDowell says, also now say
that the bodily resurrection of Jesus Christ never occurred.
"There's a greater disconnect now than ever in the history of the church in America between what a Christian
young person says they are and what they actually believe," says Mr. McDowell, who has ministered mainly
to youth for more than 30 years. "Christianity is based on truth; Jesus said, `I am the truth.' But you have an
overwhelming majority even of Christian kids saying there is no absolute truth."
Catholics have noticed the trend as well. A few weeks ago, in fact, Pope John Paul II specifically warned
several U.S. bishops about the "soulless vision of life" that seemed to be overtaking America, urging them to
"confront directly the widespread spirit of agnosticism and relativism which has cast doubt on reason's ability
to know the truth," especially among youth.
Indeed, the consequences of this theological implosion now pervade the thoughts and actions of believing
teenagers, following the moral breakdown of the broader American culture. Here's one practical example:
Only 10% of Christian teens believe that music piracy is morally wrong, according to a recent Barna survey,
not all that different from the 6% of their non-Christian peers who feel the same way.
Then extrapolate the situation to other possible big-picture results. Nearly 60% of evangelical Christian
teenagers now say that all religious faiths teach equally valid truths, according to Mr. McDowell. It's bad

--- Page 177 ---
Page 177 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
enough that they seem to have been co-opted by relativism from within our culture and even from within the
church and family. But it's even more disconcerting to realize that we're relying on this generation for the
future defense of Judeo-Christian civilization against the highly motivated forces of militant Islam.
Perhaps it's counterintuitive to believe this problem is as severe as that outlined by Messrs. Barna and
McDowell. After all, we're told that spirituality is de rigueur among youths these days and that Christianity is
right up there. But this zeitgeist largely reflects a pseudo-faith that is fed by a steady diet of pop-culture feints,
from the allegorical "Lord of the Rings" movies to the T-shirt that recently adorned Pamela Anderson saying,
"Jesus is my homeboy."
The kids in my Sunday School class really do understand that. It's their peers I'm worried about.
---
Mr. Buss is a journalist and author in Rochester Hills, Mich.

Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / Street Sleuth: Gold Bugs No Longer Fear the Fed --- Worries of Attractive Dollar Choking Off...
Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / Street Sleuth: Gold Bugs No Longer Fear the Fed ---
Worries of Attractive Dollar Choking Off Metal's Gains Appear to Have Relaxed
By Peter A. McKay
679 words
8 July 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
GOLD INVESTORS don't have a reputation as a daring bunch, generally speaking.
But analysts say there's been a widening streak of boldness lately, exemplified by yesterday's jump above
$400 a troy ounce -- a psychologically important level that the yellow metal had failed to hold several times.
It could mean that gold bugs are no longer spooked about the prospect of quickly rising interest rates, which
usually are a drain on the metal's price. Gold fell from its recent high near $430 a troy ounce in early April, for
example, because a strong employment report stoked fears of inflation and sharp rate increases by the
Federal Reserve.
Since that time, however, it seems gold bugs have not only accepted the Fed's stated policy of gradual
increases, but also decided that even a modestly tighter money supply doesn't have to hurt gold prices.
Some are predicting a rare scenario: a rise in both gold prices and the dollar through the end of the year.
Usually, the two move in opposite directions, as investors flock to gold as a haven when they're nervous
about the value of paper assets, including currency.
"Understanding gold right now is not so simple as using the usual comparison to the dollar," said MG
Financial Group currency analyst Ashraf Laidi. He expects both gold and the dollar essentially to break even
this year, with the metal ending between $415 and $420, and the currency between $1.27 and $1.28 against
the euro.
In order for that scenario to come true, though, both gold and the dollar must rise 3% to 5% from their current
price levels.
Mr. Laidi and others believe that's possible because foreign-exchange markets are digesting an unusual --
and somewhat contradictory -- mix of news lately. On the one hand, there's the Fed's rate-raising, which
would tend to drive up the value of dollars by, in effect, taking more of them out of circulation.
On the other hand, new economic data point toward just enough pitfalls to keep the dollar relatively weak. For
instance, after a government report on service-sector activity in June was unexpectedly tepid yesterday, the
dollar hit its lowest price in more than three months against the euro, at $1.2369 in New York trading.
The dollar's drop helped gold futures leap $9.70, or 2.5%, to $402.70 a troy ounce, the highest level in more
than a week on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
James Steel, New York-based head of research at the commodity brokerage Refco, says gold buyers now
see the Federal Reserve's gradual approach to raising rates as "tepid," hence they're again willing to buy the
metal at the first sign of dollar weakness.
He and other experts say the presidential election is also helping gold in the sense that it adds uncertainty to
financial markets and hence might be driving some investors to seek havens like gold. "This is the second
straight election we've had where the outcome is really up in the air going into the final stages," Mr. Steel
said. "If you go back through Clinton's two victories, and Reagan and Bush in the 1980s, we really haven't
had a string of close elections like this in a while."
Michael Cuggino, president of Pacific Heights Asset Management, says he expects a moderate decline in the
dollar through the end of the year, but a sharp rise in gold back to its high around $430, at least.
"The fear of the dollar becoming so attractive to investors that it would choke off gold is greatly reduced right
now," said Mr. Cuggino, whose firm advises the commodity-focused Permanent Portfolio Fund. "We can see
now that, even with higher rates, there are a lot of dollars floating around out there."

NEW YORK -- Home builders helped small-capitalization stocks nail down gains after the Federal Reserve
took action.
In broad buying, interest-rate sensitive home builders were among the session's strongest industry
performers after the Fed raised its benchmark federal-funds rate by one-quarter percentage point, as
expected. Beazer Homes USA (NYSE) advanced $2.15, or 2.2%, to $100.31, and Champion Enterprises
(NYSE) gained 14 cents, or 1.6%, to 9.18.
The rate increase had already been factored into stock prices. But after weeks of anticipation, "investors are
very relieved to get this behind them," said Matt Ruane, director of U.S. equities and sales trading at Calyon
Securities.
The Russell 2000 Index gained 3.69 points, or 0.63%, to 591.52, and the S&P SmallCap 600 Index rose 2.02,
or 0.69%, to 296.35, surpassing the previous record set Tuesday. The Nasdaq Composite Index of large-cap
and small-cap stocks advanced 12.86, or 0.63%, to 2047.79.
Yesterday also marked the end of the second quarter, which left the Russell 2000 up 1.21 points, or 0.2%.
For its part, the S&P SmallCap 600 jumped 9.69, or 3.4%. The Nasdaq climbed 53.57, or 2.7%.

Gold Futures Lose A Bit of Luster Before Fed Move
Commodities Report
Gold Futures Lose A Bit of Luster Before Fed Move
By Gavin Maguire OsterDowJones Commodity News
346 words
30 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold futures on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange buckled more than
2% on selling by funds and bullion banks spurred by the firmer posture of the U.S. dollar ahead of today's
Federal Reserve decision on interest rates.
The most-active August contract dropped by $8.50, or 2.1%, to $392.80 a troy ounce.
Preplaced, stop-loss sell orders around $394.30 also paved the way lower by automatically adding to
sell-side volumes, and traders agreed additional downside pressure couldn't be ruled out over the next few
days.
George Gero, a senior vice president at Legg Mason Wood Walker, said that in the wake of the recent retreat
in oil prices and early handover of power in Iraq, both the geopolitical risk of further terrorist attacks and
hedge-fund appetite for commodities in general have diminished.
With U.S. interest rates widely expected to be boosted by 0.25 percentage point today, gold's appeal as an
alternative to the U.S. dollar also has been slightly tarnished, turning its near-term bias decidedly negative.
However, given that the geopolitical landscape remains murky and the dollar still must bear the burden of
huge budget and trade deficits, a sustained selling spree in gold is considered unlikely any time soon.
Downside targets in that event would include the $382 and $380 levels, he added.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures on Nymex fell to 11-week lows as speculators liquidated previous bets that prices would
rise. August futures fell 58 cents to $35.66 a barrel amid easing supply concerns, after curtailed output in Iraq
and Norway returned to normal over the weekend.
COPPER: Futures of high-grade copper on the Comex division of Nymex fell sharply after a build in London
metal exchange inventories and a stronger U.S. dollar helped trigger preplaced orders to sell. The September
contract fell 3.55 cents to $1.175 a pound.
