Global Markets Await Action by U.S. Fed --- Investors' Hearts Are Divided Over Good Earnings Prospects, Expected Higher Interest Rates
Tuesday's Markets
Global Markets Await Action by U.S. Fed --- Investors' Hearts Are Divided Over Good Earnings
Prospects, Expected Higher Interest Rates
By E.S. Browning
976 words
30 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS ARE TRYING to put on a brave face as they prepare to take their interest-rate medicine today.
With the Federal Reserve expected to raise interest rates for the first time in four years, the Dow Jones
Industrial Average yesterday managed a gain of 56.34 points, or 0.54%, to 10413.43. Bond prices also rose
ahead of the Fed news.
Investors find themselves struggling with their emotions, trying to decide whether to feel upbeat at the
prospect of strong second-quarter earnings due out next month or downcast at the prospect of an expected
series of Fed rate increases. Investors fear the increases could last for a year or more as the Fed moves to
rein in incipient inflation.
As it stands now, people are caught in the middle, unsure whether the bull market will withstand the rate
increases or slowly crumble as the most stimulative government interest-rate policy in most people's adult
lives comes to an end. The Fed's easy-money policy fueled the bull market at the end of 2002 and throughout
last year, but uncertainty about the Fed's intentions has left the Dow industrials virtually unchanged, not only
for the current quarter but for the year to date.
"The market is getting more comfortable with the certainty that rates are going to rise. The Fed has done a
good job of forecasting its intention pretty clearly," said Andy Brooks, head of stock trading at Baltimore
mutual-fund firm T. Rowe Price. But people remain unsure about inflation, the extent of the coming rate
increases and the depth of international instability, he added. That has offset people's pleasure at the strong
corporate earnings that the low interest rates have helped produce.
Stocks and bonds both fell Monday, in advance of the two-day Fed interest-rate meeting that ends today.
Yesterday, they reversed course and rose.
Stocks got a boost from news that the Conference Board's consumer-confidence index this month jumped to
its highest level in two years, reinforcing strong consumer-sentiment numbers reported by a rival University of
Michigan index last week.
In the absence of major new international strife, crude oil fell to a three-month low of $35.66 a barrel on the
New York Mercantile Exchange. Gold also fell. The Dow Jones Transportation Average hit a fresh five-year
high of 3184.71.
The Fed's announcement, scheduled for 2:15 p.m. EDT in Washington, is almost universally expected to
involve a quarter-point increase in the Fed's target for the federal-funds rate to 1.25%. The rate, at which
banks lend one another money overnight, has been at a 46-year low of 1% for the past year. Though some
investors fear a half-point increase to 1.5%, that now is considered unlikely. How investors react to the Fed
news will depend largely on what hints the Fed drops in its post-meeting statement about how much and how
fast it will raise rates in the future.
The steady decline in Fed rates, which began at the start of 2001, has helped pull the U.S. economy out of
recession, becoming one of the biggest underpinnings of the bull market that began in October 2002. Even
after the expected boost today, rates will remain far below their levels of the late 1990s, and the Fed's target
rate still will be well below zero when adjusted for inflation, which currently is running at an annual rate of
more than 3%.
Though the reaction among bond investors has been milder than feared, the prospect of rising rates is worse
news for bond investors than for stock investors.

--- Page 189 ---
Page 189 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The yield of the 10-year Treasury bond began the quarter at just 3.8% and rose to almost 4.9% on June 14
before stabilizing. Yesterday, as stocks rose, Treasurys also rose. The yield of the 10-year bond, which falls
when the price rises, pulled back to 4.686%.
Many bond investors worry that the interest-rate increases, known in the trade as a "tightening cycle," could
be a lengthy affair. Higher rates are much worse for Treasurys than for stocks, because stocks react both to
corporate profits and to rates, while bonds depend almost entirely on interest rates. Rising rates hurt bond
prices because they make new bonds, with their higher rates, more attractive than old ones.
"History suggests that the Fed is embarking on a tightening cycle of somewhere between one and two years'
duration," David Kotok, president of money-management firm Cumberland Advisors in Vineland, N.J., wrote
in a note to clients. The 10-year yield could go as high as 5.5% -- or even 6% if inflation forces the Fed into
an aggressive stance, Mr. Kotok wrote.
Yesterday, the broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.25%, or 2.85 points, to 1136.20, leaving it up
2% on the year. The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.75%, or 15.11 points, to 2034.93, up 1.6% this year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.37 billion shares traded, 1,713 stocks rose and 1,587 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury bond rose 15/32 point, or $4.6875 for each $1,000 invested. The
30-year bond rose 22/32 point to yield 5.368%.
The dollar strengthened. It traded at 108.37 yen, up from 107.90, while the euro weakened to $1.2083 from
$1.2186.

Borrowing Costs to Feel Impact" -- WSJ June 30, 2004)

`Gradual' Greenspan
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
`Gradual' Greenspan
729 words
30 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A8
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Senate recently whisked Alan Greenspan through confirmation for his fifth term as Federal Reserve
Chairman, with a mere single vote opposed (from Kentucky Republican Jim Bunning). We will find out in the
next few months how well he deserves that bipartisan vote of confidence.
Mr. Greenspan's era at the Fed has been remarkable in many ways, and not only in length and general
prosperity. He has also had to manage his way through one stock market crash, the 1990s boom followed by
a burst investment bubble, and in recent years an epic gamble to recover from that bubble. How the latter
turns out may well determine how he goes down in Fed history.
The Greenspan gamble has been to promote a highly accommodative monetary policy for a notably long
period. His bet was that, with corporate spending having collapsed with the burst bubble and 9/11, historically
low interest rates would prop up the housing market and help the American consumer stave off a deeper
recession. There's no denying this has worked so far; the question is whether he and his Fed partners have
waited too long to blow the whistle and thus allowed inflation to revive with more trouble down the road.
Certainly the price increases of recent months seem to have caught the Fed by surprise. It wasn't long ago
that the Governors were still fretting about "deflation," even as gold, commodity and other industrial prices
were heading up.
Even more recently, Governor Ben Bernanke assured the world that something called the "output gap,"
essentially the amount of unused economic capacity, guaranteed that inflation was nothing to worry about.
Fed officials whispered that rates wouldn't rise until business hiring did, even as they underestimated the
impact of the tax cuts in spurring growth. The same Fed that managed a decline in inflation over a 20-year
period was suddenly worrying about everything except maintaining price stability.
At last, today, the Fed is expected to begin raising short-term rates from their 46-year low of 1%. But even
now the Fed mantra is "gradualism," which means that everyone expects an increase of only 25 basis points.
The argument is that a gradual rise will give markets and investors a chance to adjust, there won't be any big
surprises (a la Orange County's bust after 1994), and of course the politicians won't get too upset in an
election year. Perhaps.
The danger with gradualism, however, is that it risks always being behind the inflation curve. A steady,
gradual rise may also have the perverse effect of speeding up investment decisions as investors try to lock in
rates ahead of future "gradual" increases. This has certainly been happening in some housing markets, as
buyers bid up prices to even greater heights before mortgage rates go up. The risk is that when rates finally
do get high enough, the plunge back to earth will be all the more severe.
Mr. Greenspan has been saying there is no need to worry, and we certainly hope he's right. On the other
hand, recent price increases aren't an illusion. The personal consumption expenditure (PCE) deflator rose
0.5% in May, the biggest leap in four years. While most of that was due to energy prices, core inflation
seems to be climbing at a faster pace than the 2% rate that some Fed officials say is their target. Real
interest rates are "negative," meaning they are below the level of inflation. In this kind of environment, we'd
say the Fed's most important obligation is to regain its price-stability credentials with financial markets.
The current economic expansion looks durable enough to survive any realistic rate hikes. The incentive
effects of the Bush tax cut remain in place, and both business and consumer confidence are strong. A larger
Fed move earlier might even cause a reduction in longer-term interest rates, as investors begin to recover
their faith in Mr. Greenspan's inflation-fighting credibility. Periods of rising rates historically come with
financial casualties, but the number and size of those casualties tend to increase the longer the Fed waits to
act.

--- Page 193 ---
Page 193 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
After 17 years in his job, Mr. Greenspan well knows that every central banker can cut interest rates. The
great ones know when and how much to raise them.

Honeywell, 3M and Altria Climb, While Washington Mutual Falls

NEW YORK -- As the market rose ahead of today's anticipated interest-rate increase, the Dow Jones
Industrial Average was assisted by smokestack stocks including 3M and Honeywell International.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average gained 56.34 points, or 0.54%, to 10413.43, the Nasdaq Composite Index
rose 15.11 points, or 0.75%, to 2034.93 and the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index advanced 2.85 points, or

"The market has already discounted the Fed's interest-rate decision 23 times," said Alfred Kugel, senior
investment strategist at Stein Roe &Farnham, "so people are moving on to the next thing, second-quarter
earnings, and I think they're going to be good."
The industrial average's best performers included 3M, rising $1.11, or 1.3%, to $89.80, and Honeywell,
gaining 31 cents, or almost 1%, to 36.65. Lehman Brothers raised its price targets for the diversified
manufacturers, saying there may be more steam to the industrial recovery than originally thought.
Dow industrial component Altria Group rose 62 cents, or 1.3%, to 50.22. The parent of Philip Morris USA
continued to benefit from word on Monday that a federal court judge cleared the way for cigarette makers to
request an immediate appeal of a ruling in the government's racketeering case. Fellow cigarette maker R.J.
Reynolds advanced 47 cents to 67.32.
Dow component J.P. Morgan Chase gained 34 cents to 38.29, while Commerce Bancorp shed 3.31, or 5.1%,
to 61.15. Former employees of J.P. Morgan and two currently suspended officers at Commerce were among
those indicted yesterday on charges stemming from a federal probe of alleged corruption in Philadelphia city
government.
There were developments for other banking companies, with Washington Mutual dropping 2.84, or 6.9%, to
38.47, after lowering 2004 earnings guidance, saying higher interest rates have cut its mortgage production
outlook.
Some other home lenders appeared to be affected; with Countrywide Financial falling 2.18, or 3.1%, to 69.32
and Astoria Financial dropping 76 cents, or 2.1%, to 36.38.
Retailers were shaken as Target said June same-store sales will likely come in "well below" expectations.

warning Monday, shed 48 cents, or almost 1%, to 51.98, making it the worst performer among the Dow
industrials.
Nortel Networks rose 25 cents, or 5.3%, to 4.99. The telecom-gear provider agreed to sell most of its
remaining manufacturing operations to Flextronics International, in a pact valued at $675 million to $725
million. Flextronics, an electronics-manufacturing company, rose 61 cents, or 4%, to 15.98 on Nasdaq.
Jacobs Engineering plunged 6.43, or 14%, to 40. The provider of technical and construction services lowered
its fiscal third-quarter and full-year earnings guidance, citing order delays and cancellations by a major
customer.
American Greetings rose 90 cents, or 4.1%, to 22.80. The greeting-card company said earnings for its fiscal
first quarter exceeded internal estimates, despite weaker sales, and forecast "about break-even" per-share
earnings for the second quarter, a period in which it has historically reported a loss because of the seasonal
nature of its business.

Global Markets Await Action by U.S. Fed --- Other Central Banks Will Face Pressure to Follow Suit on Rates; Borrowing Costs to Feel Impact
World Stock Markets
Global Markets Await Action by U.S. Fed --- Other Central Banks Will Face Pressure to Follow Suit on
Rates; Borrowing Costs to Feel Impact
By Craig Karmin in New York, Michael R. Sesit in Paris and Martin Fackler in Tokyo
1,697 words
30 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
A CHART accompanying yesterday's World Stock Markets column incorrectly switched the labels on the
federal-funds interest-rate target and the European Central Bank benchmark rate.
(WSJ July 1, 2004)
(END)
IT IS THE QUARTER POINT heard 'round the world -- and the trigger hasn't even been pulled yet.
As the Federal Reserve prepares to increase interest rates today, other central bankers will face pressure to
follow suit, raising borrowing costs for corporations and governments world-wide. The Fed tipped its hand in
April, so much of the world-wide market reaction already has taken place: Global bond yields have been
rising and stocks falling. The dollar surged and is coming off its biggest six-week strengthening against a
basket of foreign currencies since 2000.
That powerful and continuing response by the world's securities markets and central planners underscores
the role the U.S. plays in the global economy, where the dollar acts as the world's reserve currency, its capital
markets are the widest and deepest, and the American consumer is the prized target across the planet.
"In a globalized world economy, U.S. interest rates and the Fed sit at the heart of the system," says George
Magnus, chief economist of the investment-banking division of UBS AG in London. "Japanese, Chinese,
British and Italian companies and governments raise and use capital in U.S. dollars as well as their own
currency."
The Fed announcement today at 2:15 p.m. EDT will be watched by millions outside the U.S., many staying up
late to get the news. Most everybody is expecting a quarter-percentage-point increase in the benchmark
federal-funds rate (to 1.25% from 1%), but the nail-biter will be in the details of what the Fed says about its
intentions -- a statement that likely will move the markets one way or the other and provide speculators a
chance to make some quick money.
"I suspect Hong Kong brokers, cellphones in hand, will be waiting for last call while watching the live feed
from CNBC at 2:15 a.m.," says Tim McKenna, head of the New York sales desk for the brokerage house
CLSA Asia-Pacific Markets. He used to do the same himself when he was in Asia.
The immediate impact of the Fed's expected move will be to force all investment securities to offer higher
competing returns. That likely will lead to even higher bond yields in Europe, emerging markets and maybe
even Japan, where relatively low government yields recently hit a four-year high. Rising yields could continue
to undermine stock markets, which have struggled for months despite healthy earnings prospects across the
globe.
More ominously, rising U.S. rates could hinder the ability of the world's riskier companies to acquire financing.
Highly indebted countries such as Brazil and Turkey may find it tougher to convince creditors to roll over their
debt, especially if rates rise faster this year than expected or continue to rise well into next year.
Since consumer demand in Europe and Japan remains modest in comparison to the U.S., foreign investors
are closely watching to see if the Fed move damps that demand. "To the extent that the Fed is acting to
reduce growth prospects or control inflation risks, this will likely have a ripple effect around the globe," says
David Scott, head of global fixed income for Citigroup Asset Management in London.

--- Page 197 ---
Page 197 of 204 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A rate increase would start to reverse a Fed policy that pushed short-term U.S. rates to their lowest levels in
46 years, dragging down European rates along with them. This easy-money policy fueled one of the biggest
global stock-market rallies ever, drove the dollar down 27% against the euro and 18% against the yen since
the start of 2002 and stoked a colossal mortgage boom. It accelerated global growth, pulling the world
economy back from the jaws of deflation. Multinational corporations guzzled low-cost funds, enabling many
with too much debt from the 1990s acquisition-boom years to clean up their balance sheets.
Now many monetary overseers are starting to reverse course. Central banks in the United Kingdom, New
Zealand and Australia already have raised rates. The Fed's move will increase pressure on others. "The Fed
exerts more influence on global markets today than in the past," says Paul Donovan, a global economist at
UBS in London.
The European Central Bank, overseeing a still-fragile economic recovery, is resisting the Fed's powerful pull.
The economies of the dozen nations that use the euro are expected to expand a measly 1.8% this year, well
below the global average forecast of 4.5%. At the ECB's monthly meeting tomorrow, policy makers likely will
leave their key rate at 2%, where it has been since they cut rates a little more than a year ago. At a
subsequent news conference, bank President Jean-Claude Trichet probably will try to tamp down
suggestions that the ECB will be compelled to follow the Fed.
"Contrary to the Fed, the ECB at the moment is not leaning toward higher interest rates, but neither to
lower," says Axel Weber, a member of the bank's governing council and the German central bank's
president, in Die Zeit, the German weekly newspaper. "We're closely observing whether the economy is
recovering and whether the labor market is improving."
Japanese traders and analysts say an increase in rates by the Fed won't force the Bank of Japan to abandon
its zero-interest-rate policy immediately. They say a move by the Fed intensifies pressure to clarify when the
Bank of Japan would begin raising rates or to take more aggressive steps to battle Japan's stubborn
deflation, perhaps by setting an inflationary target.
Foreign bond markets aren't waiting for the major central banks to act and already feel the tug of higher U.S.
rates. At the low point of March, 10-year German "Bunds" were yielding 3.85%. By the end of last week, the
yields were up to 4.32%. "Higher U.S. bond yields have clearly exerted a pull on the euro-zone bond
markets," says UBS's Mr. Magnus. In Tokyo, concerns about future inflation drove yields on the 10-year
Japanese government bond to near a four-year high of 1.94% last week, raising the cost of servicing the
massive public debt.
Emerging bond and stock markets look even more vulnerable. Rising U.S. rates make the more volatile
developing countries less attractive and put additional pressure on their economies when borrowing rates go
higher.
Yesterday's Market Activity
Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index fell 0.2%, or 0.38 point, to 192.76. Excluding the U.S., the index
fell 0.71%, or 1.08 points, to 150.43.
In TOKYO, the Nikkei Stock Average fell 0.2%, or 23.25 points, to 11860.81. Weaker-than-expected
industrial-output data hurt sentiment. Supermarket operator Aeon rose 3.2% to 4,570 yen, and Mitsui
Fudosan, Japan's largest real-estate developer, jumped 2% to 1,321 yen.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 Index fell 0.1%, or 6.3 points, to 4512.4. Mining stocks were hurt by weaker gold
prices. Anglo American fell 1.6% to 1,138 pence.

G. Thomas Sims in Frankfurt and Phillip Day in Singapore contributed to this article.

Fed Needs to Address Dollar's Wide Swings
Letters to the Editor
Fed Needs to Address Dollar's Wide Swings
312 words
29 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
In response to Milton Friedman's June 22 Letter "Stabilizing Exchange Rate Won't Control Inflation"
criticizing my June 18 editorial-page commentary regarding inflation: I agree with all of the economic points
in Dr. Friedman's letter -- that "stabilizing the exchange rate" won't control inflation, would subject U.S.
inflation to foreign inflation and foreign central banks, and would be a bad idea.
However, Dr. Friedman should not associate these concerns with my article. The article did not propose
stabilizing the exchange rate, nor did it mention the "exchange rate" in its text. My point was that the wide
swings in the value of the dollar (in an absolute sense such as gold and commodity prices) have an impact
on the price level, contributing to deflation and inflation. I proposed that economists and the Fed recognize
this and incorporate it into monetary policy. I don't think the U.S. can achieve stability in the price level if the
value of the dollar changes substantially, since the change will affect the price level.
It would be useful to have Dr. Friedman's thoughtful input on this. In his own Aug. 19, 2003, Wall Street
Journal article on "The Fed's Thermostat," he wrote: "Velocity is ordinarily very stable. . . . So long as that is
the case, changes in prices (inflation or deflation) are dominated by what happens to the quantity of money
per unit of output." The problem is that velocity is not stable under a floating exchange rate system because it
responds to changes in the value of the currency -- as a currency's value rises, people want to hold more,
slowing the velocity and vice versa. I think economics and monetary policy have to begin taking this into
account.
David Malpass
Chief Economist
Bear Stearns
New York
Document J000000020040629e06t0000h

Hard Choices as Fund Returns Shrink
Personal Finance
Hard Choices as Fund Returns Shrink
By Arden Dale Dow Jones Newswires
558 words
29 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Finding mutual funds that are outperforming the market has gotten tougher this year, as most
categories notched up relatively even performance numbers.
The market rally of 2003 helped most funds perform well, but now markets stand to enter a period of more
measured returns. Mutual fund investors may have to tone down their expectations, said Kunal Kapoor,
director of fund analysis at investment-research firm Morningstar Inc. in Chicago. "It's much tougher now to
find investment ideas," he said.
Recent numbers bear this out: Lipper Inc. data released this week show uninspiring performance this quarter
across much of the fund universe. To be sure, among equities, the energy sector has done well, with
natural-resources equity funds showing a 4.3% uptick, according to Lipper. And some equity funds stood out
as substantially weaker performers: gold, emerging markets and some Asian funds, for example.
Bond funds overall performed more poorly than equities, but not significantly. The weakest showing was
among general domestic taxable funds. Target maturity funds were down 5.6%, for example. U.S.
government treasurys fared better, with the exception of TIPS funds, which lost 3.5%.
Nonetheless, most funds performed within a relatively small range, leaving an overall impression of
sameness. Large-cap growth funds, for example, gained 0.65%, while small-cap value funds gained 0.23%,
Lipper reported. Multi-cap growth funds were down 0.15%, and small-cap core funds lost 0.57%.
Some fund managers say they don't expect the situation to change much too soon. "Over the course of the
next five years, it wouldn't surprise me if equity returns would be 5% or less," said Robert L. Rodriguez, chief
executive officer of First Pacific Advisors Inc., a Los Angeles investment-management firm. Mr. Rodriguez
plans to close his $1.5 billion FPA Capital Fund on July 9 because it is proving so difficult to find attractively
priced investments these days, he said. The fund, which invests in stocks, bonds and convertibles, closed
once before, in 1996, and didn't reopen until 2001.
One area that could become more volatile is bond mutual funds. In fact, investors have already begun pulling
money out of the funds at a quick clip on signs that the Federal Reserve may raise interest rates at its policy
meeting today and tomorrow. Because bond prices move in the opposite direction from interest rates,
investors could see capital erode as rates rise. Lipper data released earlier this month showed that investors
withdrew a net $17.5 billion from bond mutual funds in May, a monthly record.
Not everyone agrees that it's hard to find winners. "In a market like this, it's wise to dig deeper than the broad
market indexes to look for opportunities in different sectors," said Thomas McManus, chief investment
strategies for brokerage firm Banc of America Securities. Energy, material, and consumer staples have all
been attractive to Banc of America.
Morris Armstrong, president of Armstrong Financial Strategies in New Milford, Conn., is bullish on large cap
funds and the international sector overall. Real estate has been another high point and will continue to be, he
said. "There are some sectors which have outperformed others, and there always will be," said Mr.
Armstrong.
